CAMEO
THE RED PAGE.
That is the situation. But this is how “K.”
put it in his comment of 30th October, and the
sentence I quote contains nearly the whole pith
and substance of his reply to my observations :
‘ Generalised, the dispute between Brunton
Stephens and the young school of Australian
poets is the dispute between the Romantic and
the Realistic School of Art.’ Now, in this short
sentence there are no less than four assumptions,
all equally groundless, and two of them, to my
mind, particularly arrogant as well.
• • * »
First of all, it is assumed that the coterie of
railers against whom my remarks were directed
represent, nay, are, the young school of Austra-
lian poets. This is pretty large to begin with.
Bless my soul, when I think of the up-to-date
school of’ Australian bardhood, I don’t think of
these men at all. I think of men who have done
positive, independent work. I think of Lawson,
and Paterson, and Boake, and Farrell, and Victor
Daley, and O’Hara, and my dear friend Essex
Evans (for though Essex does his bit of denuncia-
tion now and again, his fine mind is essentially of
the constructive order), and half-a-dozen others
who are all linked together by the common desire
to give expression to the life of this strong young
country, and not merely by a bond of dismal
wretchedness and apparently a common poverty
of resources. These, whatever differences of
opinion there may be as to the value of their
work, are men who do. My little quarrel is with
men who don't —for work that is not of a positive,
independent character counts for very little in
my mind as a contribution to the literature of the
country. Between the method of these men I
have named and that of the objects of my animad-
versions there is all the difference that exists
between begetting and expectorating.
* * * *
The second assumption is equally groundless—
that there is a dispute between me and the young
school of Australian poets. There is nothing of
the sort. Far from being hostile to the young
school of Australian poets, the composition of
which I have already indicated, I love them. I
brag about them, with a pleasant sense of reflected
credit. I make myself a nuisance to my friends
on their account by shoving their works into my
friends’ hands, and insisting on their being read.
I even get snubbed and sat upon on their account
sometimes, and rejoice in martyrdom for so worthy
a cause. It is very neat, though— more. ingenious
than ingenuous—on the part of ‘ K. to isolate me
thus, and place me in a position to draw on me
the hostility of the whole literary forces of Young
Australia.
* * * * .
The third assumption is that this band of railers
represents ‘ the Realistic School of Art! Really
this is too much. lean only say as the Yankee
champion-swearer said when his cart of potatoes
up-ended on the hill and the eager crowd gathered
round to enjoy the expected volley : 4 Gentlemen,
lam not equal to this occasion.’ If ‘K.’ is one
of the set—and internal evidence shows that he
desires to be identified with it— then I can but add
that if ‘K.’ has ever prayed the well-known
prayer, * Lord, give us a good conceit of ourselves,’
it is his bounden duty in common gratitude to
bear aloft, in this unbelieving age, the testimony
of his experience that the Deity does hear the
prayers of His creatures.
* * * •
I have no quarrel with the Realistic School (we
are now, by the way, at the fourth groundless
assumption), nor with any other legitimate School
of Art. I have no idea myself what School of
Art I belong to, or if I belong to any, though if I
have any predilections at all they are rather in
favor of Realism. I don’t make much account of
Schools of Art. I enjoy all good work, from
4 Treasure Island ’ to 4 Tales of Mean Streets.
I begin to doubt if I did right in
calling the writers under consideration a
school at all, as it seems to have put notions
into ‘K.’s’ head. I called them the Autistic
School, but this seems only to have led, God
knows how, to a confusion with the Realistic. So
I must find another name to express more clearly
what I mean, and Providence, assisted by an
apparently respectable workingman, has supplied
the want.
* * • *
I was seated on the front of a ’bus some months
ago between two workmen —a position in which
this alleged intellectual dude (vide ‘K.’ passim)
always experiences the pleasurable.feeling of being
amongst his own sort— perusing with accustomed
relish the pages of the week’s Bulletin. My
companion on the right was similarly engaged,
and presently he passed the paper across to his
mate, indicating with his finger the passage he
wished him to read. I spotted the place, and
turned to it, and read also. What I found was a
poem of the denunciatory sort, of good technical
quality on the whole, but (excuse me, Mr. Bulle-
tin) made up of the usual cheap material. Anon,
my neighbor on the left handed the paper back to
its owner, with the observation, ‘ By God, don’t
he give ’em slops, eh?’ and it occurred to me at
once that this expression precisely defined the
very class of poets which is my pet aversion. It
is the ‘ give-’em-slops school.’ I think ‘K.’ and
I will understand each other better now. Only
he needn’t run away with the idea that I have laid
it down as a rule that no poet shall ever give any
man, or any system, or any anything, slops in
metre. I only go the length of saying that the
school the main scope of whose work is defined
by that expression is one which I would warn any
son of mine, who gave reason to fear that lie might
develope into a poet, not to join, or, having joined,
to come out of as soon as possible. And if 4 K.’
would but sensibly realise what it is that I am
fighting against, he would recognise the utter
superfluousness of insisting, as he does, that he
has 4 just as good a right to select from the wealth
of material everywhere ’ as I have. Why, my
grudge against these men is just that they don’t
take the trouble to select anything from the wealth
of material everywhere ! What sort of 4 wealth of
material ’ does a man employ when he’s giving ’em
slops ?
* * » »
Before passing from this part of my discourse,
let me slip in a remark about ‘K.’s’ attempt to
make my advice appear as the advice of the con-
servative, slow-paced, old fogey to the bounding
young. Chronological reasons have compelled me
to refer to the writers in question as young, but
chronology or no chronology, it is just because
they are so confoundedly old that J object to
them. Railing and wailing, grumbling and growl-
ing—are these the things that give assurance of
youth ? Come out from your Cities of Dreadful
Night and your haunts among the cinder-heaps of
burnt-out universes, and give us some manifesta-
tion of your youth, and, as old Dr. Johnson said,
4 I’m with you, boys,’ whatever form the mani-
festation may take f But no; it won’t do ; they
are too old for me. —Of course, it suits ‘K.’s’ pur-
pose to represent me as an antiquated intellectual
dandy, holding myself aloof from the stress and
strain of life, contemplating abstractions, dealing
only with the rosy-hued and aesthetic, touching
the realities of life, if I touch .them at all, with
lavender kid gloves on my fingers (I knew those
lavender kids would come in ; there is always a
3a,ir of them floating in the ‘ slops ’), shutting my
jars to the discords of life, cherishing delusions,
and all the miserable rest of it. What a mercy it
is my mother’s dead ! And all this because I re-
fuse to fill my inner man with the east wind,
which is all these men have to offer me in the way
of fodder. From which refusal, too, the.inference
necessarily follows that I have no stomach for
strong meat. Well, well; where the tokens of
youth are so scarce, even a rash inference is wel-
come as a sigh of it. It is funny, moreover, that
this imaginary Postlethwaite-Bunthorne should
be the creation of a professed representative of
‘ the Realistic School.’ Why, ‘K.’ turns out to
be a ‘ Romantic ’ after all.
* * * *
So much for the ‘ generalised.’ We now come
bo the ‘particularised.’ Says ‘ K.’—‘Particu-
larised, it means that the modern socialistic idea
is pervading Australian poetry ’; and of course it
means also, ‘particularised,’ that if I remark un-
favorably upon the methods of a certain number
of Australian poets, I am making war upon the
socialistic idea. I had really no notion I was
doing anything so big that. Will ' K.’ allow
me to tell him that, f • the point of view of
literature, I don’t c,-.i a button whether his
poetry is socialistic, or capitalistic, or feudalistic
—if it’s only good stuff. Give me a good socialis-
tic poem —a poem, mind ; it’s poetry we’re speak-
ing about —and I’ll boom it for all I’m worth, and
it won’t be the first time I’ve done the like. It
must be poetry, though—not a mere projection cf
slops over other people. ‘ K.’_ does not say that
he and his confreres are Socialists, but if they are,
then I say that they ought to be ashamed of
themselves for doing so little for so grand
a cause beyond the poor, shabby, ciieap-and-
easy work of denouncing and railing and
jibing and jeering. I say advisedly ‘so
grand a cause,’ for, though not a Socialist
myself. I am not blind to the nobility of
Socialism as a system, or insensible to its power
as a source of lofty aspiration. All the poet
w itliin me thrills to the possibilities it presents of
social redemption, and universal mutuality of
brotherly and sisterly kindness, and were it not
that the practical man witlun me keeps constantly
asking the question, ‘Would it work?—would it
work?’ and as constantly answering ‘No, it
wouldn’t—no, it wouldn’t,’l would be a Socialist
to-day. God !if I had the stimulus of such a
creed, I wouldn’t be the mere minor poet I am!
Were I animated by such a creed, I would strain
to"make my cause illustrious by showing to what
noble work it is capable of inspiring a* writer, by
doing something that would raise men’s aspira-
tions to its lofty level, by proving that ‘ the other
side ’ bad no monopoly of creative thought and
daring imagination. I would strive to build out
of the music of verse such a City of God as would
make all men turn to it. saying, ‘Lo, there ! The
Kingdom of Heaven!’ What a chance these
fellows have ! What a chance ! And what do
you do with it, oh, unpregnant of yonr cause?
Ah, pardon me, I forgot. You give ’em slops.
* * * •
‘K.’ informs me that ‘the great mistake ’ I
make is in the belief that the school of poets in
question is purely destructive in its writings.
Well, if the school he means is the school I mean,
I don’t see muoh to alter in the belief, unless per-
haps “ purely ’ is rather stronger than I pub it.
But surely the best condemnation of such a belief
would be to cite the particular writings of the
School which have a different tendency. Where
are they ? ‘Oh,’ says ‘ K.,’ ‘ Shelley, Swinburne,
James Thomson, William Mor ’ Hi! stop!
Wbat have these to do with your School ? These
are men who did, and I have already told you that
my little quarrel is with a particular set of men
who don't. This is another of the curious assump-
tions of ‘the School,’ that if you meddle with
them the reputations of Shelley, Swinburne, James
Thomson, William Morris, ‘and many another,’
are endangered !
* * * *
I am further informed that ‘ Mr. Stephens ob-
jects to the “ brutal truth ” primarily because it
“ makes us wretched.” ’ I wish these champions
of the brutal truth would have the brutal truth-
fulness to quote correctly. What Mr. Stephens
objected to was (to give the precise words) ‘ what
they fondly call the “brutal truth,”’ which is a
very different matter. The remark clearly refers
to their subjective view of the truth and their ob-
jective presentment of it, and I freely confess
that the latter does often make me very wretched
indeed. But the cool assumption that ‘ what they
fondly call the “brutal truth”’ and the actual
truth itself are one and the same thing is the worst
instance of its kind I have yet adduced, and the
bigotry exhibited in it is only equalled by that of
the Presbyterian laddie'who declared that his was
the only possible true Church, because God was a
Presbyterian.
• » * *
I am credited, too, with saying that ‘he (the
poet) must (the italics are mine, hut no f the word)
learn to express the most dreadful sentiments in a
pleasant way.’ This also is given in quotation
points, as if literally mine. However, it is not
much of a perversion, to be sure. But suppose,
for the sake of placating ‘ K.,’ I retract and reverse
the precept, and say instead, ‘ The poet must learn
to express unpleasant truths in an unpleasant
way.’ How’s that for an Ars Poetica ?
» * * *
Another misquotation (and there are several
others still). lam quoted as saying that it is the
mission of the poet ‘ to make society worth saving.’
What I said was ‘ to make society more worth
saving.’ There is a mighty difference between
these two versions. But, of course, the passage,
as misquoted, serves the purpose of the lavender-
kid theory.
Just one word more. The whole tenor of ‘K. s
article justifies one in believing that he regards
Art and Truth as things opposed to each other.
Now, telling the truth —the brutal truth, if you
like—the truth as it is, the truth in its full and
proper relations, is a far more difficult Art than
telling lies. The poet can never get away from
Art, try as he likes, and it is stupid even to try.
The art of being artless is the most difficult Art
of all. In fact, none of us can ever tell the truth.
It takes an Infinite mind to do that, and even an
Infinite mind can only do it when speaking to
itself. M oreover —no, there shall be no moreover.
Things are getting too big. Good-bye, ‘K.’
Here’s my hand. Put it there. I wonder which
of us will look the bigger fool to the great critics
at home, if they chance upon the spectacle of this
little tempest. But perhaps you have settled that
question in your mind already. No matter; put
it there again.
In ‘ Aphasia and the Cerebral Speech Mechan-
ism’ (Loud., 1897) Dr. W. Elder, physician to
Leith Hospital, formulates more decided views
regarding the graphic centre of the brain than
those recently referred to. His conclusions from
a study of the whole subject of agraphia are—
1. Tnat agraphia and disorders of writing may
be symptoms of any of the types of Aphasia.
2. That there have been no true agraphias alone
proved by a post-mortem examination to be due
to a destruction of a special graphic centre.
3. That if cases of paralysis of the right hand
be considered as agraphias, then many post-mor-
tems have shown that the centres for the hand are
in the middle of the ascending frontal and ascend-
ing parietal convolutions.
4. That the fact that no authenticated case of
agraphia pure and simple, verified by post-mortem
examination, has been recorded, tends to show
that if there is a graphic centre it is not patho-
logically separable from the centres for the hand.
5. That the cases on record where agraphia re-
mains after return of speech in motor aphasia are
probably due to lesion in the fibres joining the
psycho-motor articulatory centre to the graphic
centre or centres for the hand.
6. That a theoretical study of writing, as well
as a study of mirror-writing, tends strongly to
the conclusion that there is a special graphic
centre in the left cerebral cortex.
* * » *
Mirror-writing is writing that can be read in a
mirror. It is written from right to left instead of
from left to right; and Dr. Elder, as the result of
testing 451 ordinary patients, found that 5.1 per
oent. were mirror-writers with the left hand.
Over the age of 15 no less than 14.73 per cent, of
females, and 3.39 of males, were mirror-writers
with the left hand. Dr. Elder's theory of the
matter is that mirror-writing is precisely what
one would expect if the left hand were guided
entirely from a left cerebral centre, and no advan-
tage were derived from sight ; and his explanation
of the phenomena is highly interesting. It
amounts, in essence, to the affirmation thatowing to
some interdependence between the brain centres
for the hands, it is easier for the hands
to make the same kinds of movements—such
as twirling the thumbs in the same direction
than to make different kinds of movements—such
as twirling the thumbs in opposite directions; and
the muscles which the left hand uses in inirror-
writing are homologous to those which the right-
hand uses in ordinary writing. ‘Patients who,
when they get paralysed on the right side, learn
to write with the left hand, require to call in the
visual centres to guide the'left hand in writing,
and therefore left-hand writing m the. usual form
is directed at first by tin- visual centres. Through
practice, however, the left hand gets more expert
at writing, and a special graphic centre gets
specialised on the right side of the brain for ex-
pert left hand writing, just in the same way as
on the left for expert right-hand writing.’
THE MARSEILLAISE.
(On hearing the Press Band play in the Embankment
Gardens.)
A band a-playing ; a few sooty rods
Of London garden in a foggy cloud;
Mill’s effigy and Forster’s for sole gods;
Pale workmen, a tired crowd.
Oh, but the dullest scene is oftenest rife
With gleams and visions, and that witching power
Which charms the flagging fancies back to life.
So now, at this dead hour,
The drums confer, according to old use,
In low, restrained, yet strangely-stirring ways ;
Then, like armed Pallas from the brain of Zeus,
Leaps forth the Marseillaise !
Song that was framed where my forefather dwelt,
As thrills the Clansman when old pibrochs shrill,
I bred in Saxon schools, I nowise Celt,
To thee I always thrill!
The dark-faoed rabbles of the past arise—
Maenads and beggars in untoward rags,
Exalted faces, fierce ideal eyes,
'Neath a Republic’s flags.
O’er the huge rounded cobbles of the street,
By the gaunt beetling houses, in a throng,
They march and pass with bare but steadfast feet
And mouths rotund in song.
The tocsin shrieks : the kennel underneath
Blackens with blood: there’s blood in dew and rain:
All things are sullied by the crimson death,
Save that august refrain !
You oannot say of fire or tide “ they err,’*
Or predicate of joy and anger shame :
Laughter and tempest are commingled here,
Billows and wrath and flame !
And if to some this Song that greatly comes
Out of the past bring but a tale of wars—
Of sweating horses and of roaring drums,
Sword-strokes and heroes’ scars,
To some it brings, not these, but Man’s old rage
For Truth, Peace, Justice, Life’s augusl er plan,
The poor man’s Hope, the meek man's Heritage—
Not France alone, but Man !
VICTOR PLARR, in The Speaker.
WRITES J. BRUNTON STEPHENS:—
In an article published on The Red
Page of The Bulletin of 2nd October, I took
occasion, while giving my estimate of Boake’s
Poems, to animadvert pretty strongly upon
the methods of a certain number of Australian
poets—l said “too many,” but that does not
neoessarily imply that they are even numeri-
cally formidable—who instead of adding to the
literature of their country by contributions in
the shape of original, positive, independent work,
give themselves up entirely, or almost entirely, to
denouncing this, that, and the other thing, and
complaining of the universe generally—or, as I
expressed it (not very beautifully), who are
‘always on the anti-lay.’ Not that I said, or
implied, that no poet is ever to denounce, no poet
ever to bewail, or even, for that matter, to curse.
What I complained of was the excessive devotion
of the faculties to a form of work the exclusive, or
almost exclusive, cultivation of which is hostile to
original production —and 1 ought to have added
(though I couldn’t possibly guess till now that it
was necessary to do so) that nothing of all I said
was applicable to geniuses, of the first order, who
are a law unto themselves, and to whom no poor
animadversions of mine could possibly have any
relevance.

PRIVATE, NERVOUS & SKIN
DISEASES.

origin and course, and has discovered the quickest

many years, obtained the highest position in his
profession. The various diseases embraced in his
speciality are those which have hitherto caused
more misery and suffering than any other kind of
trouble that affects the human frame.

Memo, for would-be Australian warriors
of all nationalities : A Greek society in London
selected volunteers there, and forwarded them to
Athens, for enrollment against the Lurks m the
late belligerent fiasco. In many cases, the wives
and children of these volunteer Greeks tell to
public charity, and were turned over to the work-
house authorities. When the valiant descendants
of Leonidas returned they were promptly billed
by the authorities for the sums expended on their
families, and, wherever the money was not forth-
coming, they were ordered to gaol by the com t».
Those who went wept profusely. Moral: It takes
more bravery to fight the common doorstep-wolt
in esse than a turbaned Turk or other foeman m
poitc.

A Dream Flight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Weary was lof Earth. My body lay,
Its fires turned down and slaked to faintest heat.
My soul went out into the night away
Where wing hath never beat.
The green earth like a marble ’neath me spun ;
The shoreless ether and the island-stars
Rose up before, and sun and mightier sun
Flamed on their chariot bars,
Cleaving the blue abysmal without sound ;
Pressed on my soul I felt the awful seals
Of that vast Cosmos without depth or bound,
Blazing with golden wheels.
I marked Orion’s armour glitter cold,
Where o’er dark bars the milk-white river runs ;
I marked great Sirius flood the heavens with gold,
The sovran of the suns.
All stars grew dim, all suns changed sullen red,
Waned, and went out in that victorious light—
Heaven’s mightiest star swung on a viewless
thread
His mightiest satellite.
And like some storm-tossed pilgrim of the sea,
Who hails the savour of the shore afar,
I felt the challenge and the mystery
Of that majestic star.
The giant planet in the golden stream
Turned all her niassv bulk against the glow,
I watched her storm-blue mountain-turrets gleam
Crowned with unconquered snow ;
And all her table-lands and wooded leas,
And emerald plains through which white rivers
run,
And all the smoke and thunder of her seas
That shout unto the sun ;
And all her marble cities and her towers
That climb the hill or shine through deepmost
brakes,
And all her dazzling valleys, soft with flowers,
And all her silver lakes ;
And, lastly, with a strange new majesty,
The face of man did pass before me there,
King of the Earth, and Victor of the Sea,
And Lord of all the Air ;
Whose fleets have lit the caverns of the deep,
Whose wings have breasted all the winds that
blow,
And flashed his signal from his airy keep
To worlds above, below.
On the faint limit of the air to north,
On utmost marge of that gigantic girth,
The grey-haired Warden of the sky looked forth
And called : “What news of earth ? ”
“ Ah, woe is me ! ” I said, “ that T should bring
To this fair orb the shadow of my pain ;
The earth is full of toil and suffering,
And the fierce lust of gain.
“ The earth is full of travail and unrest,
And hearts grown old and weary ere their time,
And shameful yokes upon men’s necks are prest
That some may ride sublime.
“ They love the foot that spurns them. Let them be
Slaves to a conquering name or flattering breath.
Heroes have sought to teach them to be free,
And their reward was death.
“ The salt of earth—the blood that loved them
best,
Out of the ground it cries that all may hear,
From the dark cross on sullen Calvary’s crest
To Bruno’s flaming bier.
“They gave to Socrates the poisoned bowl,
They closed Hypatia's noble eyes with fire,
They drove proud Dante forth, an exiled soul
Reft of his heart’s desire ;
“ The Spaniard laid an Empire at their feet
And died despised. In chains Italia’s sage,
Great Galileo, at their judgment seat
Knelt in his hoary age.
“ The cell, the cross, the gibbet, and the chain
Thus have ye crowned, O World, your mighty
sons !
The Earth is drunken with the blood and pain
Of all her noblest ones.”
Then answered he, and o’er his face there shone
A sudden rapture, as the lightning breath
Of some strong thought that quickens and is gone,
Yet bids us smile on death.
“By what strange guidance of the central power
Thy soul draws near I know not, but I know
All that has crowned with joy this world of ours
Was won through bitter woe.
“ Out of the hearts’ blood of the hero few,
Out of the lonely strength that scorned to flee,
Out of the sorrow of the souls that knew,
We made the world you see.
“We too, have swung the mighty orbit round,
Chained by the toils that hold ye bound to day,
When all men’s eyes were fixed upon the ground,
And no man saw the way.
“ Yet was the germ within us, and the power
Of that great Unseen Truth to which we draw,
That from the seed may come the perfect flower
To crown the perfect law.
“ The white suns sail the waveless seas of Space,
Where once their bulk was but a starry flow,
Down the long curves each System keeps its place
Around some mightier glow.
“ From less to greater, through the scale of change,
All things ascend in their appointed time.
Who shall adjudge to Man the utmost range
His thoughts may climb ? ”
GEO. ESSEX EVANS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 13, 1897.
A Matter of Mere Sentiment.
Graham Berry is old ; Graham Berry is
feeble ; Graham Berry is poor. It is 75
years since be was born, 45 years since he
landed in Victoria. Of the vigorous writer,
the stirring agitator, the enthusiastic poli-
tician of the sixties and seventies, there now
remains only the empty husk. Graham
Berry’s career is over ; he is no longer of
any use to the Victorian democracy, and at
the recent general elections the Victorian
democracy threw him away like an old shoe.
* * * *
And all the logic, all the reasonableness,
is on the side of the Victorian democracy.
Not only is Graham Berry old and useless :
he is even noxious. Advancing feebleness
of body has brought feebleness of mind in
its train ; and Graham Berry to-day is no
longer the staunch Democrat of old. He
looks and talks like a typical Tory ; opposes
reform ; upholds abuse ; and shakes a dismal
grey head over the prospects of a country
which passes strict factory-legislation and
establishes a minimum wage. How much
changed from the Hector we knew of old !
' • * * *
There was a Man ! The young generation
never knew him ; but there was a Man!
Protection ; payment of members ; a land-
tax for land-monopolists ; —he was foremost
in the fight for all these, when the Tory
oligarchy was far more powerful than now.
It was Graham Berry who advocated the
first Australian referendum, in the plebiscite
through which he wished to make the Vic-
torian Upper House bow to the national will.
It was Graham Berry who, year after year,
struggled to break down the privileges of
that Upper House—still the most obstinately
Conservative in Australasia. Imagine what
it was before Berry’s Reform Act reduced
the money-qualification for voting and mem-
bership ! For* 25 years, as journalist and
politician, he did all that in him lay to pro-
mote Liberal measures ahd establish a Liberal
policy. No man was so feared by the
squatting and importing rings ; no man
Wrested from them so much of the power
which they used to obstruct progress
and to plunder tbe people. And now the
veteran lags superfluous on the stage, his
mantle torn, all his fripperies faded, unable
to mumble a word of the part which once
won him welkin-shattering applause —and is
hissed by the young Victorian democracy.
* * * *
And all the logic, all the reasonableness,
is on the side of the Victorian democracy.
If Graham Berry gave himself without stint
in the service of Victoria, he gave himself
because it suited him, because it paid him.
The country owes him nothing. Politics—
that was his metier :he liked powet and to
wield power ; he loved combat and to be
victorious in combat; he has had his cheers,
and his incense, and bis Salary as he went
along. It is true that Cheers and incense are
like the west wind for filling and sustaining
a man ■; that for every pound of Australian
political Salary there are ten —twenty —a
hundred beggars ; and some of them can
show a substantial claim, many of them a
claim which it is practically impossible to
disregard. Nevertheless, Graham Berry
deliberately valued the game and the candle,
and decided that the game was worth the
candle. The rules of the game were clear :
if he was not satisfied with the reward of
politics why did he enter politics ? Why
was he not content to stay by his news-
paper ? Decidedly, the country owes him
nothing.
Besides, if, as some suggest, the Victorian
Democratic party should pension Graham
Berry because he was a good Democrat, and
because it has at present command of the
country’s cash, then the Tory party, when in
the natural course of things it succeeds to
power, will have an admirable precedent for
pensioning Gillies, because he was a good
Tory, and good Tories will have command of
the country’s cash. From the Tory point of
view, Gillies has been just as good a ser-
vant of his country as Berry from the Demo-
cratic point of view. If Berry, why not
Gillies 1 and if Gillies, why not Tom Bent 1
and if Tom Bent, why not—well, why not
Matthew Davies ? There is no political
system more subject to abuse than the pen-
sion system ; and every fresh pension-prece-
dent created —no matter with what good
excuses or what excellent intentions —is
liable to bear fruit in a host of bad practices.
* * * *
On the other hand, Graham Berry’s case
should be judged on its merits, irrespective
of what went before or what may come after.
If Victoria owes Berry a pension from a
Democratic point of view—or if, without
admitting a debt, it grants the expediency
of the favor—then Berry should get his
pension, and the shadow of the coming
Gillies has nothing to do with the case.
Yet, again, you cannot consider any political
act fairly without looking to its probable
consequences to the country. When Berry
gets his pension the matter indeed ends so
far as he is concerned, but it does not end so
far as the country is concerned ; and the
country has a right to consider itself first.
So for every pro there comes a con ; to
every argument there is a more effective
counter-argument. And the age of chivalry
is gone : your young Democrat is a sophist,
an economist, and a calculator, and he mea-
sures out his gratitude on a strictly cash basis.
» * * *
He is quite right : gratitude and the need
of it are myths nowadays exploded. There
is not an altruistic emotion which is not con-
ceived and nourished by the purest egoism.
The mother who sacrifices herself for her
child, the patriot who gives his life for his
country, the individual who throws himself
beneath the iron wheels of an idea—what
are these but egoists seeking each the satis-
faction of personal cravings, most selfish
when to the common eye they appear most
unselfish ? All the logic, all the reasonable-
ness, are on the side of the young Victorian
democracy. And yet
* * * *
You may consider Graham Berry, if you
choose, not in relation to what he was, but
in relation to what he did : not in relation
to what he earned from the country, but in
relation to what he saved for the country.
And, so considered, Victoria’s debt to Gra-
ham Berry can never be paid. Paid for his
time he was, indeed ; but Graham Berry
was never paid for his services. As member,
Minister, Agent-General, and Speaker, he
has drawn from the Treasury many thou-
sands of pounds—possibly much more than
he could have earned out of politics ; but
how many millions of pounds was Graham
Berry worth to Victoria as a Democratic
Force, a Samson who shook the pillars of the
Tory temple and brought many a time-dis-
honored fetish thundering to the ground ?
Granted that Berry merely did his duty by
the Victorian democracy, and that for doing
his duty he was fully paid on a liberal
scale and on well-understood terms, can any
democracy afford to sell to the knackers the
old war-horse who no longer keeps pace
with the young colts, whose worn-out temper
is no longer trustworthy 1 The Bulletin
admits that many very potent arguments
plead in favor of the knackers, and that it is
clear loss to maintain old servants who are
far past service. It is a matter of mere
sentiment. Graham Berry was Graham
Berry. And Graham Berry is poor ;
Graham Berry is feeble ; Graham Berry
is old.
Compensations of the Crapulous.
Protracted practice and a predisposition to
aqueous delirium tremens have enabled the luiid
professional “temperance 1 ' apostle with the
rolling eye, raucous voice and mouth, to paint
in a sufficiently blood-curdling manner the
pains, penalties, and tortures which, he alleges,
are the inheritance here and hereafter of the
dipsomauiacal child of wrath. Put the herald
of the glad tidings of the water-tap is severely
silent concerning the drunkard’s compensations
—those agreeables of his condition which
temper the severity of his jim-jams and assist
him to bear up under the snakes., sCObpions,
and pea-green hyenas which hover about his
bed and board. Silent, chiefly, because these
pleasantnesses are not in the humid missionary s
brief; partly, because nature, art, and a
water - logged brain unfit him to view
more than one side of a subject -and
that crossjack - eyed. There is no chiaro-
osouro in the great teetotal portrait of the
unmitigated boozer. It is all obscure. Of
course, when the arid sinner turneth away from
his wickedness that he hath committed, and
doetli that which is lawful and right —and re-
deems his Sabbath coat, Waterbury, and the
ancestral blankets, and puts the price of “deep-
sinkers ” of hell-wort into the apostle s collec-
tion-plate and wears a renovated shirt, and, on
his nose, a less vivid magenta —-he shall save
his soul alive. Otherwise lie is, according to
temperance logic, doomed to unceasing torment
here and to eternal wrestle with the undying
worm in the bitter by-and-by.
* * * *
The writer, however, desires to show that
the hardshell toper has, speaking generally, a
far more gaudy time here than either moderate
drinker or total abstainer. The argument may
be succinctly started with the proposition that
nearly everybody thinks inconceivably more of
the average drunkard than of the average
person who refrains from wallowing in fusel
oil, who never pervades the district, mad, with
straws in his hair, and never hunts his kindred,
simply attired in a tomahawk and uncorked
bottle of sudden death. Take any two men of
the same trade, profession, or calling, of equal
natural mental calibre and original attainments,
the one intemperate the other temperate, and
what will, as a rule, be the verdict of their
contemporaries ? Ninety-nine per cent, of the
persons who know both will aver the drunkard
is a genius of heaviest tonnage and the tern*
perate man a painful mediocrity. They may
neither, or may both, be men of exceptional
talent. If both are without ability, many will
still contend for the genius of the tippler,
accounting for its non-manifestation by the
man’s “ unfortunate ” inebriety. If both are
naturally gifted, rum will add triple lustre to
the drunkard’s large-sized halo. Who has not
had his attention directed to some barnacle-
encrusted derelict, with awful hair protruding
through appal ling hat, with sockless, un laved toes
writhing among the gaps of detestable remnants
of dust heap boots, with trousers deep bitten
at the heel by mire and stone, with eyes blood-
shot and bulged as octopus eyes, and with
breath more deadly than was breathed in the
faces of Sennacherib’s host ? And who, on
such occasions, has not listened to some such
hoary lying legend as—“See that man?—
cleverest lawyer” (or liar, or butcher, or
journalist, medico, policeman, or something) “in
Sydney ! —toonderful fellow ?—drink, you know ;
yes, drink ! — might have been anything—any-
thing ’.—perfect genius ! ” And who that has
ever taken to verify the myth has not dis-
covered that the allegedly-gifted two-legged
swill-tub has, at best, shown only the most
intermittent symptoms of cleverness? Who,
pursuing research, has not found that there
are at least several men in the glorified grog-
guzzler’s own line, with twice his intellect, and
scores with 10 times his power of concen-
tration and industry, who have absolutely
no glittering reputation at all? Surely,
here is one compensation for any little
inconvenience experienced by the drunkard
when green-eyed cats with mauve eyebrows
caterwaul upon his chest, and tarantulas large
as lobsters toy, the long nig jit through, with his
amaranthine whiskers !
* * * V
But mere effulgence of reputation, however
glorious and gratifying, like the soft word,
“ butters no parsnips.” More tangible com-
pensations are, however, showered upon the
crapulous hero. Let a man bear a life-long
reputation for temperance, and, in his maturer
years, but once look, in public and ostentatious
manner, upon the rum when it is red, and, un-
less he be a member of Parliament, a teetotal-
lecturer, or of independent position, his fate
will be as surely sealed as that of the woman
detected in her first sexual faux pas. He may
plead of his first drunk—as pled the girl of her
initiatory illicit baby—that it was “a very
small one,” but Society, ever pining to
heave half-bricks at good reputations, dooms
him and downs him. And though he may
for many subsequent weary years abdominally
collect the snowballs of frigid abstinence,
neither society, employers, nor friends will
ever forget or forgive, and the wife of his
bosom will awake him on the night of his
golden wedding to remind him he “ made a
beast” of himself 45 years before.
* * * *
How vastly different the soaker’s fate ! What
sympathy he evokes when he sells the family
bric-a-brac, the fire-irons, and the bed from
under his wife to afford funds for the royalest
variety of paralytic burst ! His employer, if
he have one, under dominance of the glandered
legend referred to, forgives him not seven but
seventy times seven times. And even when
the wreck has lit his pipe with the ledger, and
been unwell in the safe he w r as trying to rob,
and has permanently vitiated the atmosphere
of the office with mingled odors of a decom-
posed Chinaman and a three-penny bar when
first opened on a Monday morning, and has
stolen the petty cash, his employer generally
refrains from prosecuting, contenting himself
with idiotic gurgles about the “accursed
drink,’ and maudlin reflections about the tradi-
tional “genius” of that animate colonial “purge”
pail when it was sober, which was possibly
once in 20 times, and not then.
And when one philanthropist leaves the
creature grovelling in the gutter, another picks
him up and starts, with unreasoning faith, the
sisyphian task of rolling a temporarily sorry
soaker up the hill of sobriety. And even
women, good, pure women, will essay the
repugnant labor of reform, will succour, bear
with, and weep over the unregenerate sot; will
triumphantly exhibit him as a brand snatched
from the burning, when he happens to suffer a
spasm of sobriety, and, when he goes on the
familiar razzle dazzle, will lavfe his miasmatic
brow and nurse him until lie is sufficiently
recovered logo on another whooping gin-craw 1.
But the temperate man who falls upon evil days
and sickness may, as a broad rule, cure himself,
because he, not being a genius in disguise—dis-
guised by liquor, that is—fails to interest the
philanthropists of either sex who like sensation
in theirs.
* * * *
And when this gilt-edged drunkard dies the
chances are he is more sincerely mourned than
many hundred-weight of just men, and that his
family, rejoicing over his release and theirs,
will have to return more “ thanks to kind
friends who sent cards, telegrams, letters of
condolence, wreaths, floral offerings, and ex-
pressions of sympathy ” than if the defunct
boozer had been many kinds of a cast-iron saint
or only an honest, hard worker unaccustomed
to delicious triangles and gravel-rash. Also it
is possible much marble will be heaped above
him to keep him down and obviate the danger
of firing the cemetery fences should he spon-
taneously combust.
» * * *
So much for the compensations of the crapu-
lous here. If, in the hereafter, there be per-
sonal continuance, and, as logical corollary, re-
membrance, there must also be, Unless miracles
intervene, a continuance of personal idiosyn-
crasy, Therefore, presuming that hereafter as
now “the gods are just, and of our pleasant
vices make instruments to plagUeus,” the drun-
kard will even then Snatch Compensations un-
known—for he can be speechlessly drunk, with
no necessity for sobering up through the endless
cycles of eternity. And we may be moderately
certain that his reputation will precede him,
and that the ghouls of Gehenna will think a
great deal of him, and murmur to strangers t
“ Might have had any position in Paradise life
liked if he’d only kept sober !—quite a genius 1
—a devil of a genius 1 ’*
♦ * * *
Nothing here set down is intended to convey
that the drunkard should not be picked up, and
patched, and mended, and soldered, and re-
hooped, and rescued generally, if possible.
Also, it is not affirmed that the State,
which connives at his intemperance, should
not connive at his real reformation by putting
him in a State asylum, and, while there, mak-
ing him work for his family, and scientifically
treating him for a “sin” which is really, mostly,
a disease. And nothing is here urged against
the State softly smothering the patient if proved
to be incurable. The sole object of this article
is to solemnly and seriously protest against
Society giving the drunkard a reputation for
genius which be seldom, if ever, deserves, and
giving him also a thousand chances while giving
the sober man of equal or superior ability only
one, and very seldom that.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tasmania on Toast.
The folks in the Island of Bad Dreams are
finding the bars of the railway gridirons quite
hot enough for comfort. A meeting of Hobart
citizens, prompted by the Railway League
which represents the Great Western Syndicate,
resolved, the other night, to call upon Fysh to
resign the Treasurership of the province in
order to burst the Braddon administration
into little bits and, incidentally, save the grid-
ironed honor of Van Dobson Laud. Apart
from the fact that Fysh will probably see the
Railway League “ further ” before voluntarily
throwing up his billet, the absurdity of the re-
quest strikes outsiders as ludicrous —even for
Tasmania. Fysh is member for Hobart in the
Assembly, and it is quite within the province
of his constituents to ask him to resign his seat
if he misbehaves himself; but to ask him to
resign the Treasury billet while retaining bis
seat in the House at once brands Hobart as the
possessor of the most stupendous cheek ever
owned by any single constituency. A parallel
case would be for the electors of King Division
to call upon Reid to resign the Premier-
ship of N.S. Wales and burst up the
GoD-fearing Ministry because it proposed
to build another bridge at Monkey, instead of
one to North Shore. Moreover, Fysh, unlike
Attorney-General Clark, who left the Ministry
because his professional feelings were piqued,
has no quarrel with his colleagues, and has no
constitutional shadow of excuse to rat on them.
Yet, virtually, the Hobart meeting requests
Fysh to blackleg on his mates. The Bulletin
doesn’t particularly admire Fysh, and looks
upon Tas. politicians generally as a somewhat
hopeless batch; but to expect Fysh to
sacrifice the remaining shreds of his personal
and political reputation at the behest of a cor-
morantic railway syndicate, is to place him far
below even this paper’s estimate of Tasmania’s
elongated figure of speech. Meanwhile, the
embroglio is just as tangled as ever. Though
both syndicates are undoubtedly “on the
make,” the Great Western crowd is the chief
sin-dictator. The Emu Bay co. has made some
sort of a start at its railway, and looks fairly
in earnest; the Great Western spent so much
time in hawking its concessions about before it
found buyers in the Baker - Rymill - Elder
crowd that it has now less than seven
months wherein to float in London and
make a start at construction works genuine
enough to secure a refund of its £lO,OOO
deposits. As experts say the business cannot
possibly be done in the time, it is only reason-
able to infer that a Supreme Court dividend is
hoped for. Great Western agents are under-
stood to have a writ ready to serve on the
Government which allows any other syndicate
to come within 10 miles of their terminus at
Mt. Lyell ; the Northern end of the island

In combating the notion—strictly enforced in
England, by the way—that a Judge’s relatives
should not be allowed to practise before him,
the S.M. Herald refers to a Judge as a
person whom the State “ takes away from a
lucrative practice, to place him in a post of ease
and comfort, where 16 hours’ work a day is
probably the longest he is called upon to do.
But there is another side to the question. A
Supreme Court Judge in N.S.W. is a (never
young) personage who —besides being saddled
from the hour of his appointment with the dis-
tressing knowledge that his disablement from
duty will at any moment secure him, for the
rest of his days, the paltry pittance of about
£36 a week—has further to struggle with exist-
ence upon a miserable present stipend of £5O
a week—£6s, or so, in the case of the Chief-
Justiceship, with a commensurate retiling
allowance, claimable, like those of the puisne
Judges, at the end of 15 years’ service, or im-
mediately upon previous incapacitation from
duty. And few indeed are the barristers
whose “ lucrative practice ” pans out anything
like £3OOO a-year—to say nothing of the cer-
tainty of continuance, and the security of
future provision, which pretty well double the
nominal value of an unfortunate Judge’s screw.

Dear Bulletin,—! have seen a Melbourne
suburban policeman making an arrest, and
wish to describe that arrest for the enlighten-
ment and edification of policemen m general.
The street is deserted, saving for the victim and
constable O’Hooligan O’Halligan. lhe vic-
tim is in a condition of baby drunk and at
peace with mankind and all the animals ; he is
delivering a non-partisan speech to a paling-
fence. Suddenly O’Halligan swoops, grip-
ping his victim by the collar, with huge
knuckles digging into the back of Ins nee .
“Come, move on, will yeh ? cries tie
officer of the law, giving the man s collar a
twist that makes his tongue shoot out ridicu-
lously and his eyes goggle like bubbles in a
washtub. The victim chokes, and struggles
for life and air. “Oh, yeh won d
go, won’d yeh ? ” cries O’Halligan, and
then begins a ferocious struggle in which the
amiable drunk acts the part of a spineless
dummy. O’Halligan goes down pulling the
drunk atop of him, then he rolls over lus
prisoner, and his prisoner rolls over lnm, and
they continue rolling, the desperate encounter
reminding one of Whitburn’s life-aud-death
fight with a man of straw'. Presently the brave
officer lets up, flushed, dusty, and bedraggled,
and bears his prisoner off in triumph, taking
with him evidence of a gory battle. Later 1
read in the local paper of the heroic conduct ot
O’Hooligan O’Halligan in arresting a drunken
ruffian after a desperate struggle, and of the
proper punishment of the “ruffian” for drunken
and disorderly conduct, insulting behaviour,
resisting the policeman, assaulting the same,
and tearing me uniform.—S.S. *
Isn’t this something like the philosophy of the
well-established tendency of the Ezekiels, and
Jedediaiis, and Ebenezers, et hoc, towards
brutal sexual offences? The parents who in-
flict such labels on their progeny are mostly ot a
mental and moral tvpe in tune with the brutal
fanaticism of early Bible times—whose predomi-
nating affinities are with so-called religion of
the kill-the-man-and-dishonor-the- woman type,
but whose tendencies—in further transmission
to offspring more liberally-nourished and more
sensuously environed —develop more along the
line of sex than of slaughter. Natural religion
means reasonable self-restraint; doctrine is fust
cousin to cruelty and lust.
By some curious kink in the legal mind the re-
tail publican is always held responsible for the
quality of his liquors, even though he proves
that he bought them in all good faith, and sold
them in the same condition as when they were
received ; while the wholesale man, who traded
off the objectionable stuff to him, goes free.
For instance, a Melbourne publican was hauled
up lately for “ falsely applying a trade descrip-
tion to"a bottle of rum,” the said falsehood
be in" that he said the rum was Jamaica, while
the Customs rum-expert and connoisseur in
schnapps alleged that it wasn’t. Then the
wholesale dealer was called :
The merchant from whom the rum was bought deposed
that he was agent for a London firm, who, he believed,
imported the rum from Jamaica. Rum was made in the
islands of the West Indies, but was exported from Jamaica
in different qualities, though they all bore the name of
“ Jamaica.”
Then the publican was fined <£l and £2 costs
for deceiving the community, and nothing was
said about prosecuting the wholesale man who,
if there wasanv deception, had plainly deceived
the publican. Yet, as the person who deceived
the wholesale man was beyond the jurisdiction
of the court, evidently the said wholesaler was
the first responsible person within reach and
the one who ought to be fined. If the importer
deceives the licensed victualler, and the licensed
victualler unconsciously deceives the public,
and the public unconsciously deceives its own
stomach, it is impossible to see wherein lies the
justice of fixing the responsibility on the mare
intermediary. Probably the explanation is
that the importer is mostly in Parliament,
while the retail publican isn’t. So the im-
porter makes the laws under which he can sell
anything he pleases with impunity.

Dear Bulletin, “The woman-snob,” de-
clared a recent Bulletin paragraphist, ‘ is
the snob”; and, as it has further been
recently made abundantly evident that the
Woman-royalist is the royalist, there is in-
terest in a closer investigation of these pie-
siunably allied phenomena. Very little obser-
vation will demonstrate, in the first place, that
the average woman is much more keenly inter-
ested than the average man in the personal or
concrete aspects of existence. Drop suddenly
upon the conversation of any two women, in
any rank of life, and, ten chances to one, the
talk is of “ her ”orof “ him of what is said
or done by, or to, or of, some person or persons ;
whereas, in the case of two men of any but the
lowest intellectual grade, the odds are at least
as great that the exchange of ideas will be about
something which more concerns mankind m the
mass than as individuals. The mere “ person-
ality of a personage,” therefore, is always ol
vastly more immediate interest to a woman than
the personage-making social conditions about
the broad significance of which a man is more
likelv to concern himself. Woman, again, is
more mechanically reverent than man fuller ot
unreasoned veneration ; and with no hook ot
steel does she grapple herself more closely to
the characteristically masculine soul than with
her proneness to place affection where it is not
deserved, or to preserve it where it is not re-
ciprocated. And since, in human nature, we
have always to accept, with the good, more
or less of complementary evil, man can well
afford—for the sake of these, its indirect
developments—to condone in woman that over-
appreciativeness of personality for wluo 1
“snobbery,” as man applies the term to man,
is perhaps too harsh a designation. In this
matter, as in others, the standard of each sex
for its own conduct appears to differ, after an
ill-defined and elusive fashion, from its standard
for that of the other. The characteristic faults
of each sex—simply because they are charac-
teristic —are as necessary as its distinctive
merits to its complete appreciation by the other.
Woman, without what have come to be regarded
as her special weaknesses, might be so much
the more theoretically admirable a creature,
but—she would he also exactly so much the
less woman ! Departure from type in one sex
commends itself only to correlative departure
in the other. The sub-masculine woman—
physically or mentally—repels rather than
attracts the man who is not himself in some
degree sub-feminine; which is probably why
the most intellectually robust and virile-
thoughted men appear to take their woman-
kind none the less seriously for being over-
reverent of pomps and dignities ; for being
royalists —lord-worshippers, Queen - worship-
pers, priest-worshippers —sentimental reaction-
aries, generally. And it is just here that the
New Woman—because, though she has ceased
in some degree to be woman, she has
failed' in any degree to become man
fails utterly to apprehend the masculine point
of view. “Man pretends,” she shrieks, “to
admire these futilities in woman', because he
knows that only by carefully fostering such
weaknesses can he hope to retain his supposed
intellectual supremacy.” Not so! I lie man-
complete dreads no advance on woman s part
that is not made at the expense of some of her
womanly essentials. But, of these, the very
smallest is, in his eyes, an exceedingly real
price to pay for an exceedingly problematical
advantage. And, the only man who can recog-
nise any possibility that his sex will ever be
relegated by the other to second intellectual
place, is the man who already occupies that
position. —A.M.

Ed'. Bulletin, —Let me draw attention to the
practice which, prevails in the N.S.W. Crown
Law Department of sending opinions on dif-
ferent matters to country police-magistrates,
With the result that, when those questions
arise, they are decided in accordance with the
opinions given by either the Crown-Solicitor or
the Attorney-General. A matter of this kind)
lately came before the Licensing Bench alt
Moree. The Bench refused to grant transfer
of an hotel license to a woman who had
obtained a decree absolute for dissolution of
her marriage with her husband. Looking at.
section 29 of Act 45 Vic., No. 14, and at sec-
tion 46 of Divorce Act of 1893, I think the
Bench was wrong, but that does not matter..
In the process of giving their decision, they
produced two opinions—one by Mr. Jack
Want, as Attorney-General, and one by the-
Crown-Solicitor —and, as these were against
the woman, the magistrates refused the ap-
plication. The case, therefore, st amis thus: the
judgments of country P.M/s are not their own
judgments at all, hut are very often the halt-
considered, cut-and-dried opinions of some
Jack-( WANT)-in-office. The question arises,
too, why the bench is to be swayed by the
opinions of the Crown-Solicitor or Attorney-
General in preference to the opinions of any
other lawyer. The pair are only lawyers, and
by no means the best lawyers in N.S.W. It a
P M. is not fit to administer justice without
opinions from the Crown Law Office, then he
is not fit to sit on the bench ; and if he is fit to
administer justice, then it is an insult to him
to send him opinions by which he (having a
billet to keep) practically must be bound. it
the Crown Law office is right in what A does
in this respect, then it ought not. to send the
opinions to mngistrat.es only, but to all the
Judges in the land, for surely a man should
get as good justice (in his sphere) from a I.M.
(who has to pass an exam, in law) as from a
Judge (in his sphere). Anyhow, I submit that
the proper exponent of the law is not the Attor-
ney-General, but the Supreme Court.—Attor-
ney Smith.
A Poor Scribe
Why should a picture-maker have any greater freedom
under the gambling laws than a novel-maker, a poem-
maker, or a bootmaker ? The other day an alleged artist is
reported to have “sooled” on the police to suppress
Chinese and other gambling-law trangressors, w hile on his
wav to assist at a Tombola or some other pedantically-
named game for easing pockets by lot. Taking them in the
mass, artists-those who draw or paint-are, next to
blind organ-grinders, the most inveterate and insatiable
of social beggars. If they are not posturing and dancing
before wealthy people—“ leaders of society —to obtain
“distinguished patronage,” they are rigging up some
sort of lotterv to shove their wares off their hands. Ami
yet they assume to be cuts above pak-a-puh men and
boss ioc-keys, and with tine free trade chests tell the local
bard'and novelist, “ Well, you see, you «)«** n
vour merits ; to put a premium on local matter would he
L cultivate mediocrity. Haw!” The ehairmaker and
bedsteadmaker—the maker of anything has as much
right to claim exemption from the scope of gambling-
laws as anv other workman in Australia, and politicians
should get up pluck enough to brave the Haw- . and
say so in the Act.
The lapse of a couple of Legislative Assembly
Bills in N.S.W. Leg. Council lately, because,
as the Attorney-General explained, there was
no member of that latter Chamber prepared to
take charge of them, calls attention to a serious
oversight in the constitution of Parliament.
The Bills, as it happened, were not of wide
importance, but wliat befel them may befall
any private member’s measure that lias passed
the Assembly. A Govt, measure can always
command attention in the Council, because the
Govt, is directly represented there. But a
private member’s Bill which has received the
sanction of the Assembly may be refused an
audience in the Council, not necessarily because
the Council is hostile, but .sometimes through
sheer indifference and “ that tired feeling. In
many countries—among others Cape Colony and
Natal—a private member who carries a Bill
through his own branch of the Legislature is
permitted to attend and explain his measure in
the other branch. Something like this would
seem to be necessary in Australia.
The hopeless futility of diseased-meat regula-
tions which apply only to centres of population
is well illustrated by the pleasing discovery
that on most pig-breeding stations cancerous
and tuberculous cattle, after being shot, are
skinned and deposited in the pig-paddocks,
where, according to a Sydney weekly’s corres-
pondent, little piggy-wiggy may be seen dis-
porting himself within the horrible carcase
from which his elders are assimilating disease
for broadcast dissemination, in due course, pet
medium of bacon-curers who are alleged to
give a good price for pigs thus fed. Pigs, it is
in fact asserted, are only bred on stations for
the express purpose of thus utilising diseased
cattle. Good care, of course, is taken not to
use any of the pork on the station and d n
the other fellow !

Though far from “ pressed-down and run-
ning-over,” the Centennial Hall was well popu-
lated on Saturday night by people desirous both
of giving the “ sacred cause ” a lift and speeding
•the Hambourg brothers upon their conquering
way. For which reasons the tornadoes of applause
that raged violently as ever around the principals
had probably less in them than usual of that
bogus musical ecstasy which it has apparently
been considered in Sydney the correct thing to
fall into over this couple of clever boys. The fact
that the net proceeds of the concert were to go to
the Consumptive Hospital-fund had also doubtless
a share in the sound and fury signifying “recall!”
—and yet again “ recall! ” In which the audi-
ence, for sudorific reasons, was kind even unto
cruelty; for, while the temperature appeared to be
quite rough enough on Jakoff Hambourg, Mark
came out of each terrific encounter with the piano
a victorious but badly-busted man. Of the open-
ing “ Fashingschwank,” The Bulletin has only
to say that it was as fashingsohwanky as heart
could desire, and that the piano didn’t afterwards
appear to have suffered material damage. Mr.
Woodhouse artistically wailed a tenor-wail from
good old “ Don Giovanni,’’ Jakoff Hambourg
fiddled delightfully in all the colors of the rain-
bow, Mr. Lemmone’s enchanting flute “ put him
away ” for more encores than the thermometer
had any use for, Miss Edith King lifted up her
voice and whooped in a satisfactory manner over
the injudicious Mr. Ernani, and Mark Hambourg
topped off with an earthquake in three acts that
must have gone near to breaking the Sydney
acclamation record. After which Mr. R. Teece
fell figuratively upon the necks of par nobile
fratrum, bade them Sydney’s collective Godspeed,
and thanked them for an undertaking which
will benefit the Hospital fund by about .£l6O.
** * *
In this column, last week, it was noted
that the management of the Lyceum Theatre, in
staging “ The Prairie King,” expected great
things from the Tank. The Tank more than
realised the wildest expectations. In fact, it is
doubtful if any Tank ever before seen on the melo-
dramatic stage was so well up in its “ business,”
and won such enthusiastic plaudits. Before the
play commenced, the Tank suffered a little from
stage fright, as was natural in a beginner with
such an important rdle. It was studying its part
behind the scenes when Charlie MacMaKon noticed
that it was trembling, as if about to faint. Know-
ing that it had been too nervous to eat anything
all day, and that nothing but a few thousand
gallons of water had passed its lips, he hastily
ran to support it, but was unfortunately too late.
The Tank’s legs gave way under it, and 160 tons
of eau-de-Nepean dropped dead to the dramatic
world. Restoratives were brought, but it was
long ere the Tank became conscious. Mean-
time, the play had been proceeding. Maud
Williamson had been captured by infuriated
Indians, and was being borne off in triumph
to the wigwam of Potted Bear, the great
Sioux chief. With incredible barbarity the
Indians refused to permit her to bring a 2cwt.
trunk containing her trousseau, so she resolved to
make a bold dash for freedom. But how ? Ha !
the Tank ! Upon its placid surface a canoe
floated. On the farther side lay liberty and a
long vista of profitable engagements in leading
rbles. Instantly MJaud Williamson’s mind was
made up. Shrieking appropriate defiance at
Potted Bear, she leaped wildly into the canoe. A
few strokes of the paddle and she would have been
out of danger. . . . The canoe toppled over !
. . . One scream ; one airy parabola; one
splash ; one pair of moccasined legs seen for an
all-too-brief instant above the surging waters;
one convulsive kick; one total disappearance !
Maud Williamson was Tanked !
* * » *
The audience held its breath ; the Tank
held Maud Williamson. It knew Maud was not
sticking to her lines ; she was only gagging ; but
what a delicious gag ! The Tank embraced her
coldly, yet O how warmly ! Potted Bear looked
uneasily over the edge, but could see nothing ; for
the Tank was hugging Maud under the lee of the
canoe. Painted braves ran to and fro; the
Prairie King and Potted Bear buried the hatchet
near the orchestra, and fraternised in the bond of
a common anxiety ; and the Tank hugged on. It
knew it would never get such another chance, and
the violence of its embrace quite took Maud
Williamson’s breath away. At last somebody
dived in and brought her up, and somebody
brought her round, and she pluckily came on
again and was cheered as she deserved to be. But
the Tank was desolate and inconsolable. The
management put a bridge across it, and King
Hedley rode a horse across the bridge, and the
bridge gave way, and King Hedley clung to the
railing with one hand and potted Potted Bear and
his minions with a revolver m the other, while the
horse dropped into the Tank. But the Tank was
not deceived; it knew the difference, and threw
out the horse in disgust. The curtain fell on 160
tons of Grief weeping through canvas sides—there
was not a dry ton among them.
* * * *
Yes, there was some sort of a play, but on
the opening night it didn’t matter in the least
The audience was absorbed in Maud Williamson
and King Hedley and the Tank, especially the
Tank. The Tank is still the feature of the drama,
but it plays its part sullenly, in a blighted spirit!
AH lta hfe and joy departed when it lost Maud
Williamson.
** * *
At Sydney “Majesty’s” “Two Little
Vagabonds ” approach the end of their mournful
course; and at the end of next week “ A Royal
Divorce ” will be granted by Mr. Justice Tither-
adge and co. At the Royal George Rignold
started another season of military drama on
Tuesday night, after this paper had kindly but
firmly gone to press ; consequently notice of what
exactly George did with “Tommy” is postponed
with reluctant gladness. And the Tivoli still
renders its celebrated double bill every evening
and an audience which is usually expected to pay
every bill it gets waxes quite enthusiastic over the
novelty of twice bills for once cash. The manage-
ment announces that the tremendous combination
must positively expire to slow music in a few
nights only.
** * *
“ The Brand of Cain,” at Melb. Royal, is
an undeniably awful drama, based upon the
ancient ballad story of two brothers. The
brothers of the drama were probably Mrs.
Quinn’s Twins in the first instance. They grow
so like one another in form and feature, face and
limbs, that, ’spite their Varied “clobber,” the one
whose forte is singing hymns gets taken for a
robber. H. R. Roberts plays the hero in two
alternate capacities. Sometin.es he figures as a
desperate character, addicted to dropping tourists
down a Spanish chasm, and shortly afterwards he
is a pious and rather effeminate sort of widower
with a nonconformist conscience and one fair
daughter. It transpires casually that the pious
twin must have turned his fatal resemblance to
account, years ago, by marrying his brother’s girl.
The plot gives no details of this old love circum-
stance, but judging from a remark passed by the
wrong Roberts, the girl was originally captivated
by his appearance, whereupon the right Roberts
came along with exactly the same appearance, and
a more respectable behaviour, and married her for
her money. YVhich accounts, more or less, for the
other fellow being the bane of his twin brother’s
existence from that time forward.
• * * *
The sins of Roberts the Ruffian are visited
freely upon his relative’s head. A defunct tourist,
resurrected from the chasm in Spain, turns up and
denounces the model widower as a man who cut
him down when he was descending into the
bowels of the earth by a rope, to secure a bag of
diamonds “as big as cherries.” Also, an evil
accomplice, who had added to the horror of the
chasm scene by stabbing Roberts the Ruffian in
the small of the back, is unable to discriminate
between the two Robertses when he meets them
anon.. Ruffian Roberts was dead, strictly speak-
ing, in the first act, but he returns to London
along with the indestructible tourist, and applies
dynamite to his nonconformist brother. Luckily,
the explosive goes off at the wrong moment, leav-
ing Righteous .Roberts and his daughter un-
unharmed amidst the fragments of Ruffian
Roberts. Also, the fragments of the evil accom-
plice lie scattered thereabouts. “ The Brand of
Cain ” is not a drama to be taken seriously, and
where the tragic characters make a mistake is in
forgetting to laugh at their own misdeeds. H. R.
Roberts supplies most of the acting. As a quick
change artist he achieves complete success, whilst
in the ruffianly half of his dual identity he far
surpasses Roberts the Righteous. Miss Maggie
Moore brightens up the agony with a brogue, and
the rest of the company seem to be knocked out
of memory by the dynamite explosion.
** * *
“The Gay Parisienne,” at Melb. Princess’s,
was so thoroughly in touch with Cup sentiment
that money had to be turned away from the doors
on several occasions last week. As an entertain-
ment for those who have backed the winner, and
feel determined to enjoy a good larf, “ The Cay
Parisienne” is quite equal to any other musical
comedy. It. is wild, without being obtrusively
witty, and it contains sundry suggestive hints
concerning ladies’ underwear, which, as all experi-
enced playgoers know, are even superior to the word
“ dam ” for theatrical business purposes. When
Miss Ada Reeve plays at buying four yards of calico
from Coleman, and Coleman, having heard the
lady’s whispered statement re the object of her
purchase, says “I suppose linoleum wouldn’t do?”
it is then that the people who went to the theatre
for a good larf feel quite certain they are getting
it. Miss Reeve deals verv cleverly with this class
of work, and is bright all the time. Bert Gilbert,
in the Prenchman part, doesn’t lose weight on
further acquaintance; Coleman, as the kilted
masquerader, is a more shining light than at first,
and the great gathering of other well-known per-
formers goes for a good deal, especially on the
programme. One or two of the comparative
strangers don’t go for much, but, if they were
omitted from the sum total, their absence might
be felt. “The Gay Parisienne” marks a great
change in the taste of the Melbourne public, and
will doubtless revel in prosperity for some time to
come.
** * *
The Alexandra season was to end this
week (Wednesday) with a benefit to lessees Wilson
Porbes and Brskine Scott, who have worked hard
and honorably to keep a roof over the heads of a
very decent co. They have tried all sorts of
dramas— good, bad, or indifferent, paying more
attention to stage-management than their audi-
ences were disposed to appreciate at the time, and
if they should give their luck another chance next
year they will deserve to reap some reward.
** * *
Professor Bristol and his intellectual horse
show are proceeding peacefully at Melb. Bijou.
The evening audiences are small, but the matinees
seem to be popular, and the fact that Bristol's
horses and mules can give many points to the
average performer that sleeps in a stable will no
doubt become generally known when the show has
gone away. It will hold the stage until the 19th,
and then travel around with a tent.
** * *
Manager Rickards has taken a few weeks’
lease of the Bijou, from the 20th instant, on which
date the enterprising Henry R. will introduce his
Sydney Palace Theatre importations to Melbourne
notice The Biographe, Miss Fanny Wentworth,
Phil Newbury, Miss Lilian Tree, the boy soprano,
ventriloquist Emmett, and prestidigateur Morritt
are to be the leading items on a programme that
is not intended to distract attention from the
other Rickards show at the Opera House.
• * * *'
Meanwhile, the Opera House is putting up
attendances and working the horsey possibilities
of the cinematograph for all they are worth The
Cup-race, like the Derby and Caulfield Cup be-
fore it, was flashed upon the sheet six or seven
hours after Gaulus had got home, and the picture
did more than reproduce the beginning and end of
the Flemington struggle. It reminded the audi-
ence of the gloom that prevailed when the num-
bers, went up. There are not more than two hat-
waving figures in the foreground, and the only
person visibly enjoying himself is a harmless idiot
who appears to have jumped up at intervals in
front of the operator whilst the negatives were
being reeled off.
* sfc * *
The Axemen’s Carnival, held in the quad-
rangle at Melb. Exhibition, was unhappily such a
financial frost that the men who won plenty of
cash on “ paper ” will, it is sajd, get naught but
glory for their reward. Race-week in Mel-
bourne appears to be quite the wrong time
for a wood-chopping show, anyhow, apart
from the fact that the general management of
the affair broke down when the manager was
unavoidably called away. Therefore, if the
United Australasian Axemen’s Association is to
derive any benefit from the patronage of Gov.
Brassey and Gov. Gormanston, and the vice
patronage of other distinguished elderly gentle-
men, they had better send along their little
cheques forthwith. The poverty of this muscular
Association is a sad matter to reflect upon. Every
honest chopper deserves his hard-earned chips
especially in a city where politicians prosper by
rolling logs and grinding little axes. For instance,
F. 0. Mason, who won the Speakership, the other
day, will draw his prize-money reg’lar.
At Poverty Point .
A charity-concert given by the Hambourgs, at Melb.
Town Hall, the other week, was a subject for much foolish
remark by the press. The Hambourgs were profusely
thanked for their generosity, whereas chief credit for the
affair should have been given to John Lemmone, who sug-
gested the concert in the first instance, and carried it
through to a successful issue. Lemmone, being practi-
cally the “proprietor” of the Hatnbourgs in Australia,
was the actual giver of the £2OO odd, profit from the
matinee, and little Mark, recognising this fact, was
astonished to find the kindly action of the local artist
getting no honor in his own country.
The Potter-Bellew performance of “ Francillon,” al-
though it gave much pleasure—not to say considerable
delight—to the “beastly kawlonial” public, Was* seem-
ingly, damned by the London critics. The piece, we are
told', “ is not one that appeals to the tastes of an English
audience. All the wit and brilliancy Of the original have
been lost in the adaptation, and there is a pretty unani-
mous opinion that Mrs. Brown-Potter is not equal to the
character of the heroine ; while Mr. Bellew gets but faint
praise for his rendering of the husband of the jealous
countess." To which The Bulletin would reply that the
alleged super-excellence of the French original is a ques-
tion quite apart from the charm of the English version.
The play, as we saw it for the first time in Australia, was
bright and interesting in comparison with the average
society play by Pinero, Grundy, or H. A. Jones, and Mrs.
Potter appeared quite equal to the heroine's character in
its Anglicised aspect.
There was quite a bustle on the stage of Her Majesty’s
Theatre, Sydney, one night, when it became known that
a real earl was calling on one of the leading performers
—male. The boy who took the card up could not remem-
ber the name—thought it was Hardface, or something
like that. Somebody cuffed him for such an impious sug-
gestion, and all waited expectantly. By-and-bye some-
thing in tan boots waddled along, and was bowed out of
the door with great empressement. The girls crowded
around to know who it was, but the callee waved them
off majestically, saying : “ It will cost you fourpence each
to speak to me ; I have just had a visit from my old
friend, the Earl of Ilardwicke. I knew him well in Eng-
land." A pity that actor left so soon ; he might have
gonp bail for his noble friend, and kept him out of the
beastly colonial quod.
Harry Rickards’ Cine, pictures of Melb. Cup race were
received in gloomy silence by a crowd that had failed to
get on to Gaulus, and was harrowed all over again to see
its money cantering in with the tail of the procession. A
popular win is all right in the Cinematograph, and would
be an uproarious success if ’Arr.v could arrange to pay all
bets over again nightly for the sake of verisimilitude.
As already explained, Bert Gilbert, the galvanic French-
man in “The Gay Parisienne,” and Miss Ada Reeve, the
Firm’s recent importations, are man and wife. Bertie is
quite a youthful-looking Johnnie, and Ada is a pocket
Parisienne with a copious smile. Ada's name used to be
Beatrice, but Beatrice is not a good name for a Gaiety
girl; and, besides, Miss B. Reeve sounds lugubrious.

“ One who knew him ” as “ a splendid fellow ” writes
that William Henry Maddocks, tragedian, came to the
Princess Theatre, Melb., in 1859, “and, if my memory
serves me right, Avonia Jones arrived at the same time,
and was to have played leading lady to him, but of this I
will not be too positive.” “ Hamlet ” was put in rehearsal,
and billed, but the new man did not act, being seized
with cancer of the upper jaw. Our informant visited
Maddocks in the hospital. The poor actor, bandaged up,
could not speak, but wrote on a slate about a cruel opera-
tion he had undergone mostly without chloroform. He
only survived a week.
. Dear Bulletin,— The first act of my Napoleon play
appeared in the Imperial Review, May, 1890. The idea
was to give Miln a London part. The writing is prose,
on Sardou’s model, only to illustrate the action. It begins
just where Wills does, but Metternich appears as the
second leading part, instead of Talleyrand. Otherwise,
the treatment is very like Wills’s. Anyone would say my
piece must have been written after seeing Wills’s. Dates
may be compared. I think that the play of “ A Royal
Divorce ” had not been mentioned in any way then. I
take more credit for the mock heroic blank verse of the
supposed extracts, in the Imperial, from “The Girl
Queen,” a play on the loves of Queen Victoria and Prince
Albert. London Dailv News gave it about a column. I
only learnt this from a reprint in Melbourne Standard,
treating the production as English.—Yours, “Demos”
Palmer.

headdom at the Opera House, and of whom a portrait is

repertoire comprises oongs which are principally of a
frisky nature. Combined with advantages of thisdescrip-

which the public are permitted to enjoy a glimpse when
she points her dainty little toe at the electrolier.

Tenor Philip Newbury tells an interviewer that he was
once saved from drowning at Jersey by the unfortunate
reeently-dead Mr. “Lily” Langtry. Another narrow
squeak for the warbler’s life was when, after making a
spring for the mail-van of his departing train, the mail-
man at the same moment, closed the door, and Newbury
had to hang on to the handle outside for 30 terrible miles.
