Newspaper-manager to editor of a “great
daily”; “What’s the use of reviewing books at
this end ? The publisher can get it better done in
London —better literature, better insight, and
more of those little personal touches about the
author than you can get here. Just look at this
column-article about Ding-Dong’s new novel. It’s
exquisitely written. Why should we pay a man
here to write a worse review?” “Looks like
Ding-Dong’s own writing.” “What matter?
We’ll get paid for putting it in as a review—instead
of having to pay for a worse article.”
There is real grit and novelty, and a real
Australian atmosphere of heat and flies and sand
•and blanky, in the clever drawings of J. A.
Commins, which, photographed by the well-
known Sydney Arm of Kerry and Co., have been
transformed into characteristic Xmas cards to
send to distant friends. There are still touches
of the amateur in Mr. Commins’s art; but the
vigour, and motion, and reality of some of his
pictures leave many a better draughtsman sigh-
ing, as Bulletin readers have been able to judge.
Country agents and salesmen should certainly
write Kerry and Co.
Melbourne people had almost forgotten
that Bertram Mackennal was sent to Europe at
the expense of a Fund. The other week there
came a bronze statuette from the clever young
sculptor, cohsigned to Frank Stuart, who is sup-
posed to hold it in the capacity of trustee for the
Mackennal Fund subscribers. The subject is
“Truth,” and the modelling and artistic senti-
ment of the figure of the bold young woman mid
nodings oh couldn’t be finer. It is a little figure
to see and remember. The unsatisfactory part of
the work is the arrangement of Truth’s long hair,
which appears to have got caught in a pair of
spiky and inexplicable wings. It is not easy to
say where the tresses end and the wings begin,
nor does the theory of wings for Truth seem justi-
fiable. Philosophers reckon that Truth merely
walks hither and thither upon the earth, and very
slowly at that.
The report that the hulk of the Osprey has
been found, if it be true, affords the solution of
what has been a mystery of the sea for more than
20 years. The Osprey, it will be remembered,
was the strangely-rigged three-masted barque on
which the Australian Tichborne claimant declared
he had been rescued from the wreck of the Bella.
His story of his career, up to the time of that
wreck and from the time he left the Osprey, made
a narrative which would have established his claim
to theTichborne estates if he could have proved that
he had been rescued from the Bella by the crew of
the Osprey. This one missing-link in his chain,
however, made his entire story worthless. The
Osprey disappeared in the latter part of the year
1875, and was not heard of again until a few days
ago, when the well-known wrecker, Whitelavv,
discovered on the coast of Vancouver Island the
hull of a vessel on which could be made out the
name Osprey. It seems that this is the barque
which ran into and sent to the bottom the steam-
ship Pacific, with all but three of her three hun-
dred and odd souls, in the latter part of 1875, a
short distance below Cape Flattery.—S.F. Argo-
naut.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Broken Hill Junction report for the half-
year to the end of September is the most humiliating
document issued since the revival, four years or so ago.
It is a confession, without purporting to be one, of terri-
ble miscalculation—and something more. Seven months
ago a creep occurred in the mine. To the ordinary ob-
server with any knowledge of mining the movement was
extremely serious ; blit the statement was officially put for-
ward and reiterated that the thing, so far from being seri-
ous, was to some extent distinctly beneficial, inasmuch as
it permitted of safer and cheaper working. In their
recort six months ago, in fact, the directors said:
“Your board has the assurance of the mine manager
that on the resumption of work below operations
in stoping will be facilitated and considerable economy
promoted.” And a few weeks three and six
were mentioned on different occasions at the beginning
was to suffice to get everything into order again. Now,
over six months afterwards, the general manager pre-
faces his report to the shareholders with—“As you are
aware, at the commencement of the half-year we had a
very serious collapse in our stopes at M'lntyre’s shaft,
extending from the 675 ft. to the 400 ft. level.” How could
they be aware of it ? How, at least, without disbelieving
the general manager? Still, it was very serious. “New
drives,” quoting the manager in this present report,
“ had to be put in at the 675 ft., 550 ft., and 450 ft. levels,
the old drives at the 550 ft. and 450 ft. having collapsed the
full length of the workings—viz., 150 ft. from shaft south
and 140 ft. north.”
But the figures in the financial statements are
even more striking. The half-year was begun with
£37,944 to credit of profit and loss. Ore and concentrates
sold this half realised 128,572, against £93,470 in the
previous half. Yet though but one dividend of £5OOO was
paid, only £3119 remains to be now carried forward. Cer-
tainly the creep is not responsible for swallowing up
the whole of this very large sum, though it is entirely
responsible for the comparatively miserable extraction of
ore. Again and again Tim Bulletin has warned share-
holders and others interested in Barrier mines to pay no
heed to “ estimated surpluses.” Last half-year the Jun-
ction swelled its “ assets ” by estimating that a consider-
able sum was to be got from surpluses on shipments. It
now has to debit itself with £9371 —“defichncies on esti-
mated surpluses.” The London auditors of the British
B.H. Co., it may be remembered, simply declined to cer-
tify to a sheet containing an entry similar to that con-
tained in the Junction report six months ago. Further,
at one swoop £11,250 is now written-off for “depreciation
and bad debts, and provision for income-tax.” The
directors explain : “ As the mine assets and the smelting
works at Pt. Adelaide have hitherto been standing at
their original cost, yet directors have taken this oppor-
tunity of writing-off an amount of 25 per cent, for depre-
ciation.” Land and plant at Pt. Adelaide—the smelting
works—cost £16,475; for depreciation £4475 is now
written-off. The co. used these works for a few months
many years ago, and there seems no reason whatever to
suppose that it will use them again, for a long time to
come, at any rate. The furnaces were valuable in the
mine’s carbonate days ; those days are no more. Whether
this tardy writing-off is, then, anything like sufficient is
a matter concerning which most shareholders are likely
to hold a different opinion from that implied by the
present action. But, anyhow, what is the value of docu-
ments which confessedly have contained such absurdities ?
And with how much confidence are we inspired with re-
gard to the present document ?
Nor does this more than briefly suggest the
charges which the management of the B.H. Junction
brings against itself and its predecessors. Take two
more instances. The general manager, Mr. Phillips, is
describing the work that has been done in the manning of
King shaft—a working long idle. “ We have standing
reports in the office,” he says, “ of a lode at King’s 300 ft.
level 40ft. wide ; but on examination the lode was found
to be from 4tt. to 6ft. wide.” Still, that may be passed ;
Junction management in the old days was a fearful and
wonderful thing ; but the public had begun to think that
it had all been changed. Again, the manager is “ not by
any means satisfied with the result of the present jig,”
but does “ not propose to make any alterations, seeing
that our new plant is well under way.” Yet this is the
plant (in The Bulletin stigmatised a mere makeshift
when it was erected) about which we have almost weekly
and half-yearly read it that it was doing good and satis-
factory work.
For the future in the distance the prospects of
the Junction are good still. For the immediate future
they are no better than they have been this last half-
year. The co. has “ sundry creditors, including
provision for income-tax ” of £11,121. It is over-
drawn at the bank £403. It has liquid assets
amounting to £6137, including £133 in cash. Its
liabilities over liquid assets are thus £5388. It has
a contingent liability of £4OOO in re new plant now being
delivered. Further, a good deal of partly unproductive
work still remains to be done in the “ creep ” section and
elsewhere before extraction on the old scale can proceed ;
and then it cannot proceed on the old class of ore. The
Junction’s second boom-days have passed. Six months
hence, supposing all things to go fairly well in the mean-
time, it will take its regular place on about the same level
as Block 10—a few grades, that is, higher than Block 14,
the South, or the British ; jn point of quality only, of
course, since as to quantity nothing but mere surmises
can be indulged in.
* * * •
B.H. Central —the Sulphide Corporation (Ash-
croft process) mine—is preparing to do still more “ on its
own,” independently of the works at Cockle Creek, which
continue to give disappointing results. Large concen-
trating mills have just been added to the works on the
mine ; and most of the product will go to Europe, whither
for some time past half the material has been shipped.
» * * *
North Central, practically abandoning hope of
striking the underlay, is now following indicators—
“ stringers ” is a less grandiloquent word, but it generally
expresses something nearer the truth—after the manner
of the Consols. But the situation is not so favorable for
these barely-connected bunches of ore ; and, anyway, that
sort of “mining” is unadulterated gambling with the
odds at about 20 to 1 against even moderate success.
* * * *
Emmaville, N.S.W. —“Several wolfram shows
are being worked here with more or less favorable results.
Miners complain of not being able to get any reliable in-
formation as to the commercial value of wolfram.” It
was ever thus with all metals of which the consumption
is limited, and, as a rule, between the price to the pro-
ducer and that to the person who actually uses it there is
a gulf fixed which only the rapacity of metal-brokers and
smelters can bridge. From working wolfram, bismuth or
antimony mines—may the Lord deliver us !

* * * «
Milton (N.S.Wi), the land of milk, has now a
copper-mine. “ A parcel assayed at the Illawarra works
gave 28 per cent, copper besides small quantities of gold
and silver. An assay at Sydney gave 30 per cent, copper.”
Look at that, now—and all the big copper-mines of the
world are under 5 per cent. Yet in the poorest copper-
mine of the world it would be a remarkably easy matter
to get 30 per cent, assays, which are a delusion, more
especially when made for the benefit of milkmen.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —You print two depreciatory
pars, in re two hydrauliking enterprises on the Shoal-
haven. The first is, I understand, an English venture, the
other local. In re the first- speaking from a considerable
experience of the London mining-promoter and under-
writer—l respectfully submit that they are more than
capable of taking care of themselves, and the B.P. is by
no means the fool it is sometimes deemed at this end.
As to the second, or Braid wood, scheme, why discourage
people who are spending their own money, and who, pre-
sumably, also know what they are about ? My special
object in writing-, however, is to say that I consider the
depreciatory remarks made about hydrauliking in the
Shoalhaven are undeserved. That there is along the
valley of the Shoalhaven a track of auriferous alluvial
deposit of considerable depth and width, and between 30
and 40 miles long, is unquestionable. That hundreds of
thousands of tons of this “ wash ” have been treated in a
most primitive way, the excavations show, and, as work-
ing diggers cannot work for nothing, presumably their
labor did not go altogether unrewarded. I submit,
also, that no single co. has spent ,1100,000 in attempting
to treat this enormous drift, though the Spa Jessop
wasted many thousands over a pumping scheme which
any practical hydrauliker could have told them from the
start must prove abortive. On the other hand, I main-
tain that a well-devised and properly-conducted gravita-
tion scheme would treat the Shoalhaven material at a
cost of from Ito 2gr. per yard. That there are large
areas possessing the principal desiderata for hydrauliking
-—payable gold, potential high head, and hundreds of
feet fall from tail-races—is demonstrable to any experi-
enced man. I Lave no interest in either of the schemes
criticised, but am largely interested in endeavoring to
prove the Shoalhaven the large permanent goldfield which
I am convinced it is.—S.A.
» * * •
From the writer’s correspondent, who has been
to Klondyke, getting back to ’Frisco just one month ago :
“ I made the journey across the country through the
Alaskan Territory into the Yukon River region, and
walked nearly all'the way. I had a horse and packed my
stuff. I assure you it was a very hard journey. The
mines there are not as good as represented. This, of
course, one expects to find ; but, on the whole, they are
good. The great drawback to that country is that it is
frozen to bed-rock, which is 20ft. deep, and it is a
country that never thaws out—except about a foot
or 18in. of the moss on top. The ground is frozen
the Lord only knows how deep; holes have been
sunk 50ft., and still it is frozen as hard as an anvil.
The method of mining is by the burning-out process.
Thej' sink a shaft by heating rocks on the surface, then
lower them down and pour water over them ; the hot
water thaws the ground, and then they scrape out the
thawed portion, and keep on repeating that process over
and over again. When bed-rock is reached, they put
some wood against the face, and as it burns the dirt
trickles over it and covers the fire, and that holds the
heat in and it thaws into the face some six or eight inches
during the night. Next morning that is taken out and
another fire built. And this is the method of mining !
You will understand that the ground must be very
rich to pay for this sort of thing. You can only
do this in the winter, for in the warm weather
(which lasts just about two months) they cannot
work underground on account of gas, and if they sink
a shaft, the ice melts and runs the place full of water and
mud. So it is impossible to do any drifting (driving)
there except in winter. The dirt is washed in summer.
Provisions are very dear ; it is almost impossible to buy
them at all, and one earns every dollar he makes, no
matter how much it is. I do not believe that your Aus-
tralians could stand the climate, because the winter is so
severe. The thermometer stands from 20 to 70deg. below
zero all winter. When it is no colder than 20deg. below
they consider it fairly good weather. The snow is from
three to seven feet deep. If these mines could be worked
by the ordinary process of sluicing, they would pay very
well indeed. In sinking a shaft you may get on to some
frozen dirt, and then have about 2ft. of blue ice, and
then a 1 ttle more dirt and ice mixed. It just looks as
though this country had been suddenly frozen up, or else
as if it had never been thawed out. I never saw anything
like this region, and I have travelled over some pretty bad
countries ; this takes the cake. Along the coast, there is
a better climate, and I think they are going to open up a
very good country. No doubt they are going to have
quite a boom there, this year and next, and there is going
to be a deal of money made and business done. Like
Westralia, there will be another lot of “ wild cats ” put on
London market. People in the U.S. are greatly excited,
and they are forming all sorts of companies here, also in
London. But, when it is all said and done, I must say
that this country has a great deal of merit, and Klondyke
alone will, I believe, produce four or five millions of dollars
next year. This, of course, is not very much, considering
all the blow. However, the country I travelled through
is gold-bearing, and not a hole has been sunk anywhere,
and I think I shall return next year and do some work.
One thing, this country is well supplied with water and fuel
—you can get a drink of water anywhere. If anything, there
is too much water in the warm weather, but in winter the
creeks freeze up solid. I lost 401 b. on this journey north,
but am much better for it. I could walk almost any dis-
tance now and feel like a lightweight (usual weight 18st.).
There were no bad results from the trip except that your
clothes don’t fit when you get back. If you feel like
making the trip I will be very glad for you to come along,
It will anyhow be an experience you never had before
and probably will never want again. lam going to tackle
it again next, spring. . . . The closing-down of silver-
mines has made it very dull in some of the districts of
Colorado, and I see little hope for the future of that metal.
Everybody, including myself, is now hunting for copper,
and i think that next year I shall be able to open up some
good copper-mines in Alaska.”

Yabber, in Melb., at the reception of delegates
of the proposed mining-exhibition to be held in London :
J. S. Butters—* 1 commended the movement. It should
erive a great impetus to mining 1 , and should divest it oi
the * wild-cat ’ element.” What can Butters, of all men,
possibly know of “wild-cats”? Somers Vine: During
the last two years the faith of English investors had been
rudely shaken with regard to certain Australian mining
properties, and possibly if a properly-constituted chamber
of mines had been in existence, much that was to be re-
gretted might not have happened.” J. Lovvles, a Bull
M.P. : “ There were 420 Westralian mining eo’s registered
in London as against 210 for all the other Australias
put together. He had interests in the West, but
he felt that it was not the oniy field for
good mining investments in Australia. What stood
in the way of investors at present was the deplorable
want of information. They had an abundance of mining
experts in London to advise them —black-and-white ex-
perts —and there had been a larger percentage of the
black species.” All very sensible and nice, no doubt, 1 ut
the “oat’’-mine and monger will continue to flourish and
will always be with us. “ Cat ’’-mines have one very dis-
tinguishing qualification— cheapness —and between the
price at which they can be got and that at which at times
they can be sold by the aid of a “ black ” expert and a
lily-**white” London promoter, there is a gulf fixed
which human nature cannot bridge.

W. R. Wilson, who, The Bulletin is sorry to
say, is dangerously ill in Melb., has won the appeal made
by the London and Globe Finance Corporation against
the decision to pay him £48,750 for the non-allotment of
7500 Lake View Consols shares. Even in this deal the
L.G.F. crowd show their cleverness —in getting Wilson to
define the issue to the value of the shares at a certain
date. Had he sued for the shares alone the result in
money would be nearly double, for to day that number of
shares are worth about £90,000. As to the merits of the
case, the writer may have something rather important to
say later.

In the failure of that old Melb. firm a bank, a
trustees co., and three big merchants are mainly in-
terested. There will probably be 20s. in the £ for trade
creditors if justice is done.
• • * •
Leigh’s Creek, where has been made the only
solid attempt in S.A. to develope a coal-mine, is in
extremis. In spite of a reconstruction four years
ago, less than half of the 40,000 shares are now good
on the books, and the co. has only £lOO in hand, though,
since it was formed in ’9O, over £16,000 has been spent in
development. The reduction in freight on fuel to Broken
Hill from the seaboard cut away from Leigh’s Creek the
small but increasing Barrier trade; and the final blow
came when Govt, refused to use the coal on the G. N. line.
Yet the fuel is, undoubtedly, of fair quality already and
was getting better, and some day it may be an important
factor in developing the far-inland copper and other
mineral fields. The co. is, however, utterly sick of wait-
ing and fighting. , , *
The Menzies Barossa mine (11 miles from Gaw-
ler on the Para River, S.A.) will have 40 head of stamps
at work before Xmas, and Managing Director Menzie (dis-
coverer of the W.A. field called by his name) hopes to in-
crease the batter}’ to 100 head within the next 18 months.
Stated that 3dwts. will pay all exes., and leave a margin
for the creation of a reserve, and it is confidently expected
that the stone, taken as it comes, will average half an
ounce to the ton. This is the only big gold-mine now in
active work in S.A., and many prayers are offered up for
its success.
* * * *
It is now certain that in many S.A. districts
the coming harvests will be quite a failure—and it is not
expected that more than 25 per cent, of the seed wheat
distributed by Govt, early this year will be returned. In
the regular rainfall areas, however, the crops look pro-
mising, and good prices for wheat and hay are assured.
A W.A. correspondent : The Victoria South
mine, controlled by the Water Trust people, rails all its
high-grade ore to the Trust’s big batteries at far-away
Northam ; seconds and thirds are sent to the Mt. Burgess
battery. The local papers contain paragraphs relating to
the great work done by the Northam battery—Bo stampers
in full work, ore coming in from all quarters, gigantic
success—(all these for the edification and mystification of
John Bull). It would be as well for shareholders to have
on an early date full details of yields and expenses (in-
cluding, of course, trainage) at the respective mills
named. Has the Victoria South ever lost on any parcel
of ore it has had crushed at Northam ?
• • * *
Last month production from Lake View Con-
sols mine, Kalgoorlie, wa595650z., made up by crushing
3652 tons of ore, the cyanide treatment of 1944 tons of
tailings, and contents of a few tons of rich concentrates
shipped away. The production of the Associated Mines
for the same month was 23220 z. from 1700 tons ore. This
was got by direct cyaniding, and some gold is yet to come
from running the tailings over amalgamating plates to
take up coarse gold missed by cyanide. In addition, some
1000 tons ore were shipped away for smelting, the con-
tents of which is estimated at about 4000 oz.—making a
total production of over 6000 oz. Truly the lines of
managers Callahan and Irwin have fallen in pleasant
places since they left N.S.W.
Black Flag Proprietary, W.A. : “1500 tons,
112 oz. A fairly low-grade one -for Westralia ! Bull paid
a heap of cash, and spent many thousands in the erection
of a 60-head modern Fraser and Chalmers’ stamp-mill.
How nice it must be to pay £4O a ton freight on machinery
going to a less than two dwt. mine?
Once more the public are told that the Golden
Zone mine in the Hannan district is going to come out all
right, that the shares last week rose some shillings in one
day because of a rich strike in a neighboring mine, and
that the G.Z. is driving for the same lode.

Golden Bar, Coolgardie—“ A partial clean-up-
-170 tons, 740 z. The gold from the plates was not included,
hence the low average.” Now, what does this really
mean ?—the gold absorbed by the plates, or that no
amalgam was taken off during and after the crushing?
Always some excuse. None given for the regularity of

Published, and in W.A., too, of the Oroya mine,
Kalgoorlie : “ The most shocking waste of water ever wit-
nessed on the field is now going on at this mine. If the
water was obtained free of cost it would be a shame to
waste it in the manner in vogue on the Oroya, but when
it is remembered that every gallon is purchased, and is
used, according to common report, in treating low-grade
ore, it would not be at all surprising if the water-bill were
found to be greater than the value of the gold won from
the stone. Day after day this folly is being repeated, but
apparently the authority on the mine is so divided that
it is impossible to introduce any reforms.”

Westralian gold-return for last month, 75,6900 z.,
thus beating the record three months in succession, and
the return for the year is likely to double that of last.
And yet no boom !

Kalgoorlie : “ The whole of the workmen em-
ployed on Hannan’s Reward North mine were lately dis*
charged. It is alleged that when the lode was struck
they were the first into the market, and have received
notice in consequence.” Well, someb dy must be the
first, anyhow. What guarantee that the next batch won’t
do likewise if they yet a show ? A miner who can make
a few pounds in that way can afford to get the sack every
time. Such a chance comes seldom, and when it does
there isn't one man in a million who wouldn’t take advan-
tage of it—if he had the money.
* » * *
Anticipations in London and realisations in
Westralia. When the nohle Mr. Frank Nicolas, who is
often confounded with Prof. William Nicholas, manager
of Burbank’s (much to the latter's chagrin), was in Lon-
don last year, the shareholders of the W.A. Development
Group met at the Hotel Cecil to hear him talk of the
great richness of their mines and the divs. they were
going to draw under his guidance. His speech filled
three columns of the Financial Times. He (as there re-
ported) predicted great things for the Mackenzie, Arma-
dale, Orotava and Dorothy mines, admitted that they had
had one or two failures, but of these—“ no attempt had
been made to float them or to make them appear any-
thing but what they are.” But of the others : “There is
not one but has been proved to have sufficient ore to last
for j-ears ; not one that has not been shown by bulk crush-
ings to have highly payable ore, ami not one that is not
capitalised so moderately as to enable substantial dividends
to be paid.” Not only this, but he gave the ounce to the
ton this and that mine would go, received the cheers and
thanks of the meeting, was banqueted, and went
on his way to W.A. rejoicing. Instead of drawing
the foreshadowed steady and substantial divs., the share-
holders are now probably thinking deeply over the
following realities. Armadale: Battery of 25 head and
other machinery, erected at enormous cost, sold at a tre-
mendous sacrifice. Dorothy : “ Yesterday the Dorothy
lease, which is situated a few’ miles south of Coolgardie,
was sold by the bailiff to satisfy a judgment of the local
court. The price realised was only £22. The purchase-
money covered all the tools on the property, and the
buyers are satisfied they will make a good profit.” No
doubt the sellers in London are also abundantly satisfied
—that they will make a good loss! “The sale of the
Mackenzie mine, which had also been advertised, was
postponed.” The writer is now awaiting Nicolas’s
(M.1.M.E.) next appearance at the Hotel Cecil. Why, too,
in Heaven’s name, is it that papers with the long and
honerable career, independence, and sense of humor (w f e
smile !) of the Financial News don't once in a while give
their readers a treat by reprinting in parallel columns
some of the really good things promised for W.A. mines
only a short .year ago, with the sales of the same mines
since made by auction by bold, bad bailiffs to satisfy
“measly” £25 creditors?
• • * *
Despite the flapdoodle lately talked in public by
Capt. Oats, one of the early managers at Southern Cross,
W.A., about the present glories of Kalgoorlie (the Capt.
was comparing it with poor Coolgardie) being due to hard
work and enterprise alone, the fact remains that Kal-
goorlie has become great by luck in the finding of big
phenomenally rich mines. Any amount of Bull and
Australian money was spent in and around Coolgardie
without finding such mines, simply because they didn't
happen to be there. The Coolgardie mines placed in Lon-
don were mostly swindles. Even the Londonderry, which
had a marvellous surface patch, was bonded at a price
the owners never dreamt they would get. Why? Because
the promoters thought the sensational gold got out of a
small hole on a big reef would enable them to so work the
B. public as to place the mine at more than double the
price they were paying; the only risk was a £lO,OOO
deposit, and they put it up. It- was a noble gamble. Can
Capt. Oats explain why several of the Kalgoorlie mines
now valued at millions were originally sold by well-known
miners for a few thousands? One Sydneyite declined the
Brown Hill lease at £llOO. If Coolgardie has left any
self-respect it will not allow itself to be shown up as the
only sinner, but will undertake the very easy task of ex-
posing right up to the hilt that Kalgoorlie was no better
than she ought to be, that she owes her greatness to
nature’s treasures, so concealed when compared to
Bayley’s Londonderry and other W.A. mines as to turn
“cat-mongers’” lies into solid truths, make enormously
rich men of mining fools, and leave poor indeed many of
the pioneers, including even the discoverer —Pat Hannan.

There is to be a brush-up in the returns from
the Murchison till the end of the year. The Golconda
will help the most; after a long spell, crushing has been
begun again, and 620 tons have given 15450 z. Also, there
is a little stir about the Day Dawn.
v * * • •
The question asked in S.A. Leg. Assembly as
to the truth of the report re importation of silver half-
crowns, florins, and shillings made in Germany, was
raised by Tiie Bulletin several years ago. Whether true
or not, it is evident that in Melbourne and the principal
Vic. towns there are almost as many brand-new’ silver
coins of 1896 date in circulation as old ones. It is only
two years since a firm of Chinese merchants in Melbourne
had to account for a large shipment of shilling pieces
(identical in quality and make with those coined at the
British Mint) which they had received from China, and
which were found to have been manufactured elsewhere
than at the British Mint. If silver drops to 2s. an oz. the
profit might induce an enterprising foreign manufacturer
to lay down the £BOOO to £lO,OOO plant necessary to go
regularly into “ the business.’’

A blasphemous farmer to Hon. G. H. Reid, P.C.,
“per favor of Bulletin” : —Dear Sir, —I can't make my
farm pay, owing to the recent very severe seasons. Of
late my expenditure is larger than my income. I have
tried the usual system of book-keeping and cannot make
ends meet. You would do me and my neighbors a great
favor if you would kindly forward particulars of your
spot-cash scheme of book-keeping. Under your system
I think I could “make ado of it.” Awaiting an early
reply, your everlasting slave, G. H. Hayseed.
• * *
Be recent Yic. advt. : “Wanted a thoroughly
qualified butter-maker ; wages, 255. per week.” A Melb.
correspondent writes : Many such have been appearing
recently, and if, after all the butter bonus business, that
is all that can be paid to a qualified butter-maker, who
has to hold an engineer’s certificate and work for 12 or
14 hours per day, Sundays included, up to his neck in
grease, it is extremely doubtful if it was worth while to
start the butter industry at all. The writer was the third
man in succession who had to give up the management
of a so-called good creamery (1000 gals, per day) being
unable to keep himself after paying for wood, oil and
horse and trap to take cream to railway. In fact, he had
to live for the last week or two on rice and milk.”
» * * *
Again the W.A. Premier has announced that
the Government intends to carry out the Coolgardie
water scheme, “if it gets the support to which it is
entitled.” Ten times the quantity of water expected
has been found in the mines of Kalgoorlie, but the ex-
perience of nearly all mining camps is that the quantity
reduces with time, and it looks as if Kalgoorlie mines
will have much less water below the 300 ft. than above it.
The sooner the water business is faced the better.

shops along the street frontages, and it is expected that
the speculation will be splendidly payable.

COGMAG
The ship Phos still provides “copy.” A
Hobart Bench the other day sent two of the crew
to gaol for refusing duty. Their complaint was
that they were compelled to eat cockroach soup or
go hungry. Appeals to the captain and their
consul were unavailing, so they struck work. The
magistrates (one of whom was the aforesaid Con-
sul, who admitted having seen cockroaches in the
Phos soup) told the sailors that they had “not
taken the proper course to have their grievance
remedied,” and—fourteen days. Despite the
horrors of the shipping law, none but V.D.L.
magistrates would pass such remarks and such a
sentence on justly-protesting foreign sailors. Yet,
after all, gaol food is not so objectionable as stewed
cockroaches.
* • • •
Ranjitsinhji was the lion of the lawn and
saddling-paddock, at Flemington, on Cup-day,
quite eclipsing Gov. Brassey and the bearded
woman ; but wails of feminine disappointment
followed the boomed Oriental wherever he went.
Ranji, in spotless flannels, may be moderately
picturesque at a distance—but never, under any
circumstances, could he be anything like as lovely
as his portraits. In a dark suit and a straw-
decker he is a very ordinary-looking colored
gentleman, and on close inspection he is found to
be somewhat deeply scarred as though from small-
pox. Ranjit is not even a prince, but the fact is
not advertised, as it would materially detract from
his value as a show cricketer. Any man who is a
good cricketer is a draw, but a prince who is good
for anything is a phenomenon.
Judging by the treatment of the daily-
press representatives at Gov. Brassey’s big ball,
H.E. has decided to introduce the Westralian
principle of giving the reporters a glass of water
at a side table while the crowd feeds. The first
pressman who arrived was met by a gorgeous
flunkey who bailed him up with a lordly air, and,
learning his business, swept out a gloved fist—
“ Back-door—up the back-stairs, and you’ll find
the room for you reporters.” With much trouble,
the man of sentences discovered an out-of-the-way
door leading up about five flights of stone steps —
greasy to the foot and redolent of cabbage-water
and cooking. At the top of the stairs was a long
passage, and, at the end of this, a large room just
under the roof with half-a-dozen beds shoved in a
corner to make room. There was no ventilation,
and the place had apparently been cleaned last
during the era of Latrobe or Johnny Fawkner.
Here it was that the dozen daily pressmen—all, if
you please, in the mandatory evening-dress—
gathered for the pleasing job of “making up the
names.” In a far corner of the room flickered one
faint blue flame just making visible a row of
planks on trestles, in an open space between the
beds. A loud, concerted growl resulted in two
kerosene lamps arriving, and, without ink, the
most miserable job of a pressman’s lot, five hours’
long, was commenced. During the whole of those
five hours the work was carried on at top-speed,
yet not so much as a glass of water or a solitary
sandwich was proffered by the individual who had
made it compulsory for visitors to the servants’
bedroom to wear swallow-tails.
Some of the cards presented by the guests were
a lovely reflection on the intellect of the “ society ”
holders. Though the name of the character alone
was required, many of the dancers gave lengthy
costume descriptions on the backs of their cards,
and half-a-dozen of the male professional beauties
even had the cheek to scrawl a neat and ungram-
matical paragraph, to the effect that the writer
“looked handsome” or “wore a striking cos-
tume.” One of the hawty youths who thus
described himself had three shots at spelling the
character before he let it go, and was then a mile
out.
What a farce is the pretence that a Gov.’s ball
is a social function, at which H.M.’s representa-
tive and local sassiety are veritably host and
guests ! Fully 100 visitors to Gov. Brassey’s ball
were waiting on the steps when the doors opened

At the Brassey fancy-dress ball a constable
approached the wife of a mighty colonel, and told
her, in subservient tones, that if she required his
services in any way during the night he was at her
disposal. The lady thanked him most graciously,
and said she would not forget. Then he disclosed
himself as a certain gallant majaw.
* * * *
A few weeks back, a city policeman, on
beat at 2 a.m., encountered a stylishly-dressed
woman, very drunk, bumping aimlessly at the
side-door of a pub. At the watch house she was
found to be in possession of a bag containing over
£3OO in notes and sovs. With just enough sense
to give her name as Jane Smith, or something
like that, the prisoner slept in the cell until suffi-
ciently recovered to shift to a neighboring hotel,
leaving the usual =£s security. When the case of
Jane Smith was called there was, of course, no
appearance, and a £1 fine was accordingly entered ;
but later on the arrestee, who proved to be a well-
known actress, rolled up again to find how she
had been punished. When proffered change for
the fiver, the lady produced another note of the
same denomination and, instructing the police to
give it to the Home for Fallen Women, swept out
into her carriage with the air of a duchess.
A drunk, too far gone to speak, see, or hear was
lodged in a Melb. watchhouse cell, the other day,
and lay in an unbroken sleep for about 12 hours,
when a second man, only slightly and hilariously
bibulous, was also locked up. The new arrival,
trying to find the softest plank in the dark,
stumbled over his prostrate fellow-prisoner and
woke him up. Now thoroughly sober, but unaware
of his, whereabouts, the first guest of Her Majesty
resented what he considered an assault, and,
jumping up, “plugged” the new arrival vigor-
ously, at the same time calling loudly for “police!”
Watchhouse keeper and reserve man rushed to the
cell and found the latest drunk on the floor with
the other sitting on his chest pounding him.
“ What are you doing to that man ? ” asked the
constable. “He’s robbed me,” explained the
prisoner—“look here,” he added, pointing rue-
fully to his outturned pockets, “lie’s done me in
for everv cent I had.”

Another “Mac” appointed to medical
staff of Sydney Prince Alfred Hospital. Though
nominally offered to competition, the thing was
regarded outside as cut-and-dried. Sandy makes
a gey glide sawbones, nae doot, but this begins to
look a little too much like a second New Caledonia.
* * * *
According to Grafton Argus, the s.s.
Kallatina was, on a recent trip, practically taken
possession of by 19 prisoners who were being for
warded from Darlinghurst to Grafton Gaol “ in
charge ”of three warders ! Their irons were
taken off, and they roamed at will about the
vessel, singing and shouting, demanding drink
from other passengers, and even, in one case, en-
deavoring to intrude into the ladies’ cabin. If
this is true, surely even Botany Bay justice might
have refrained from subjecting these men (some of
them lifers) to such a cruel temptation to make
that dash for liberty which, if it failed, would be
afterwards so vindictively and unreasonably
punished.
* * * *
The Captain who was to command one of
the Australian regiments in England did not
reach Ome until a fortnight after his men, and in
his absence a young lieutenant was in command.
During their first week in England invitations,
addressed to “ the Commanding Officer,’’ came in
by the dozen from all the dukes and lords in the
kingdom—enough to last anyone but a military
buck for a lifetime. The commanding officer for
the time being, realising that as soon as the cap-
tain arrived his show of getting any of the invita-
tions would be even as “ Buckley’s,” took a day to
answer them, and (so the story goes) accepted all
the very nicest ” ones in his own name. He did
nothing for the rest of his stay but swagger round
with P.Gs. and so on, while the captain
had to be satisfied with what his thoughtful
subordinate had been good enough to leave him.
Which was not bad young ’an on his first
trip.

Whatever H. M. Addison may be in
other respects, he was an excellent chairman of
S.A. State Bank, and the greatest public loss by
the Nixon case is the necessity of his resignation
(remarks The Bulletin’s Adelaide correspondent).
His friends have determined that in future they
will quote his case as an insuperable precedent for
the dismissal from office of every public man who
goes astray. In the past many similar peccadil-
loes have been winked at in S.A. —as this one
would have been but for the aggressive presence
of the woman voter.

ROUND THE COAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is just a little sobbing
As the boat’s about to start;
And a little extra throbbing
At the region of the heart.
And he feels that he’s a sinner
To desert her in that way ;
But it doesn’t spoil his dinner,
For they haven’t left the bay.
There’s a little fall and flurry,
As he helps a girl to rise ;
And he says she’s “ not to worry,”
And he thinks “she’slovely eyes.”
Then he draws a little nearer,
To protect her from the breeze,
And he tries to make things dearer
With a tiny little squeeze.
And his voice is soft and mellow,
For he’s got a lot to say—
An insinuating fellow
Is this creature of to-day.
Melb.
M. F. KEMP.

A German once landed in Suva with a parcel. of
guns. These were considered unlawful for native
trade, and positively dangerous for Europeans to
handle (they were invoiced too cheap). They did
not give the foreigner his guns back, but took
thenPbut in a punt and dropped them over the
reef. Another time a book-fiend arrived with a
huge case containing books, photographs, &c.,
which the austere, gilt-buttoned officer considered
in toto as obscene. The whole package was
treated with the same courtesy as the guns. Even
now the corpses of indigent natives and executed
criminals are consigned to the shell-fish per this
coral wall.

S.S. : “ To me the most significant feature
of Flcmington lawn on Derby and Cup days was
the great number of fat, elderly men with slim
and pretty young women. nu ran against them
all over the place, the sweet girl radiant in a
lovely gown that probably cost more than the
nice young man could earn in three months ; the
fat, iron-grey gent, with a contented proprietary
air, keeping his prize well in tow. Cup-day, no
doubt, is responsible for many May and December
matches, and the fair damsel is induced to say
‘ yes ’ to the paunchy senior suitor earlier in the
year by rosy visions of an adorable frock for the
Cup.”

bourne’s elephantine attorney, holding conversa-
tion with Prince Najal of Hyderabad in the car-
riage-paddock on Cup-day. The Prince’s English
was very rocky, but he must have found Mac s
quite as lucid as old Hebrew. “ Shure, did ye
sa-ny ye have a lether t’ the jintlemahn ? ” said the
lawyer, and he said it often, and was informed in
reply that his colored Highness thought Positano
a certainty.

One enterprising person, who had some-
how or other got possession of an invitation to the
Brassey Eancy Ball, made an honest penny by
turning up as an advt. for a much-boomed brand
of groats or something. Which opens the door to
a fine industry. Possibly the Govt’ouse ball of
the future wiil be merely a field wherein “ Blue
Boluses for Bald Bounders ” will battle for
supremacy with “Moses’s Mixture,” “Blank’s
Pills,” and other prominent nostrums for the
regeneration of health.
* * * *
The very latest idea in mayoral munificence
comes from S.A. :
Striking figures in the crowd at the first cricket-match
against the Englishmen were the members and officers of
the Semaphore Corporation. Mr. Deslandes (the Mayor)
hired a drag, and supplied all the councillors and officers
with white cricketing- hats, and thus they were driven to
the oval.
But, then, The Bulletin knows Deslandes to be
an undertaker ; so the idea of lending special
head-gear before setting out for a drive came
natural to him.

A certain Australian potentate, when out
on a biking tour, does not hesitate, even if there
be a hotel close by,, to stop at any private house,
knock introduce himself, and demand refresh
ment. This, frequently repeated, went far to
disgust a private secretary, who took the first
opportunity of moving on elsewhere.
The occupants of a smoking compartment
of the Southern (N.S.W.) mail-train one night
lately, were surprised to see portion of a m
garment flutter in the window, the snowy robe
was grabbed, and next moment shrieks and yells
and wild tugs came from the adjoining compait
ment which was full of ladies, Just then the
train rushed into a station, when it.was foundi that
the tug-of-war was over a feminine night-gown
which a young lady had been airing put of the
window, to take the dampness out ot it.

In one Australian university
a noticeable increase, is observed
in the use of the double eve-
glass among senior students. Ihe
cause may be failing sight from
bad lighting of reading-rooms and
lecture-rooms, or it may be the
desire of the senior student to
get the peculiar physician-look or
surgeon-frown before he gradu-
ates, or it may be, and most likely
is, owing to the fact that a certain
demonstrator, who works at a
salary of £5 per annum, is an
oculist, whose yearly income is
closely under or over =£3ooo a year.
Two showers of “ red rain ”
fell in Beech worth (Yic.) district the
other day. For four days before
this occurred blinding dust-storms
had prevailed throughout the Dar-
ling Downs and Baroo country
of N.S.W., the wind coming from
the north. No doubt cause and
effect, the coloring particles m
the water being probably dust
from wind-tormented squattages
well up towards the Queensland
border, five or six hundred miles
away. So dense are the dust-
clouds on a “ bad” day in that
quarter, and with such great force
are the atoms driven, that, though
most of the. sheep have lately been shorn, the
fleeces from those which were exposed to it in
open country will at the next shearing show
the marks of this storm. _ That, at least, is to be
expected from past experience.

Dear Bulletin,— A par. in your pages of
August 21, which I have just received, discusses
the alleged cleanliness of J. Bull. Now I have
always understood that J.B. was fairly on
water. Was it not Svengali who said the English
must be very dirty, seeing that they took a bath
everyday? Anyway, be the English never so
bad in this respect, they cannot beat the Yankees.
After a 3000-mile railway journey 1 stopped at a
hotel in Indiana, with about an inch deep of dust
On me “A bath ?” I asked the boy who came to
take my baggage. “Yes, sir.” He disappeared,
returning in a minute with a large jug of water.
After a large amount of talking, I made him un-
derstand that I wanted something to lie down in,
even if it were only the old time-honored wash-
tub. “D’ye see, the boss has only just bought
the late proprietor out, and he hasn t had time to
fit out such things yet. However, he is going to
have one by next summer, because a number or
our guests have been asking after it lately.
“Hoop-ray! Cleanliness next to Godliness ! --
Veldt, M.A. College, Michigan, U.S., Oct. 4, J 7

E.D. : Abortive efforts of one White to
get off a paper on evolution before Vic. Congrega-
tional Union once more shows how scared the sects
are at the idea of a combat between Genesis and
Darwin —how they shudder at the bare notion of
Adam and Eve having swung from that apple-tree
by their tails. The Union blocked White’s paper
every time. The Church hates this theme. Left
to choose between the monkey and Moses, it
cleaves to Moses, and, to placate the unconquerable
monkev, adopts monkey whiskers as a sort of
tacit admission of his claims.

The Wages of Sin:—7s. 6d. a week.
Editor-Majah Reay, of Melbourne
Hevenin’ ’Erald, is firing off a series of
lectures at different places, descriptive of
that well chawed-out subject —the Jubilee
in London. The Majah is full to the
muzzle of that glorious event, and his
audiences seem to take him very seriously
indeed. Nothing pleases them more than
the lecturers statement that the British
aristocracy are distinguished for the sim-
plicity and unaffected ness of their man-
ners. And he puts this before his
listeners as a circumstance that took him
up short, and quite upset his precon-
ceived ideas upon the matter. The
Majah gives one the impression that he
would gladly shed his last drop of blood
in defence of an affable dook,
* * * *
The W.C.T.U. Convention at Bal-
larat has been talking of policemen. The
prim delegates have a feeling that the
she-peeler would know best how to deal
with depraved young hussies in big cities.
Apparently the saintly ladies of the
W.C.T.U. are actuated by an earnest
desire to deliver the common bobby from
evil, believing, as they do, that he is led
into temptation when he takes charge of
the forward minx with a rorty little
twinkle in her eye. “ How would we
like our husbands or brothers to under-
take the duty ? ” asked one flushed dele-
gate, and a fierce sniff ran through the
room, accompanied by a facial expression
that meant: “ I’d like to catch them at
it! ” No decision was come to on this
point, but if the W.C.T.U. members
could be prevailed upon to become police-
women the average man would go to
almost any extreme to avoid arrest. He
might even become virtuous.
Ed. Bulletin, —Concerning that rumor of
a proposed marriage between Dhuleep Singh and
the daughter of the Earl of Coventry, it seems to
me that the secret antipathy the Caucasian feels
against any colored race is not so much a matter
of racial hatred, prejudice or sentiment, as simply
—smell. Especially in regard to —let us call it
Love—the powerful influence of the olfactory
nerves has never been duly appreciated. A vast
percentage of human beings fall in love through
the nose (no pun intended), and the nose will be
the main factor in preventing promiscuous inter-
marriage of races, that would thereby suffer for
selective reasons. Why has nobody ever written
—but, hold on, I will do it myself.— Y. Kotze.
[While the writer is at it, let him explain the
“ secret antipathy” of the Caucasian female to the
Chow. —Ed. B.]

in Collins-street; the John Knox, Swanston-street;

to the rescue of Wesley College, after rumors of
its closing. The Wesleyan Queen’s College (Uni-
versity) has a fearful debt, <£HO,OOO. In like
manner, =£lo,ooo has got tied round the throat of
the C. of E. Grammar School, more especially
through the stupidity of building an expensive
chapel for the boys on the premises, where the

that he Goes orthodoxly, without any of the mis-

college. Bishop Goe, on the advice of Old-scholar
Deakin, Prof. M‘Coy, and others, has just promul-
gated a new scheme of management.”
* * * *
Miss Elsie Lander, who is taking a com-
pany to the Rrisbane Theatre Royal to play melo-
drama over the holiday season, is a young English
actress, but, like many others, she made her
greatest hit in the United States, Bora at St.
Heliers, Jersey, the little Channel Islander
played in English provincial companies for a few
years, but when her family came to Australia
abandoned the boards and turned to other pur-
suits. Misfortune—her American press admirers
call it good fortune —drove her back to the stage,
and, taking five promising plays by an Australian
author, she left for New York. There she
played character parts with much success at
Niblo’s Gardens under Harry St. Maur’s stage
management. Finally, the well-known touring
manager, Saunderson, engaged her as his leading
lady, and for over two years she starred with hup,
winning golden opinions all through the West.
An accident compelled her to rest, she re-visited
her friends in Australia, and came to the con-
clusion that, having now completely recovered,
she could charm Australian audiences in the plays
which proved her greatest successes in America.
At all events she means to try, and the Brisbane
season will see her first appearance in Australia.
Miss ELSIE LANDER.
Photo., Crown Studios .

GREAT ARE SOME
PHYSICIANS.
BUT FAR GREATER ARE GOULD’S
TINY TONIC LIVER PILLS,
FOR THEY CURE AFTER PHYSICIANS
FAIL.
So say the people ? The great surprise of the present
day, and one who is most to be pitied is the man with a
weak stomach and a sluggish liver, and who can’t eat
this, and can’t drink that, is miserable and feels disagree-
able, broods over nothing, and can’t see enjoyment in
anything ; sympathy is a nuisance, and friends a bore ;
one day a big appetite, and next day sorry and a great disin-
clination for food. Such is exactly how thousands of
people feel in this colony, and you are likely one of the
thousands. Up to a few years ago stomach and liver
troubles were almost unknown ; but to-day it is the ex-
ception to find one who has a good, strong digestion. In-
numerable cures have been tried and tried by sufferers
without avail, that is up to a short time ago; but now,
owing to the efforts of some physicians in the United
States, a cure has been discovered which not only tones
the digestion and stirs up the liver, but the blood is
cleared of taint and face pimples, heart palpitation, con-
stipation, piles, &c., speedily become a thing of the past.
This preparation is put up in the form of small pills for
convenience sake, and are called Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver
Pills.
You will very likely say, Oh ! this is another patent
medicine. This is true to a certain extent; but not a
patent medicine as the term is usually used. Gould’s
Tiny Tonic Liver Pills are a pharmaceutical preparation,
the formula of which has been improved on, until now a
preparation has been gotten which will bring joy and
happiness to many a home in Australia. Price, 2s. 6d.;
six bottles, 14s. 3d., from chemists and storekeepers or
from the Australian Branch of the E. A. Gould Sons and
Co., 19 Macquarie Place, Sydney.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 9, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
The audience at Florence Schmidt’s day-time
concert last Wednesday made up in warmth what
it lacked in numbers. Flowers were abundant; a
blue lyre was the most striking trophy. The vein
of public interest in the newest soprano has been
worked for all it is worth by the gritful Miss
Schmidt, who has done what no other vocal be-
ginner has done here before —worked up four Town
Hall appearances entirely unsupported by social
props, and relying solely on the strength of her
programmes ; but to boom her farewell financially,
since_ she wishes the European hall-mark on her
training, the usual “large and influential ” com-
mittee would come in usefully. Miss Schmidt
sang two original songlets composed for her by R.
Goring Thomas, and her other three arias had been
sung by the brilliant soprano successfully on former
occasions.
Several recent “happy couples” have gone
overland to spend a quiet honeymoon at Fleming-
ton. They are attracted by the gorgeous new
corridor car which seems to have been specially
built for parties of two. Each compartment is
upholstered in bridal-white brocade, and the
carpet is soft and luxurious, and there are jewelled
mirrors and expensive brass work, and a door that
closes tight on the dainty crib ; one extra-special
saloon bursts into blue plush and wicker furniture.
Lately Redfern has had a festive appearance at
the starting of the Melbourne express, of which
this glorified car forms part, with bridesmaids
flitting about with sweet-smelling posies and
highly-tailored best men, and much rice spilt and
trodden under the unromantic feet of the travel-
ling public, and a glamor of love’s young dream
thrown over the prosaic mail-train. Miss Good-
enough, who was married to Mr. T. Lindsay, at
the Arcadia Hotel, the week before last, has been
at Flemington in a flutter of happiness and French
muslin; and Mr. and Mrs. Mark Sheldon chose
the same sequestered retreat for their honeymoon
paradise.
Miss Ada Stacey, the bicycliste who met such a
terrible death in the Waverley-road by coming
into collision with a tram, had only ridden a wheel
for a month. A mere beginner and she didn’t
hesitate to ride in the great death-trap—Oxford-
street !
Mark Hambourp's favorite, “ Poco a Poco,” by
Franchette, has been published. Strummed out
by the soulless schoolgirl the waltz would be
ineffective, but it repays study, being much more
difficult than appears at first sight. “La Gitana,”
a mazurka, by T. Tourrier, is a commonplace and
lifeless tune in well-marked time.
In a batch of songs from Ricordi and Co., in-
cluding “ Sweetheart Dick,” by C. Blackett, the
“Troubadour Ballad,” by Hillier, and “Last
Year’s Roses,” by F. Leone, the last stands out
as the most tuneful. “ Bygone Days,” by M.
Wellings (Nicholson and Co.), is a useful little
drawing-room ballad, the piano not too difficult,
the voice part not too high. A “ Polonaise,” by
B. Wandlandt (Melb .), is a festive piece sugges-
tive of foreign revelry. The “Tivoli Album ” for
next Xmas has already arrived, containing many
recent Tivoli triumphs and other popular alleged
music, also some of the best-known marches of
Sousa, whose airs flash round the world like a
cable message, and are strummed and banged and
whistled everywhere. “ Washington Post ” heads
the list, but the directions for dancing the fashion-
able jig, given at the top of the page, are calcu-
lated to hurl a ballroom into confusion and drive a
conscientious M.C. to despair. The lady is
directed to stand facing her partner, and both
start off on the left foot. A few bars after such a
start would make dancers’ tracks as complicated
as a cyclist’s road-map. {i"
A Maoriland tenor now in Sydney used to be
pestered to sing at charity concerts mainly got up
to advertise the benevolence of the promoters,
until he hit on the neat device of offering to con-
tribute three guineas (the price at which he valued
his services) to the object, if members of the com-
mittee would throw a guinea apiece into the phil-
anthropic pool. That vocalist was never called
upon to fork over the amount. He talks of re-
peating the expedient here.
The bride of a certain gilded young Australian
was consigned to him from ’Oirie recently—to be
married on arrival if equal to sample, I suppose.
On her way out she actually objected to being
known by her maiden name. She was “ the future
Mrs. X.,” if you please. It was X., X., X., all
the time, until at last a fellow-passenger asked one
of Judge Icefrog’s daughters: “Who are these
X.’s that we hear so much about?” “Well,
really,” was the reply, “ I can hardly tell you. We
have never called on them, and I don’t think we
evah shall! ” She turned, and the f. Mrs. X. was
standing behind her, having heard all.
The Inspector of one of the N.S.W. school dis-
tricts wants his pupil-teachers—male and female—
to wear uniform. It is to be presumed there will be
some distinctive badge besides the back hair.
I don’t think there’ll be much catch-on in that
W.C.T.U. suggestion that married women should
simply tack the husband’s name on to their own.
What chance would Miss Buggins then have of
writing herself Cholomondelev, or Mary Jane
Stubbs of blossoming into Miss De Montmorency ?
Also, which name would the children take, or
would they take both ? In the latter case, a
married woman, four generations from the start,
would have 32 names to carry about with her !
An astounding story is current in Melbourne
re the love-adventure of a very select young man
who comes of one of the Naicest imaginable local
families. At a fashionable hotel he met a lady
who said she belonged to a certain high-toned and
well-gilded set of people whose name you see with
a perfectly damnable iteration in the “ society ”
columns of the daily papers. Well, the young
man became engaged to marry the lady, and wrote
to his mother to announce the fact, adding that his
prospective wife had £2OOO a year of her own,
that they would start for Europe presently, and
spend a year on the Continent. He further said
that his intended was travelling with her aunt—a
perfectly charming old lady and very like the
daughter. But he remembers that he never saw
aunt and niece together, and is now of opinion
that when he thought he was talking to his
aunt-in-law elect he was really speaking with
his sweetheart artfully made up as her own aunt.
Also, the bride-that-was-to-be, to whom the young
man gave some very valuable presents, has disap-
peared from the hotel, leaving behind in her
trunks—ironical woman ! —nothing but the bushel
of letters written her by the young man aforesaid.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“In the days of Gov. Loch, the gardens of
Govt. House used to be thrown open to the guests
on ball-nights, and couples might wander by the
smellful Yarra or into the Domain, or anywhere
out of the fierce light that beats upon a ballroom.
With the advent of Gov. Hopetoun, however, all
was changed, and, with an austerity befitting a
magnate of the Scots Kirk, this youthful lord care-
fully closed every opening, right up to the venti-
lators, and left sassiety to stew in its own gravy
on even the hottest nights. Under Baron Brassey,
however, it is pleasing to note a return to the
humanitarian principles of the ill-named Loch,
for, on the night of the big ball, the doors were
opened as in the days of the boom, and feminine
sassiety was once more free to roam whithersoever
its ‘ boy ’ might desire.
“ At the Brassey ball, the new Speaker, F. C.
Mason, famous, in the ordinary way, for a black
velvet collar to his frock coat, which makes him
look very like an out-of-date ’Arry, burst forth in
kingly garb, topped by a huge crown, and de-
scribed himself as ‘Great Britain,’ for this occa-
sion only. Why Great Britain, nobody could say.
One never thinks of Mr. M. as a.suitable successor
to the good lady who had a jubilee not long ago.
The lawful owner of a portentously grave coun-
tenance chose to appear as the jolly ‘ Bacchus,’
and a well-known sassiety matron rather para-
lysed onlookers by her get-up as ‘Night.’ A
better title would have been ‘Shades of Night,’
for she had seemingly enveloped herself in blue
mosquito-net to keep the flies off. A ‘ Svengali,’
several ‘Othellos,’ ‘Father Neptune,’ and
‘Father Christmas,’ were disguised beyond recog-
nition by even their dearest friends, but it was
sad to notice how many of the faces that
ought to have been put out ot sight were
allowed to remain quite as obvious as usual.
“The women who fancied they resembled Miss
Ferrar, and went as Mercia, were a quite distress-
ing crowd of Early Christians, with rank, “ rat-
gnawed ” tresses a-hanging down their backs, and
not a ghost of a spiritual expression to pull them
through the character. In one case, at least, it
was absurd to think of Mercia being eaten by the
lions, unless the noble beasts hadn’t tasted horse
for a week, and were absolutely famishing.
“The only Julius, after dazzling all beholders as
Romeo in fawn tights at the Brassey ball, retired
into hospital for a serious—not dangerous—opera-
tion which he postponed until after the vice-regal
revel. His Juliet was Mrs. Percy Chirnside, one
of the evening’s belles, whom it is the correct thing
just now to admire. She has much golden hair
which was hanging down her back, but she flat-
tened the front effect by a carefully-netted fringe,
and had a wax-dollish loveliness.
“ The- most-boomed personage of the Cup spree
this year has been the ‘black-eyed Indian Princess,’
as she is always labelled. The title recalls Vol-
taire’s remark about the ‘Holy Roman Empire,’
because the veiled houri isn’t black-eyed—she isn’t
Indian, and she isn’t a princess. Her optics are a
blueish tinge, her complexion is tallowy, her Arab
husband’s rank about answers to that of an English
baronet, and her father was English. Wrapped up
like a parcel in a few yards of obviously Western-
manufactured silk, and an all-concealing veil, she
has waudered into all the festivities, but, as she
doesn’t play speaks with any man except her legal
owner, and shrinks even from talking to other
women, she retains her Oriental mystery. Two
attendants generally play suite to this Eastern
lady and her lord, dressed in slop-tweed suits and
scarlet fez caps.
“Orientals are un-vulgarly reserved. The
women are veiled and the men reticent. “ Ranji ’
is a very shy bird who flutters away from the
strange hand and quietly flicks off the salt which
society tries to put on his tail. He is an under-
sized ‘chop-dollar boy.’
“ This year’s garden-party, a regulation revel of
Cup-week, was a monster fete which scooped into
its net all the little social fishes as well as the big
ones. All the world was there, but not her hus-
band, for most of the sparsely-scattered men were
R. C. bishops and such. The world now in man-
less Melbourne is distinctly feminine; whole
families of women tramp about at every day-time
show, and dismally sit around at evening parties
with never a man. Consequently there has been
much mobbing of celebrities. One of the most
stared-at was a relative of Her Ex.’s, a London
society beauty wedded to a Guardsman, who
glorifies her plain ‘ Smith ’ into four syllables and
has descended into the midst of the gaiety with
many sensational frocks and a determination to
have a good time. Lady Brassey was in grev—a
favored tint this year ; the daughter of Sydney’s
figurehead wore pepper - and - salt silk ; Mrs.
Freeman Thomas kept Mr. F.T. adjacent most
of the time, and was remarkable for her
new style of black lace sash. A pretty
foreigner had one of the many ‘sun-pleated ’
skirts, which style has descended to the ’Arriets
at ’Oine, so snortful English visitors declare ; but
is the newest craze here. The smartest women
with baggy bodice, drawn-in waist, big hips and
frilly flounces, were shaped like cracker bon-bons,
and were all toiletted by contract. It is the cor-
rect form among the Melb. Naicest women to
belong to a toilet club, and three times a week to
be crimped and curled ; they don’t disarrange
their hair between visits to the dresser, and sleep
uncomfortably in their coiffure. A feature of the
show was the carriagelessness of the guests ; the
pompous barouche-and-pair days are evidently
permanently gone. One family, temporarily sub-
merged but now struggling tooth-and-nail to the
surface again, whose carriage used to be one of the
most gleaming of boomers’ equipages, trudged
meekly out of the grounds this time and humbly
trammed it.
“ On Steeplechase-day the frocks were gay and
bright and mostly encores of previous successes.
The most conspicuous chiffoning adorned a grey
gown in multitudinous frillings half-way up the
skirt, and was a walking ad vt. for its wearer’s es-
cort, who keeps a frock-shop. Also, quite the
best of the übiquitous lavender-color gowns was in
the trade; the parallel tucks on the silk-muslin
bodice were its chief glory. The vice-regal femi-
nine contingent presented some contrasts in size
when the Vic. C.J.’s daughter, who inherits the
Stephen plumpness, paced the terrace beside
lengthy Miss Sylvia Darley. The two Brassey
nieces duplicate their costumes usually, and their
father is a typical J. Bull. Several youths and
maidens not connected with Govt. House occupied
the box, which was less-exclusively official than
ever before. A Sydney visitor in fawn, hooped
around with pink, accompanied her mother, in
black-over-green; another wore a wedding
outfit-frock in navy blue with a wormy-
white pattern. One large woman appeared
in a ridiculous straw bonnet, tied an artless
white sash around her ample waist, and looked
like a giant baby in a pantomime; a flat
green plate for a hat and a pinkish dress gave the
sister-in-law of our only Bart, a salmon-salad ap-
pearance. Several stout women were entrapped
by the ‘rich ’ look of pliss6 silk, and were conse-
quently upholstered like stuffed sofas. Fashion
always ignores the fat woman. An innocent in-
fant’s robe-like gown in white silk, flounced
with lace, was probably the most expensive on the
la,wn. Summing up the spectacle sartorially, the
disjointed gaudy-blouse-and-skirt costume still
lingers, but is dying hard; glaring colors and
screaming hats are also have-beens —leaders of
style now array themselves in neutral tints —but a
fad that threatens to be overdone is to trim hats
with green cereals : one girl’s vast hat bore a
wheat-crop that, threshed out, would yield several
bushels per acre, and a railway-official’s wife wore
a bonnet shaped exactly like the one Britannia
rules the waves in.
“ Woman’s contempt for her sister woman is
recklessly shown in the dressing-room at Fleming-
ton on a big day. ‘ Make-up ’ material is supplied
by the V.R.C. in prodigal profusion, and the
chemical beauty hustles through the crowd, secures
the necessary material, and proceeds to thicken
her eyebrows, or heighten or tone down her
color, with a magnificent disregard for criticism.
All day long there is a knot of animated frescoes
about the rouge-boxes and other fittings, and some
extravagant creatures put on several complexions
in distinct layers during the afternoon. It would
be really interesting to know what it costs the
V.R.C. for pearl-powder, and red paint, and
scent, &c., on a Cup-day. It used, by the way, to
cost a good deal for cut-glass receptacles, but the
club has grown wise, and these are now all chained
to the walls.
“ Thanks to a miserable morning and shocking
predictions by the Wragge fraternity, Gaulus’
year will rankle in the memory of woman as ‘ The
Dowdy Cup.’
“ First-comers had prepared themselves for the
worst, and at one time the costume-show was
scrub, rag and bob-tail enough for a Christian
Endeavor Convention.
“Later, as the shadows flew away, the early-
birds shed rain-cloaks and preened flattened
feathers, while the Nicest arrived in flights,
weather-proof, in coats and skirts or solid black
brocades, with such redeeming touches as dressy
boleros and brilliant head-pieces ; but throughout
a glorious afternoon the trail of the rain-fiend’s
morning malice was on the Lawn and over it all.
“Even the Govt. House party got their plumes
draggled. First thing Gov. Brassey’s daughter
did when she arrived in the shower-swept vice-
regal pen was to rectify various minor mishaps to
pa’s race-finery. Also, throughout the the frugal
clan, ‘there was sighing and moaning on ilka
green loaming ’ re poor ‘ uncle Albert’s ’ badly
blistered hat. The grief was infectious, and
everybody within earshot felt sorry for uncle A.’s
hat. Lady Brassey, still haggard and worn
from the effects of her accident, had donned a
black-brocade skirt for the Cup, with an eye to
wet grass ; and wore a cut jet bolero over her
white accord eon-pleated lisse blouse. Her toque,
with its upstanding loops of cerise ribbon, smacked
decidedly of the ten-and-sixpenny bargain table,
and her parasol, to match the ribbon aforesaid,
was openly resented by one or two of the horses in
the bird-cage when Mr. Sep. Miller led her
lydyship around just before the Cup race to
show her Bundook in all its glory. Misses Cox
and Darley, too, judging by their hats, had taken
an over-despondent view of the weather. Miss
Dorothy Brand emerged from her macintosh to
look pleasant in pale-blue and white silk, and a
leghorn flop with cream chiffon and tips.
“Mrs. Freeman-Thomas, who had made up
her mind to chance it, was the belle of the
bunch in black brocade, the skirt flowered
with pink, the deep-pink bodice blushing through
ruby-spangled net. The crown of her pink straw
hat was half-buried in mauve-tinted roses, and
here and there from her long neck-chain hung all
manner of cute little trinkets, prettier even than
those tiny, jewelled fal-lals which make Miss
Elliott Page envied by all the girls on the Block.
Lady Holled-Smith, that reminds me, sported a
pet of a ‘ pansy watch ’ at the races, and several
jewelled belts were worn. Mrs. Fred. Fairbairn’s
was gold galon studded with coral, and the same
dark-eyed Bananaland belle lent brightness to her
magpie silk and ivory satin by a deep yellow hat.
Brilliant-hued satin straw, by the way, was the
feature of race millinery. Mesdames Freeman-
Thomas, Everard Browne, Ernest De Little, Lady
Holled-Smith, and dozens more of the smartest
watched the Cup run with fleeting blushes on their
cheeks born of rose-pink hat-briins. Orange and
green were equally popular, red evidently on the
wane.
“On the whole, I believe sashes were the plums of
the show. Not ribbon, but made sashes, muslin
frilled with chiffon silk edged with lace orr iceversa.
Nothing too airy for waist streamers, from lace or
lisse scarves to gauze flyaways. Sometimes the
sash costs more than all the rest of the costume
put together, and brides, prescribing these costly
flimsies for their attendant nymphs, are expected
to order and pay for them along with the wedding-
gown. Apropos of trousseaux, no self-respecting
silk sleeping-robe is now made without quantities
of cummerbund. Fashion-prophets predict that
the sash-craze will soon affect all feminine under-
wear. But— ‘ I dinna ken hoo that can be,
Jeanie ’ unless, indeed, we all take to tea-gowns.
The very thought makes one feel embonpointy.

More or less substantial boots were naturally
a sine qua, non of the recent rainy Cup. One sweet
young thing in true-blue paddled over the damp
grass in satin slippers, to match ; but goloshes
were in the ascendancy with the masses, which
bears out the contention that it was a ‘ dowdy Cup.’
“ Tourist Albert Brassey, at present in Mel-
bourne, is a sparse, grey-moustachioed, young-old
patriarch, with winning little ways of winking
the other eye and showing his teeth insinuatingly
as he talks. Maybe when they were boys together
he annexed his pretty playfulness as an antidote
to big brother’s gloom, there being less pecuniary
inducement to take himself and his career
seriously than in the case of son and heir Thomas.
Nowadays, so far as one can see, the brethren
prefer lone-hand frisks to fraternal communion.
Nevertheless, they can’t conceal a strong family
likeness, common characteristics being short tem-
pers, long tongues, and paternal pride.
“ Not only does Gov. Brassey carry on with his
youngest cherub as if she were his one ewe lamb,
but whenever his eye falls on pretty Mrs. Free-
man-1 homas his face lights up, and Mr. Pickwick
himself couldn’t look a nicer, more benevo-
lent old thing. For her part, the crisp belle in
question has quite the air of successfully exploit-
ing dad s soft side, and walks in silk attire, so to
speak, whenever one sees her —far, far more than
can be said of all the family belongings.
“ At the Clip, the Miss Brasseys, as sar-
torially simulated sisterly sympathy in twin cos-
tumes of black satin, picked out with piebald silk,
and sterotyped toques wreathed with conven-
tional pink roses. Mrs. Skeffington Smythe (fact,
names are stranger than fiction), harmonious in
grey and white, shared the carriage of honor with
her hostess. Local society - scribes identify the
lady as one of the beauteous twin-sisters presented
at Court the same season as an equally lovely
sisterly brace, brothered by Aide Neville.'
“ Some of the costliest Cup-costumes owe their
being to paying gravel in the Golden West. One
Coolgardie visitor candidly confessed as much by
the color of her gown—satin the color of new
sovereigns sparkling under black gauze.
“ The mention of ‘ gravel ’ suggests Bananaland
and its gold kings. The Misses Dempster and
their married sister, who keep house for their be-
reaved brother-in-law, Mt. Morganaire Wesley
Hall, did the Flemington lawns in black and
white. There are three Misses Wesley Hall, by
the way, ‘catches ’ of the future,’ pretty children
generally dressed in white, white satin frocks,
quaint Empire bonnets and chiffon fichus, and
white kid boots which, for heedless young wearers,
must take plenty of pipeclaying.
“Mrs. Frank Murphy, daughter of another
Mt. Morganaire (M.L.A. Callan), came down
from Brisbane on her honeymoon trip, visiting her
relations the Duffys, and seeing the Cup, cn
passant, in a trousseau frock of gauze over pale-
blue silk.
