Once, when a very junior barrister was
defending a prisoner before Judge Windeyer, he
riled that gentle Judge by continually referring to
“My unfortunate client.” At last, Ventosior
pulled him up with, “I quite agree with you
there, sir.”
A member of the Bathurst Suttor house
was an Australian poet of the camp-fire order. A
huge mass of his eccentric manuscript still sur-
vives. Luckily, he drew an income from the
Suttor property, and was thus able to give his
heart to digger minstrelsy.
A well-known N.S. W. barrister, addressing
the jury : “ My client was not to be intimidated !
He took the bull by the horns, and indicted him
for perjury.” And, on another occasion, “My
unhappy client received the seducer into his happy
home. He placed a serpent in his bosom, but it
stung him behind his back.”
A Syme simile, verra Scotch :
For anybody with half an eye can see that to abrogate
the powers and prerogatives of the newspaper at this
time of the day would be as disastrous to society as it
might be to banish the use of small tooth-combs.
Evidently Dawvid has no high falutin' notions
about the province of the Age.
A renegade Wesleyan parson, turned
Church of England, was in the Anglican charge
of Fiji when Hercules Robinson came. Parson .
“I suppose, Sir Hercules, you will have Divine
service, at the inauguration, after the rites of the
Established Church?” Hercules: “No, sir, I
shall not have any service at all, but, if I did, it
would be that of the brave Wesleyans.”
“ American-English Ambassador Kernel
John Hay,” writes a Bulletin correspondent from
England, “ has lived to repent perpetrating ‘ Jim
Bludso,’ complimentary reference to which is
dinned into his ears at every social function he
attends. The joke is that Hay himself is rather
ashamed of a die-to-save yarn which comes peri-
lously near anti-climax. Some of the choicest
Amurkan humor lies in Amurkan would-be senti-
ment.”
The lately-deceased Fish, M.H.R. for
Dunedin, was a humorist, with heaps of leather
and a voice of brass. After an election, once, a
supporter accosted him with, “ Look here, Mr.
Fish ; before the election, every time you saw me
you stopped and shook hands. Now, you ignore
me altogether.” “ Well,” said Fish, “ the fact of
the matter is this: Before the election, I’ll kiss
your big toe; after the election, you can kiss
mine.”
The exit of Graham Berry recalls the cir-
cumstances of his first entry into Parliament. In
July, '6l, a vacancy occurred for E. Melbourne.
Edward Cohen and one “Pat’ O’Brien were
candidates. Before the day of nomination the
Heales Govt, was beaten on a motion by Dr.
Hedley, of Gippsland, and Gov. Barkly granted a
dissolution, which was to take place in a few days.
Cohen and O’Brien agreed that it would be use-
less to incur the expense of an election under the
circumstances, and they agreed to nominate a
gilded absentee, C. H. Ebden, then on his way
from England. This arrangement did not give
general satisfaction when disclosed at the nomina-
tion, and M. D’Arcy, publican, of Swanston-
street, proposed Graham Berry, then among the
crowd at the hustings. The show of hands was
in favor of Berry, and the Cohen-O’Brien party
not demanding a poll, Berry was declared elected,
and took his seat that night, introduced by Heales
and J. H. Brooke, now of the Japan Herald.
Next day Parliament was dissolved.
N. : “ The most ferocious enemy Graham
Berry ever had was long-demised Garratt, of
Geelong. This man pursued him, in and out of
Parliament, like a sleuth-hound, and was egged-on
by a conscience of double-distilled piety. When
Garratt got a Royal Commission, he thought he
had ‘ copped ’ Berry out. The most absurd
rumors were circulated about Berry having gone
on his knees in tears, and all that. Garratt used
to go about boasting he had stabbed the balloon
and let out all the gas. He stuck at nothing.
Yet Berry emerged triumphant. The thing was
overdone. Politicians said the beam was a gnat.
Berry entered on the most glorious period of his
whole career, and holy Mephistopheles retired to
Geelong cemetery.”
Seng Chai, an important personage among
the Lambing Flat rioters of 36 years ago. died
last week at Cowra (N.S.W.). Chai was sent to
Lambing Flat as part spy and part detective.
The dispute between the diggers and the Chinese
had developed into bitter civil war, and Cowper,
the Premier of the time, sent to the locality a
little army of artillery, infantry and mounted
police. Chai’s duty was to report the designs and
doings of his countrymen, so that the Govt, forces
would be able to operate without adding to the
already deplorable plight of the yellow-skins. He
did this work fairly well, and was four years
afterwards employed by Government in confiden-
tial capacities. But, like most men who take up
such work, he often came in for blame from both
sides.
“G. 8.,” on a dynastical question:.—
Dear Bulletin, —There is a movement afoot at Ballarat
to erect a monument of some sort to the memory of King:
Billy. If this foolish thing is done the wrong man will
get the honor, as usual. The nigger recognised by this
young and credulous generation as King Billy was not
the genuine article. In the early fifties he was a waif and
stray of the Buninying tribe —loafing around for six-
pennies and drinks. One day on the main road he dis-
covered the king—the real royal Billy—drunk and
sleeping under a tree. Here was an opportunity for
collaring a throne and a dynasty with one stroke of a
waddy. He did what has been done over and over again
by the King Billys of history : he killed his liege lord,
and, taking from his neck the brass insignia of his rank,
placed it around his own. Then he walked into Ballarat
and levied tribute. In those day's men were busy with
other matters than revolutions involving the rights and
wrongs of royal houses, so his claims were not disputed.
He was asked by some people where the king was ; but he
satisfactorily'disposed of their curiosity by the statement
that “ King Billy along gone a hell—this feller King Billy
now ” —and little more was said. However, his claims were
recognised by Salmon Wilson, who himself was a pre-
tender to descent from King Alfred and kinship with Her
Gracious. So let the movement rip along.— G.B.

It was Melb. Argus which
officially slew Judge Windeyer—
with its brutal, gentlemanly sub-
leader comparing him with bloody
Jeffreys, at the trial of Alice
Lisle. That Argus article cut
Windeyer to the heart. It ren-
dered impossible a continuance
of the aggressive support he had
previously received from the Re-
spectable' Press of Sydney—it
focussed the Australian public eye
on his uncounted judicial sava-
geries— it wrested from him his
coveted Privy Councillorship. It
is noteworthy that the recently-
published Apology of the now-
deceased Judge was made to
“E.S.C.” (Mr. E. S. Cunning-
ham), leader of the Argus staff,
now in London,
A memorial to deceased
Baron Von Mueller should
gratify the spirit of that grand
old toiler, who asked nothing
from the public save honorable
recognition of his life’s work. In
appealing for memorial funds the
Baron’s executors repeat the
statement made in a Bulletin
par 12 months ago, that he was
greatly “ soothed ” in his eleventh
hour by the gift of a first-class
grave-plot from the trustees of St.
Kilda cemetery. Any mark of
distinction for the botanist was
dear to old-man Von Mueller.
As he lay on his death-bed
nothing could have given him
greater pleasure than the arrival
of an undertaker armed with
authority from the Vic. Govern-
ment to execute the Baron’s personal order for a
gilt-edged coffin.
Innocence in high places. Some time ago
N.S.W. Chief-Justice Darley was trying a man-
slaughter-case on circuit. A policeman giving
evidence was asked what prisoner said on his
arrest. “He said, ‘Where is my (sanguinary)
hat ?’ ” The Crown-Prosecutor did not seem to
notice this, and the C. J. interposed, “ But that
is very important ; I understand the prisoner
asked for his bloody hat; now, if his hat was”
Here counsel “ rose to explain.”

The late millionaire Neale, of Sydney,
bequeathed—in amounts ranging up to <£sooo
.£13,000 to ten lawyers—and, it would ap-
pear, <£2s to his cook! Which is strictly
in accordance with the eternal unfitness of
things ; because, however much the spoil-
scooping fraternity may help a man in the pil-
ing of his coin, it is only by leave of his digestion
that he can properly enjoy it. The genial
Dailey was wiser in his generation. “ I have
met with a terrible misfortune ! ” he said
once, in serio-comic distress, to the present
writer —“ I have lost my cook! No more
of those exquisite little fish-dinners, those
marvellous curried prawns and devilled
oysters ! But, one poor consolation, at
least, is left me —we parted the best of
friends: poor soul, I kissed her both
cheeks and gave her my benediction.
An old man named Sykes, found dead
in his bed at a Melb. pub., the other week, was
reputed to be a brother of SirTatton Sykes, a one-
time famous Yorkshire sport and pal of the Prince
of Wales. Sir Tatton is quite ancient history,
but this Sykes, who drew remits, from ’Ome, may
have been a genuine black sheep of the family,
exported to Australia in the old orthodox way.
Australia, as a dumping-ground for black sheep, is
going out of fashion. It isn’t as far away as it
was, also the British aristocracy finds the country
to be much too healthy for prodigal nephews.
The fashion nowadays is to send ’em to places
where they are more likely to die unobserved at
an early date. '

SAVED BY A NIP.
Marvellous escape of Michael O'Rafferty , of the Dingo Arms.
Each of Vic.’B Speakers, since old Peter
Lalor, has had the bad luck to be connected with
some bung bank or other. Mat Davies’s Mug-and-
guile Bank is a recent event, and both Bent’s and
Berry’s transactions, though of lesser effluvium,
are just as close at hand. But the financial
trouble of Frank Mason, the present Speaker, was
away back in the 70’s, just after he first left his
schoolmaster’s billet, on H.M.S. Nelson, to enter
Parliament. Mason was then a director of the
Provincial and Suburban Bank, and its smash
deprived him of nearly every penny he possessed.
Canon Rich, the C.E. pastor of Darling-
hurst gaol, thinks that his successor in office
should be a plain “reverend.” A modern
Barrington composed a distich in which lne
Word of God at the can(n)on’s mouth figures,
and the pleasantry has been so contagious that the
canon’s ministrations even on the gallows lose
gravity.
COCKY'S GOT HIS BISCUIT
(A backyard sketch.)
There was killed in a Vic. mine, the other
day, William Bradley, an elderly miner, who had
known in a conspicuous degree the ups and downs
of mining. Years back he was manager of Koch
and Hilderbrandt’s (now Lansell’s 222, at Bendigo)
and afterwards of Burrowes’ Freehold. Then he
took heavy tributes, and made a pot of money out
of the New Chum and Victoria ; played it up on
other tributes and mining specs., and so got back
at last to hammer and drill. But Bendigo gave
him a “good funeral,” anyway.
Time was when Kipling was anathema
maranatha to the gentry of the black robe. They
voted his ballads “vulgar” and his yarns im-
moral ” ; he was “an unsaved worldling, only
one degree less obnoxious than Zola himself. But,
mark the change. The “Recessional Hymn ' has
been published, with its cant refrain—an effusion
which, had it appeared eight years ago, would
have caused scarcely a word of comment, but at
which, now that Rudyard has been hoisted to
fame by appreciative (if unregenerate) millions,
the parsonic person holds up his hands and unc-
tuouslv exclaims, “ How treuly be-eautiful ;
calls Kipling “the great poet of the British
Empire,” and preaches a sermon from said refrain,
on to which he grafts sundry Divine maxims. So
Kipling has conquered the realms of darkness and
light, and, willy-nilly, is canonised a saint of the
second magnitude.
Butters the perennial (Melb.) is arranging
a smoke-night picnic for 40-year-old colonists.
Butters himself has been, o\ ( r that time in these
parts —counting Fiji in, Baillie and Butters were
in business in Melbourne in the early fifties.
Baillie died at Levuka or thereabouts. Butters
lived, to mystify Thakombau, and play at
Government.’ Afterwards he ran the great dia-
mond, and was “ had ” properly—the only time
on record. A wonderful man. Age has no show
with him. He is as he was 30 years ago. Wears
the same sort of coat —blue frock, Amurican
shoulders ; hasn’t a wrinkle or a grey hair ;is as
vain of his foot as ever, and with reason. A step
as elastic as when, ages since, he trod his native
heath. And always has a young and handsome
wife. Is that the secret - ? David, the King-
just so.
S. V. Winter, ■ general manager of Melb.
Hekald, is going ’Ome. He has never been out
of Australia before, but as his - editor, Majah Reay,
has been introduced to Queen Victoria and hob-
nobbed with dukes, Winter feels in duty bound to
do likewise. S.V. W. is the champion —let us call
jt—objurgatorof Australasianjournalism. “Sayyid
Burgash in a tantrum” was a mild-mannered man
compared with him. He can objurgate for an hour
on end without twice using the same oath. To be
present while S. V. Winter stood by the “formes”
in the old days when foreman Bannon was
“ making-up ” was a liberal education in the great
Australian art of anathema. The present writer
has heard it debated whether Winter was English
or Irish, but has always maintained that he was a
true-born Australian. No mere foreigner could
swear like that.
“A journalist stationed at Berlin, and
who knoweth all things that move, has sent me
the following explanation of two apparently-un-
connected and believable accidents,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent. “On a recent
cruise of the German Emperor’s yacht, Hohen-
zollern, a triviality and a fatality occurred.
William, the Lord’s anointed, got a black-eye
from a bit of loose rigging, and Lieut. Hahnke,
one of his A.D.C’s., biking at Odde, Norway, off
which the yacht lay anchored, was run away with
by hxs machine, and dashed to death over the
cliffs. He got almost a royal funeral—he had
earned it. The G.E. is noted for his contempt of
women and his steady, moderate p’raps, devotion
to Beer. These yacht trips are mere drinking-
bouts, to which only seasoned swillers are in-
vited, and thus it is that scandals re “ divine
service ” being celebrated by the royal crank and
other dotty-isms have had their existence. It
was one evening after dinner, and in the heavy
drinking, that the Emperor made a terribly-in-
sulting remark about Hahnke’s mother, the wife
of General Hahnke, Von Moltke’s friend. Like
a man the lieutenant blackened his Sovereign’s
eye, and, like a fool, next day committed unmis-
takeable suicide. P’raps he knew his autocratio
master too well, though.”

Aboriginalities.
A 44 lapidary’s ” romance, as told to a Bul-
£mtin man. He was sitting on his heap of broken
metal, eating mutton sandwiches and drinking
beer out of a billy—a regular 44 hatter ” type. Did
it never occur to him to get married ? No, not
exactly, but he was pretty near the same as being
married once. He was breaking on the roads, in
a rural district, and took up with a “bloke”
whose hut was handy to his tent. One night he
was in the bloke’s hut i( doin’ a pitch,” when the
bloke was mending an old coat. Noticing how
handy the latter was with the needle, the “ broker ”
asked him to tinker up his old trousers. The bloke
said he would, and the old trousers were stripped
off and handed over. 44 An’ I jes’ perched round,
sociable like, m my boots, while he patched up my
duds. Well, that was all right. Nex’ day, I
shifted down to another pitch, an’ what do I hear
on the follerin’ Sunday but that the bloke was
dead, an’ it seems he was a blanky woman all
along. That’s ’most as bad as bein’ married, mate,
ain’t it ? ” ’
* * * *
In the days when the Georgina River was
called the Herbert, Jack Skuthorpe (of Leichhardt
relics fame) and his partner were looking after
their cattle on some country they had taken up on
that river, and, running short of rations and
ammunition, Jack’s partner started in to Stony
J™’ some 300 miles, leaving Jack with but
half-a-dozen cartridges. Seeing only one man,
the nigs, grew saucy—whereupon Jack hit on a
brilliant idea. He got his boy, a Herbert River
native, to explain to the myalls that his boss had
only to point his finger at anything and it would
go bung.” Then he strewed round the camp
bits of poisoned beef, which were at once pounced
upon by the hawks, and when one gave evidence
of feeling uncomfortable, Jack would point his
finger at him ; presently down he would drop.
After the third hawk died the myalls departed,
believing their turn would come next. Jack was
known to the natives as “Good Many,” possibly
from the number of nigs, he had helped to dis-
perse but probably from the number of 44 fairy-
tales he used to pitch.

The present champion pea-picker at Carlingford is Mr.

* * * *
The screaming incongruity of prevalent
child-naming is nowhere more apparent than
in the bash. After a storm Aubrey Clifford,
Douglas, Beatrice, and so forth—ragged, wild-
haired and bare-legged—may be seen rushing,
whooping and yelling, to the filth-laden gullys and
wallowing with squeals of delight in the muddy
water. Claud Stanley grubs stumps, digs d ins ;
Raymond Clarence repairs the roads ; Harold and
Leslie are cursing, tobacco-chewing teamsters ;
Grace Naomi, gnawing a “ror” onion, sits serenely
amidst an accumulation of filth with a 3d.
novelette; Mona May is a muscular masculine
damsel with coarse hair and a red skin glowing
c .T 1 ..y through a cloud of freckles, who boasts her
a bill ty to ride, fight or swear with “any man;”
and Olive Vivienne plods about the farm attired
in the greater part of a blouse, the lesser part of a
skirt, and a pair of large feet encased in an ancient
pair of man’s boots. No wonder the Naicest are
falling back on “simple names.*
* * * •
The other day, near Barcaldine, a man
driving a waggon, laden with some three tons of
hardwood, slipped and fell. The wheel passed
over his legs close to the thighs, and bruised him
severely. Never a bone was broken, and the
tough Centralian resumed his journey im-
mediately.

Boondingee : “ 4 Ben Hall ’ can’t be
‘counted a bush-song,’ for an old friend of mine
told me, long ago, that he had heard 4 Ben Hall ’
(or 4 Sam Hall ’). sung in London pubs, before he
oame to Australia (much over 30 years ago), with
the 4 Damn your eyes ’ refrain and all. Real bush-
songs (the words themselves are proof of their
Australian origin) are 4 The Wild Colonial Boy ’
(Jack Dowhng) and ‘General Sessions They’ve
Gone Past/ both of which I well remember hear-
ing sung in the country 20 years ago. But they
are both mere doggerel, and utterly unworthy of
immortality, except as curiosities.”
* * * *
Something about a Barcoo shepherd. In
the latter end of the ’7o’s, 44 Old C ’’ was
P.M. at Blackall, on the Barcoo. Blackall being
a very bush town, his worship did not go in for
orthodox P.M. rig, but, adapting himself to
climatic conditions and local fashion, wore only a
dark Crimean shirt, in addition to the usual pants,
bluchers, and slouch felt hat. This outfit, with a
water-bag of more than ordinary dimensions, and
his coat hung over his arm, completed the P.M.’s
uniform as he appeared en route to court. At his
heels a mongrel, a waif which had originally taken
to kindly Old C by reason of the fact that,
seeing a vagrant dog hungry in the alleged street,
the old man had 44 shouted ” for it at the butcher’s.
In this trim Blackall’s chief magistrate was not a
bad impersonation of a Barcoo shepherd, and a
drover passing through Blackall with sheep, and
inquiring for a hand, had the P.M. pointed out
by a local joker as “the very man he wanted.”
The drover rode up to C . “Good day,
mate. lam Mr. S , in charge of Bobiang
sheep, and can give you a six months’ job at forty
bob a week ; so pack up smart and come down to
the camp.” “Yes ? ” replied his Worship; “and
lam Mr. C— , m charge of this town, and can
give you a six months’ job at nothing a week ”
(pointing to the look-up). “So pack up your
swag and clear —unless you want the job.”
R. : “ Apropos ‘ Ponte ’ on
bush inter-marriage (Oct. 16).
In the Mornington peninsula,
Yie., close to Port Phillip Heads,
is a big district wholly populated
by Pattersons and Oairnses,
descendants of two patriarchs
who settled there long ago. So
mixed are they by continuous
inter-marrying that they have
to be sorted out as Tom’s Jack’s
Jim’s Peter Cairns, and Dun-
can’s Murdoch’s Donald’s Will
Patterson. In the local state
school the teacher makes all the
Pattersons sit on one side of the
school and the Cairnses on the
other; yet such is the facial
resemblance of the youngsters
that the teacher is never sure
whether they have sorted them-
selves out according to order or
not.”
* * *
Nothing refreshes the
bucolic mind so much as an ac-
cidental mistake in defining
the sex of an animal. “ Was
the bullock wild ? ” asked soli-
citor Levien in Tamworth court,
the other day. “Yes.’ “And
was its mother a wild bullock
also? ” Tamworth is still laugh-
ing, and for a year or two the
local booksellers have no chance
of selling a copy of Mark Twain
or any other humorist.

X.Z. : “.Re rabbits climb-
ing trees, &c. ‘ Balmalcolm ’
asks how would a Scotch game-
keeper swallow the statement
that rabbits can swim the
Darling, when it is running a
banker? Well, he’d swallow all
right, for he would know that,
when beasts swim, their action
is precisely the same as when
walking on land, and he would
also know that all mammals—
if not, indeed, all four-footed
beasts —can swim. How would
‘ Balmalcolm ’ swallow the state-
ment that I have seen a hen swim
across a pond 20ft. wide by 80ft.
long, for no apparent reason but
that she wanted to get to the other side and was
too lazy to walk round dry-footed ? That’s as
good as his acrobatic rabbits.”
• * * *
To show what fly-flats some countrymen
are. Jim and Dan had come down with stock and
put-up at a decent Melb. pub. one night. The
landlord, hearing them say next morning that
they were going out to the yards to see the boss,
said, “Stay where you are, and I’ll speak to him.”
He rang-up and got the answer back. A short
time after both men told him they were going to
leave. 44 What for ?” said the landlord. “Well,
it’s about time,” said they, 44 when ye take us for
a pair of mugs—trying to kid us ye been speaking
to the boss. It’s the confidence-trick ye’re trying
on.” And off they went.

The tale of a Gulf squatter. During the
Batavia River rush two diggers started overland
from Croydon. Getting out on the Mitchell they
resolved to call at station, as their ration-
MALCOLM M‘EACHARN, Melbourne's
Mayor.
Mrs M'EAGHARN, Mayoress of
Melbourne .
Photos., Stewart Co., Melb.
bags were empty. From directions received they
guessed they ought to be somewhere near the
station, but, towards sundown, coming across an
old yard on a lagoon, they camped and made a
fire at one end of a hollow log. Presently, hear-
ing a coughing noise in the log, they went to the
far end from the fire and were astonished to see a
man crawling out on his hands and knees. From
his appearance he had apparently been there for
days. In angry tones he asked what the blazes
they meant by setting fire to his premises. On
being assured that they were only camping there
for the night and were making for station, he
said loftily: “ This is station. lam the
owner, and I make it a rule never to allow
travellers to camp so near the head-station; so
you’d better git.” They gat.

* * • *
A Coolgardie bar-belle returning from the
Jubilee with her employer was much admired by a
young Anglican clergyman until, one day in the
Red Sea, the parson remarked that “it was very
hot, but very nice and interesting doncherknow.”
Said the fair one, “Hot, yes—the apostles were
mad to live in a country like this.” Then, noting
the shepherd’s anguish—“ Don’t you know who I
mean ? The people we used to read such a lot
about at school. Of course they could go about
dressed in a sort of sheet, but we can’t. You
wouldn’t like to see me in a sheet! ” Whereupon
his reverence “retiah’d.”
* » * *
The manager of a large Sydney financial
institution was unkindly recognised recently,
thus: A country bumpkin came in requesting a
loan on security, but he was hard to deal with,
and, to thaw him, the manager suggested a drink.
Thinking to delight the man with his affability,
he donned a long coat and shining topper and took
him to the Metropole instead of treating* him in
his own sanctum. Drinks were called for and
drunk, when the manager found he had left his
pocket-book in his other coat, and laughingly ex-
plained this to the barman, who was acquainted
with him. But Mr. O’Cocky from the Big Scrub
was on the alert. “ I’ve been had that way
before!” he shouted, and bolted as if the devil was
after him.

The old order changeth giving place to the
w in municipal matters, and the incoming Mayor
of Melbourne, Malcolm Donald M‘Eacharn, is not
a ponderous potentate who drbps aitches on the
floor, or refers proudly to the time when he had
only tuppence in his pocket. On the contrary, he
wears a waxed moustache, and is otherwise calcu-
Jij'ked to run the city show on Madden—meaning
Chief Justice M.—principles. His verra Scottish
name is tempered by the fact that the firm of
Mi Ilwraith, Mi Eacharn and Go. started business
as ship-owners in London, migrating to Aus-
tralia when the subject of this notice was still
a very young man. He is now a few years
short of 50, and comparatively juvenile at that,
.things have always gone well with the firm in
Australia, according to all accounts, so it . was
Tv/r Ute ni- n T^ e natura l order of such matters that
Mr MeEacharn came to marry a daughter of
millionaire J. B. Watson. By so doing the
Mayor-elect has given Melbourne sassiety a
chance to pay its respects to the Mayoress with
exceptional fervor, and should Providence some
day see fit to place a * handle ’ before the
name of MeEacharn—and Providence has it
in view, The Bulletin supposes—the lady
will share her husband’s greatness in her own
right, so to speak. The new Mayor can afford
to enjoy himself in office; but he is rather
troubled by a newspaper rumor to the effect that
he prides himself on the possession of a silver
tenor. As a matter of fact, he has felt sorrv for
his voice ever since the elders of the kirk offered
to supply a way-back pew gratis, as an inducement
for him to retire from the reserved stalls, where
his warble, they said, was driving the rest of the
aristocracy away.

A Sharebroker's “ Tip.”
It is not always safe to take advice from even
the best of sharebrokers, but when it results from
experience, as in the case of Mr. L. W. Wells,
Sharebroker, 78 Collins Street, one cannot fail to
recognise its value. Mr. Wells is one of Hobart s
prominent Sharebrokers, and his numerous friends
and acquaintances know that he never advises
without a knowledge as to the value of the invest-
ment, and he does not hesitate to advise Warner ■
“ Safe ” Cure for kidney and liver diseases. He
says: “I gladly give my testimony to the valu-
able properties contained in Warner’s ‘ Safe
Cure. Some years ago I was attacked by severe
and acute pains in my back, which almost doubled
me in two. Severe headaches, accompanied by
palpitation of the heart, loss of appetite, with a
frequent desire to urinate. Upon medical advice
I gleaned I was suffering from kidney complaint,
and was induced to purchase Warner’s ‘ Safe
Cure, with the result that after taking four
bottles I waß able to resume my duties in this
city.”
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Speoifio for Live*
and Kidnet Disease.*

Even when a man stands 6ft. 4in. it
is seldom that he can turn the soale at 17st
without appeal ing and being clumsy. Yet
to glance at the figure of Dr. W. J. M.
Barry, the Irish giant now tossing huge
masses of iron over the surface of sunny Aus-
tralia, nobody would know he was a giant
were it not for the comparative insigni-
ficance of surrounding objects. Barry hails
from the fastnesses of Tipperary, where he
was born 35 years back, and where his
prowess as a weight-putter and Rugby
footballer earned him fame before he opened
his Quain’s Physiology preparatory to a
'medical course at Queen s College, Cork.
Taking his degree there, the massive Barrj
began practising at Southport, Eng., and
people at once began to wonder why Ireland
was less downtrodden. Among the Saxons,
Barry recommenced hurling weights about
despite the fact that so small an island as
England is manifestly unfitted for such exu-
berant conduct. However, after losing
several hammers in the German sea and a
couple of shots in St. Georges Cha.nnel,
Barry’s aim perfected, and in competitions
he scored off the hated Sassenach with
vigor. Dividing the shot-putting cham-
pionship in ’Bl, Barry carried it off the two
succeeding years, putting up 42ft. lOin. on
the last occasion. The 161 b.
ing championship he won in 85 with 108 ft.,
but by four years later he increased j} l ® d lB '
tance to 130 ft., and at ’92 he established a
world’s record with 137 ft. Even this he has
recently succeeded in passing with 138 ft.
10in., which still stands as a world s record
for the wooden-handled, iron-headed harn-
mer. Flanagan, a compatriot of Barry,
holds a record of 157 ft. with a lead-and-wire
hammer, but the English athletic world re-
fuses to recognise this Yankee innovation ; and anyhow,
Barry met and beat Flanagan during the present year.
Phil Sheridan’s definition of a cricketer: “A
voung man with so little brains that he must devote all
Chas entirely to cricket.” This definition has some
notable instances: Spofforth in his palmy days used to
eat drink, talk, and sleep with cricket, and Banjitsinhji
will’ stop night to talk about the noble game.

Mac. : “Jim Meenan used to tell a yarn of
another set-to he had near Gundagai. Riding along the
road he found a bullock-team pulled up on a bridge oyer
a creek. Bullocky, asked to move, talked fight, and Jim
got off to have a go. After about five minutes bullocky
stopped and pulled off his vest, when Meenan stopped—a
hot one on the jaw. Five minutes more, and bullocky
stooped again to take off his shirt—and again Jim got it
on the jaw. When bullocky stopped a third time to
null off his singlet Jim got on his horse and orossed the
oreek lower down. Bullocky hadn't learned from a book.

Some of the Melb. undertakers give commission
on orders. V.R.O. course; dead jock, taken in cart to
casualty-room. Tall gent, stops driver, takes out his
pocket-book, lifts his hat and says: “Beg pardon, sir,
oblige me with name and address of the unfortunate
youth. It’s fearful sad.” Driver: “ Are you a reporter,
sir’” Tall Gent. : “Oh, no, I am travelling for Stiffem
and Co., the cheap undertakers. Please accept our card.
The ambition of the Graziers’ Meat Co. (Bris-
bane), recently collapsed, was to raise a cricket-team able
to hold its end up against all comers, and any cricketer of
repute was certain of a billet upon application ; but
results show that a crack cricket-team doesn’t reduce the
overdraft. In the good old days, before the smash, at
least two Sydney banks ran on the same lines, and
Buckley’s show was the man’s who could not play cricket.
An officer of one of the banks aforesaid impudently said,
in his letter of resignation, “ I much regret that the fact
of my not being a cricketer, or related in any way to the
general manager, compels me to look further afield.

THE BOSTON SHOWER.
When one is hot and dry as summer dust, a
shower bath is probably the most welcome form of
refreshment possible—if obtainable. But even in
our city homes this is not always available, while
it is not too much to say that away back in the
country many thousands of would-be cleanly
people know of no shower save that which is
Heaven sent, when
The hooded clouds like friars,
Tell their beads in drops of rain.
The lack of a good water supply, want of space,
and, not least, the expense of fitting up a room are
some of the causes preventing the general posses-
sion of what is both a luxury and a necessity—a
111 Jil i . .r'limp |B
•v
>
I
pod shower bath. There are few difficulties,
however, which modern ingenuity cannot over-
come, and we give an illustration of a cheap,
compact, and satisfactory arrangement by means
of which anyone—be he an isolated selector or a
city man m cramped quarters—can enjoy to the
full all the healthy comfort of a good bath. The
Boston Shower (introduced by Messrs. M. Moss
and Co., Wynyard-lane, Sydney and Melbourne)
can be fixed by anyone, anywhere, in a few
and the pump connected with it, worked
with the greatest ease, gives a continuous down-
pour, having a force sufficient to throw water 150
feet. A single bucket of water is ample for a most
satisfactory shower. The simplicity and small
cost (375. 6d.) of the Boston will appeal to all ex-
cepting those who do not need a bath, and of such
we may say “Everyone is as God made him—and
sometimes a good deal worse.” — Sydney Mail
Oct. 16, 97.

PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD.
50 Young-street,
. Sydney, 29th October, 1897.
A C TTTWT^T 3 t^^T,? XAM1 ' N 4 TI( - >N of Candidates for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney and at various country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 20th DECEMBER next
and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 25. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination ; of good moral character, and in sound
?, e - rs 2 ns alread y employed in the General Division
of the Public Service will be admitted to the examination,
pioviaed a £[ e does not exceed thirty years,
lhe subjects m which the candidates will be examined
are • Handwriting, English (dictation precis writing 1
grammar, and composition), Arithmetic (full course),
Geometry (Euclid, First Book), outlines of Physical and
Political Geography, and the Geography of the Austral-
asian colonies m moderate detail; English History from
the middle of the eighteenth century to the present time,
especially with reference to the colonies and depen-
dencies. Candidates will also be allowed to take any
two but not more than two, of the following subjects
Mathematics, English—a prescribed author—(the same as
that prescribed for the Junior Examination of the current
year—Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynette ” (MacMillan),
Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and
Elementary Geology. Application to attend the examin-
ation must be made on a form for the purpose, which can
obtai, ‘ ed the office of the Board, and must reach
rP e l lot lan the Ist December, 1897.
Candjdates will be required to pay an examination fee
of 10s., which must be lodged with a clerk of petty sessions
o 6 T J" easur .y> and the receipt therefor forwarded to
a* 1 ?- Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates, with centres of examina-
tion, will be published in the Government Gazette after
e u- 1 ? 1 i tor the receipt of applications has expired, and
candidates will also be advised direct of the place at which
1 1 1 ■ The regulations and copies of the
papeis set at the last examination can be obtained at the
Government Printing Office and the principal booksellers.
Jj.y order of the Board.
J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

King O’Malley has introduced into S.A.
Assembly a Bill to enable juries in certain cases
*o fix the maximum term of imprisonment (pre-
sumably within statutory limits) to be inflicted
upon the prisoner. Good, as enabling the jury to
say now guilty they think a convict.
November is an unlucky month to attempt
s<^J riersau lt from the left of the Speaker on to
the treasury benches. In 41 years of Parliament-
ary gymnastics, no Leader of Opposition in N.S.
Wales has jumped into a Premier’s shoes in the
month of November. Lyne’s luck prodded him
in November.
. M‘Pherson, leader of the S.A. Laborites,
w so ill that unless he takes complete rest he must
utterly collapse. The Charleston feud was a great
trouble to him, and he finds it hard to safely steer
Detween Opposition Downer’s seductive solicita-
tmns and Premier Kingston’s pronounced coldness
and indifference.
About three weeks ago N.S.W. Lands
Minister Carruthers declared the whole of the
Mercadool selections—the subject of the big Land
Board inquiry—forfeited. A. few days since the
same Minister issued a notice reversing the for-
feitures. It would be interesting to the general
public to know why the forfeitures were gazetted
in the first instance and reversed in the second.
The prospects of pretty little Bowen are
brightening. The other day a deputation of 11
M. sli.A. interviewed Bananaland Minister for
Kail ways re connecting Bowen with the Northern
railway. Philp says Townsville doesn’t care if
■Bowen docs get connected with Charters Towers
and every good Townsvillian echoes the boast—
and shivers m his shoes. Premier Nelson brutally
■ays what nearly everybody thinks: “ There
mightn’t to be any Townsville at all.” And
Bananaland at large wonders when it’s going
to derive any benefit from the .£300,000 already
lavished on terrible Townsville’s unspeakable
Breakwater.

Ed Bulletin, —
A sound, simple and
inexpensive way of
making up the elec-
toral rolls on a one-
man - one - vote basis.
Every man to be com-
pelled to register him-
self on reaching 21, as
births are registered
now ; his name being
placed on one general
roll and also on the
roll of the district
where he lives, the
latter to be changed
on qhange of residence
if desired without
fee ; to be removable
only on death, lunacy,
or permanent im-
prisonment. Immi-
grants to register
themselves after, say,
six months’ residence
in one electorate. -
Failure to vote with-
out good cause to •
entail disfranchise-
ment ; re-registration
to be allowed after
a certain time on pay
ment of small fee.
Similar system for adult-vote. This would also
allow for representation of minorities. —F. Croson.

Kingston has now
held office as S.A.
Premier longer than
the aggregate service of
any previous Prime
Minister. On his birth-
day (Oct. 22) he had
completed four years
four months and
seven days of con-
tinuous service, the
next greatest total
(4 years 2 months)
having been reached
by the late Henry
Ayers, who was head
of five different Minis-
tries.
Published, and
never officially denied
by the Crown Lands
authorities, that by
payment of a paltry
survey fee, Tas. saw-
mill owners may “ con-
ditionally purchase,”
and thus evade the
regulation departmen-
tal charge of so much
per axe for a timber-
getter’s license. When
all the good timber on
the “c.p.” is cut out,
and just before the first
purchase - instalment
falls due, the land is
forfeited and a fresh
selection applied for.
It comes vastly cheaper
than paying the license-
fee, and assists the
timber industry in the
most Vandemonian
fashion. Genuine set-
tlement is effectually
scared off by the tree-
stumps and desolation,
and the Isle of Bad
Dreams remains
simply remains.

Ihe humors of legislation. Bananaland
Assembly, it is well known, possesses a very sub-
stantial anti-separation majority, and yet this very
Assembly last week passed a most astounding
motion in favor of separation. Kidston, ML A
had brought in a motion to the effect that the
question of separation be referred to the electors
of Central and Northern Q. next election. Curtis
moved an amendment that three-fourths of Kid-
ston s motion be omitted, and words substituted
therefor asserting the desirableness of separation for
N orthern and Central Queensland. And the House
aid assert the desirableness of separation on the
casting-vote of Speaker Cowley— to the horror of
Curtis, who only wauted to cook Kidston’s motion
T couldn t be found when the division-
bell rang. A thin House and preponderance of
Northern, Central, and Labor M.’sL.A. explain
the matter.
The newest bush proposal in S.A., where
State-helped settlement on the land still proceeds,
is suggested by the much-advertised success of
Peter Waite, at the Ohara experimental saltbush
blook. “ Instead of taking so much rent from the
poor squatters, let the Govt, work a few thousand
miles on the minimum-wage, profit-sharing
system ” —that is the idea in brief. But, so far, it
is discussed seriously only in bush-land, where, of
course, nothing ever is discussed in any other way.
Cardinal Moran :
And, though I mention the name “ Commonwealth," I
must say that I cordially dislikt it. Although some of the
enthusiastic supporters of Federation refer to Shakes-
peare and other old writers who use “Commonwealth’’ as
a general name for State, yet, since the Cromwellian era,
the word “Commonwealth ’’ has expressed a clearly de-
fined idea, and that idea is one that would be repugnant
to the best interests of Australia.
Was the Cardinal talking as the Irishman who
naturally hates Cromwell, or as the Tory who
hates Republicanism ?
Melb. : Listening to Cardinal Moran, at
St. Patrick's, one inevitably reflected on his can-
didature for the Federal Convention. Not for a
moment can I suppose it possible that this was a
mere impulsion his part. Rome does not act in
that way. Fancy Card. Vaughan standing for
the Commons, or Card. Gibbons for Congress. It
would be a scandal. No, take it from me that
Card. Moran’s action emanated from Rome,
whether or not on his own advice. The object
was to teach the Australian and American Re-
publics that the Church would claim the right-of
ecclesiastics in Parliament, as in the French
Assembly. Nor do I suppose Card. Moran was
far out as to the degree in which even Catholics
would refuse to vote for him. His. mind is Ovid’s
Temple of Fame, in catching all the voices. Some
fools whispered ‘Vanity!’ The Cardinal might
bite his lips, but they were sealed.”

America once more. K. M. Marquis, on
Senator Powers’ ranch, Sunnyside, Montana, with
a Wolseley machine has shorn 275 sheep in one
day. The previous record was that of R. J. Howe,
who, at Alice downs, Q., shore 237 sheep by the
machine in the gold-medal contest of 1893. On
the same occasion he shore 321 by hand in a day
of eight hours.
Ayrshire Downs station, near Winton,
Q., holds the record Australian artesian bore, the
depth being 4438 ft., or only 842 ft. less than a
mile. It also contains the deepest string of 6in
casing in Australia—37o6ft. The flow is 800,000
gallons daily of splendid water, equal to rain
water. lor height of flow above 6in. casing the
record is held by Quambetook, Q., with 54in.,
Amslie s Block bore, near Richmond Downs, run-
ning a good second with 52in.
NO BUYERS.
At Vic. Congregational Union’s recent
session, Rev. White asked leave to move that
the doctrine of evolution, as applied to the physi-
cal, mental and moral nature of man, was in direct
opposition to the _ revelation contained in the
Bible, and frustrative of the plan of redemption.
Pathetically humorous is the notion of half-a-
dozen smug mediocrities, in black coats and
monkey beards, passing votes of censure upon the
everlasting nature of things because it won’t
cram into their little tinkered creed-pot.

Sydney Health Board’s Sept, return shows
that the month’s percentage of condemned cattle
was nearly 3, and that out of 4270 pigs slaughtered
5 per cent, were diseased, though out of 75,000
sheep only 10 were found unfit for food. Mutton’s
“ the only wear.”

his (respondent's) wife. Whereupon the holy
nian explained that he had kissed her merely “ as
a sister in the Loard.” Nyum !

The she-missionary shamble business is
again making sign in China. A pity, both ways
—for there’s no doubt that the “ martyr’s crown ”

of apparently otherwise sane young Australee
women to be “ expatriated, with the chance of
being slaughtered.”

In a S.A. up-country police-court, the other day,
the manager of Sutton’s big Anlaby station sum-
moned some shearers for breach of agreement.
Defence —the sheep were wet. Information dis-
missed. Thereupon the manager was sued for
wrongful dismissal, and ordered to pay £5 com-
pensation and costs in each case.
Living like a fighting-cock ! The Smith-
field (N.S.W.) nuisance - inspector applied lately
for an increase in salary his screw is now
JR a month ! One alderman having enquired
what he did for this regal honorarium, the greedy
official pointed out that it was part of his duty to

threatened to “ plug ” him if he ventured to inter-
fere with them. “ How would Alderman Hall
like that? ” the pathetic applicant concluded.
Messrs. Swallow and Ariel, Ltd., Mel-
bourne (perhaps the largest firm in their special
line in Australasia), have signed a contract for the
sole Victorian agency of Hunter’s Paw Paw Sauce,
and it is the first time this large firm has under-
taken to distribute another firm’s manufactures.
Mr. Hunter and Bananalanders generally no
doubt feel highly gratified at the success of this
unique tropical product.*
At last sittings of Sydney Divorce Court a
man was seeking a divorce on the ground of his
wife’s desertion. He gave evidence that he
‘‘ found out something ” and went home and taxed
his wife with it. “What did you say?” queried
the Judge. “ I told her I knew it, and then I said
to her, * Get to (Sheol) out of this ; ’ that’s what I
said, your Honor, ‘Get to Sheol out of this.’”
“What did she say?” “She didn’t say any-
thing, your Honor, I didn’t wait; and then I
went to a funeral. When I got home she was not
there, and she never came back.” Petition dis-
missed.
A curious light is thrown upon American
detective methods by the descriptive bills of
Frank Dunham, the record - murderer of seven
people inside of five minutes, now posted in all
the Australian detective-offices. These bills are
adorned with the portrait of Dunham, and, after
a detailed description of his person and habits,
comes an undertaking from Jack Want’s friend,
the ’Frisco chief of police, guaranteeing, in the
event of a capture, not to attempt to “ cut ” into
the reward. Criminal pursuit in Yankeeland
must indeed be in a parlous state when one cop.
has to gi ve another a guarantee of the “ bunce ”
before the second will exert himself.
The other day at Sydney Quarter Sessions
Judge Murray tried a man on a charge of stealing
his mistress’s cash-box. The defence was that the
accused had taken the box simply as a warning to
prosecutrix to be more careful in future, and that
he had intended to return it to her. The Judge,
in summing-up, told the jury that this remark-
able defence reminded him of a country bank-
manager who, when charged with robbing his
bank, said he had merely taken the money as a
practical joke. “ The funny thing about it,” said
his Honor, “ was that the jury too said it was a
practical joke.” After being out for but a few
minutes the jury said “ not guilty,” and found in
this case as well that the accused had played a
practical joke. Example is better than precept.
A glaring instance of the heartless manner
in which S.M. Herald frequently neglects its
firmest friends has been evoked by last week’s
meteor. It reported that
The mayor of Sydney saw a magnificent meteor this
(Tuesday) evening; he was in the garden surrounding his
home.
furthermore, that
Mr. Washington Soul saw the same meteor from Parra-
matta.
likewise, that
Mr. Palkard was driving over the Parramatta River
Bridge, and at 20 minutes to 8 p.m. he saw a very large
ball of fire pass overhead from west to east.
But no letter was published to assure the com-
munity that the meteor had been observed and
identified by E. J. H. Knapp.
“ Mac Schwindle ” to The Bulletin ;
Dear Bulletin, — The clannish exercise of the power of
the dominant Scotch “push” in Sydney University Medi-
cal School has long been a public scandal. But the Mac
Parritches’ latest atrocity “lays over” all previous efforts.
For the vacant position of junior assistant surgeon at
the University Hospital Prince Alfred there were
several candidates from the rising generation of medicos,
among them an M.D., M.S. (Lond.)-probably the only
possessor of such a distinguished surgical degree in N.S.
Wales. This was backed by testimonials from some of the
most eminent English surgeons, and a record of 12 years
experience in London hospitals. Among the rest were
several brilliant British and Australian graduates. The
students looked forward to being taught by the distin-
guished Britisher aforementioned, but were fairly para-
lysed when the joint committee of the Senate and Hos-
pital Board hoisted the son of the boss Scotchman
(Chancellor of the University) into the billet, without
even the formality of a discussion. This happy youth
has a very ordinary Edinburgh degree, and, if I am cor-
rectly informed, has had about a year’s experience as a
house surgeon. Also as a teacher he must labor under a
disadvantage to which it will be chivalrous not to specifi-
cally allude

Something new in saddles. We now offer the option
of the celebrated Brantford Hygienic saddle, soft as
velvet. We will fit them to Red Birds, if desired.
Oh, yes 1 Red Birds always lead in improvements..
Tiie Red Bird is the mount of the people who like
to be in good form. Its graceful design and beautiful
crimson finish make it the ideal wheel for cyclists of
the better class.
When you see the crimson wheel, that’s the Red
Bird or an imitation.
The best price to pay for a cycle is the price of the
best cycle. There has never been a broken RED
BIRD frame in all Australasia out of the many thou-
sands in use. You can now understand why it is
better to pay for RED BIRD quality than pay for
repairs.
The New Models have arrived with their little oil-
bath bearings, adjusting alignment, bottom bracket,
and indestructible frames. These are only a few of
their many improvements.
There are some new things no better than the old,
but every added feature of the famous Red Bird
cycles is a positive advantage.
The new Red Bird is improved beyond all others ;
its little oil-bath bearings and adjusting alignment
bottom bracket, make that cycle as superior to all
others as the pneumatic tyre is ahead of the solid.
It costs money to perfect a cycle like the Red Bird,
and therefore they cannot be sold in competition with
the products of the repair-shop manufacturer.
Red Bird cycles never require repairs, and are,
therefore, selected by the cyclists of the better class.
An ounce of protection guarantees a pound of satis-
faction. All Red Bird cycles are backed by a guar-
antee with a reputation of 20 years’ standing.
We do not offer you the product of a repair-shop or
a plough-factory. We have made our reputation in
manufacturing the best bicycles, and produce the
famous Red Bird as a bicycle thoroughly and under-
standingl.v made. Its fine points—such aa little oil-
bath bearings, adjusting alignment, bottom bracket,'
and indestructible frames—complete a bicycle as
superior to others as the pneumatic tyre is ahead of
the solid.
Don’t be fooled by the festive bicycle salesman who
is trying to convince you that other cycles are as good
as the Red Bird.
He won’t hesitate to tell all sorts of yarns stating
that Red Bird cycles can be bought at any price, and
that they are cheap wheels, and do not stand the test,
and other fairy tales of a like nature.

The hippodromey character of the Melbourne
Cup struggle, which always discounts its sporting interest
for new-churas who know aught of horse-racing on the
bigger English courses, was this year more noticeable
than usual. The 29 starters seemed to run themselves as
closely as possible to the rails, consequently they were
soon so “well strung-out” that the tail-end ones, and
even the horses hanging together in the middle bunch,
couldn t be expected to thread their ways to the front
without the assistance of very good luck. As a matter of
history, none of the early back numbers, barrino- Gaulus
and Coil, ever got near to the fore, and, also as a matter
of history, it may be noted that the two “cracks,” Posi-
t -^ m^er^e > who did hold prominent positions in
the first quarter of a mile, were never dangerous after
their jockeys had taken a steadying pull at them. Little
Aurum got away well, and remained in the leading divi-
sion all the time, whereas his Derby conqueror, Amberite,
after running beside the St. Alban’s colt for a short dis-
tance, was “eased” back into a place where the mud
“yin? hoofs bespattered him, and where, as
the result shows, he might just as well have
been buried. This sort of thing* makes punters’
fancies and horse - prophecy in newspapers appear
unduly ridiculous. Here we saw the winner of two
Derbies and a Caulfield Cup mixed up with a crowd
of commoners, whilst Aurum, Gaulus, and The Grafter-
horses that he had lately “smothered ” —were havino- the
finish of the race to themselves. A muddy course is at
all times apt to upset calculations, but this couldn’t be
accepted as a reasonable excuse for some of last week’s
Melbourne Cup running if it were not a general rule with
jockeys to court, rather than avoid, the risk of getting
“ shut in,” or “ shut out,” or “ interfered with.” The
boy on Gaulus had the sense, seemingly, to rush his horse
away from the ruck at the time when the pace of the
leaders had slackened, and thus he was able to get within
a possible winning distance of them before they began to
run the last six furlongs in sprinting time (lmin. 16sec.)
The warm pace towards the finish settled the chance of
The Bulletin’s tip, Coil, who didn’t begin to make up
much of his ground until the horses in front of him were
going their hardest. Coil finished neck-and-neck with
Bundook, not more than a length behind Aurum, and of
allthe top weights he was the only one to make a respect-
able show. Positano finished sixth, and Delaware (lame)
was scratched on the morning of the race.
Some of the interviewing questions put to jockey
Steve Callinan after the Cup were manifestly superfluous.
For instance, the young man couldn’t very well answer
“ Yes” to the inquiry as to whether he had fancied his
chance in the two-mile race, otherwise he might have
been asked to explain why Gaulus started at 8 to"l for the
Hotham Handicap, three days before, and merely dead-
headed with Canoona for third place, behind Metford and
Surge. What sort of chance could Gaulus have on his
recent Melb. performances—“ nowhere” in the Caulfield
Cup, and a dead-heat with a third-rater for the Hotham
Handicap? Nevertheless, owner and trainer Forrester
%J°ld bis friends to back the horse, and his starting price
m the big event ranged from 10 to 14 to 1 only.
The Victorian crowd of punters, bearing in mind
how Bill Forrester’s pair ran second and third for Glen-
loth s Cup-in-the-mud race, might have turned their
thoughts to Gaulus and The Grafter this year, but they
didn’t. Probably not one in a hundred" of the local
guessers put £1 on either of the Gozo horses from Sydney.
Many of them lost money over other Sydney fancies—
Positano, Amberite, Parthenopseus, Fucile, and even
Toreador, for these had, at one time or other, done some-
thing that looked promising. But Gaulus and The Grafter
—how could the uninitiated believe in them?
The daily press can’t be accused of endeavoring
to open the public’s eyes to the glorious uncertainty of
turf “form.” In days of yore it was usual for a Cup
winner’s performances to be set forth in detail under-
neath his pedigree. This wasn’t done in the Gaulus case,
anyway. It would have been somewhat painful. Why
gall us with hard facts concerning miraculous improve-
ments in “form” now that the V.R.O. stewards have
abolished their ancient right of holding retrospective en-
quiries ?
Melb. Argus put the Gaulus matter very
sweetly in a casual paragraph : “ Followers of form could
hardly fancy Gaulus for the Melbourne Cup after his
Hotham Handicap failure, but those who supported the
Sydney-sider so heavily for the two-miles race evidently
knew that the running on Saturday need not be taken too
seriously.” Ye gods ! Can the Argus tell The Bulletin
whether the Melb. Cup running of certain horses is to be
taken seriously? Also, will the respectable daily journal
try to imagine the effect of the par. aforequoted upon the
feelings of any constant readers who may have backed
Gaulus for the Hotham Handicap ? People did back him
for the H.H., of course, on the strength of his being
notoriously fancied by the stable for the Melb. Cup. Is
the fact that a horse has been supported for a very big
race to rank as a sound turf reason for not taking him
seriously when he runs in a small race three days previous
to the great event?
The Messrs. Miller swore by the chance of their
Bundook—a hollow-backed, angular son of Carbine—for
Melb. Cup. Had this rough ’un stayed in front a little
while longer sassiety would have waved its silk bell-
toppers and lace-edged handkerchiefs with great enthu-
siasm, for sassiety was “on it” at about 40 to 1. There
were four sons of Carbine in the field, and the writer of
this little par. decided to back three—Amberite, Clarion,
and Fucile, leaving their hollow-backed half-brother to
struggle along without his assistance. And when he
(the W.O.T.P.)saw the one Carbine scion that he didn’t
back coming round the corner in front of the field—well,
he cried “Bundook!” in a tone of voice that sounded
very like bad language.
Dreamland holds the record for Melb. Cup
failures. The well-meaning animal has started in four
successive Cup races—’94, ’95, ’96, ’97. Three times out
of the four his colors have been prominent for about a
mile and a-half, or longer, and each time he faded away
when the real business of the meeting commenced. Some-
oody dreams Dreamland every year, too, and yet the
orse’s inability to land the dream remains unabated.
Here and there one heard a foolish remark that
The Grafter could have won the Melb. Cup if he had been
wanted to beat his stable-companion. Nevertheless, that
desperate struggle between two horses running in the
same interest was the “whitest” and healthiest incident
of the Flemington meeting. The Grafter’s jockey ap-
parently didn’t yield a single inch to the other fellow.
The greasy going for the Cup was all in favor of
Aurum, with his large feet, and crack in one of them.
* * * *
Gaulus is still another instance of a horse doing
well after easing-up in his work. He was eased-up for a
few days after his knocking about in the Caulfield Cup,
and a proposed gallop with Positano on the Thursday
before the race was spoiled by the English horse refusing
to try, so that the only good run he had before the Cup
was in the Hotham Handicap.
* * * *
The second “e” in Eleusive, W. R. Wilson’s
filly, is puzzling the papers. They all had it for a time,
and then the Argus (“ The St. Albans Gazette ”) dropped
it. Now it turns up irregularly. Her dam’s name is
Eleusia. Hence the second “ e.”

This is the sort of thing that encourages punters
to persevere. The Grafter wins the Coburg Stakes, at
rlennngton, on Derby-day. He carries Bst. 21b., starts
first favorite, and wins the mile race from the jump. Then
( 7st - runs prominently all the way in
the Melb. Cup, and gets beaten bv a head onlv. Two days
later. The Grafter is asked to carry fist. 31b. in the Spring
Handicap (IJ4 mile), and he naturally starts first favorite,
this time he never gives his backers the least hope. He
runs nowhere” all the way. Then look at the running
of Aurum with Amberite 1

Dear Bulletin,— l don’t fancy that Gozo de-
serves very much credit for the stoutness of Gaulus and
Ihe Grafter, his breeding is not attractive on either
side. Wild Oats, the sire of Gozo, was a big, non-staying
horse who used to roll in his gallop at the end of a fast
race. Considering that the Melb. Cup winner and runner-
up are own brothers, it seems only reasonable to attribute
their stamina to the dam—a Musket mare.—Studdy
* * . *
Aurum s defeat in the Derby is now definitely
decided by the “ talent ” to have been due to an unfor-
tunate collision with another horse during the race.
There were six starters for the Derby. It is asserted, too,
that Key would have beaten Aurum and Amberite for the
Flying Stakes last Thursday had she not been unlucky.
there were five starters for the Flying Stakes. Tun Bul-
letin would suggest that Australian horse-racing be con-
ducted for the future on the tournament svstem. Why
not play the game in heats—two horses to 'race for each
heat-jockeys to toss for positions and a foul to disqualify,
as in boat-racing—first prize to the winner of the greatest
number of heats? That’s what the Turf must come to if
punters are to continue to take an intelligent interest in
the “sport.” Meantime, Aurum’s Derby catastrophe
should warn tipsters against predicting the result of any
race which isn’t reduced to a walk-over.
t Tbe V.R.C. stewards held an inquiry into the
alleged ” (a local newspaper thought it necessary to use
the term “alleged”) inconsistent‘running of Surge at
flemington. Surge (7st.) ran in the mile and a-half
Veteran Stakes on Thursday. He was never near the
front,, and he finished 20 lengths, or more, behind Posi-
tano and Bundook, who ran a dead-heat. On Thursday
the same Surge (6st. 91b.) ran in the mile and three-
quarters V.R.C. Handicap, meeting Positano and Bundook:
on 161 b. better terms. Surge this time was in front all
the time, and he won pretty comfortably from Positano
and Bundook, the pair happening to run very consistently
with their previous form. The explanation of Surge’s ex-
traordinary reversal of form was considered satisfactory
by the stewards, and indeed it didn’t call for particular
inquiry. Dozens of similar cases pass without causing any
unpleasantness except to the public who try to pick
winners.
Parthenopaeus (9st.) ran for the Spring Handicap
on Thursday, distance 1 j mile. His starting-price was 12
to 1, and he made no show in the race. On Saturday he
carried 9st. 4ib. in the Final Handicap, run over the same
H-nnle course, and won it comfortably. This time Par-
thenopseus started at 6 to 1, although he was meeting a
much bigger field than before. s
They stewards—they are beginning to get quite
severe, doncherknow—held an enquiry into the Par-
thenopseus peculiarities, and they decided that a
trainer and jockey had' apparently defeated the
ends of sporting justice, unbeknown to the two book-
makers who own the horse. So the naughty pair were dis-
qualified for ari uncertain period, and the stewards one-
supposes, passed a vote of sympathy with the bookies.

Whernside, who won the two steeplechases at
Flemington last week, is the modern Redleap of the
Miller stable. A gigantic horse, and a splendid jumper
he wins his races all the way at a pace that brings most-
of his pursuers to grief.

card-men, pea-and-thimble-riggers, and race-gam e-runners

poor backer be in with a woman bookmaker who should
welsh him? He could hardly “ take it out of her hide ” 1

* * * *
Stoddart and co. did nothing wonderful in'
their match with S.A. (writes Tiie Bulletin’s correspon-
dent). At the finish the wicket was cutting up, and with
120 runs to the good and five wickets to fall the Wheat-
landers had much in their favor. The match shows the-
strength of S.A. batting and the weakness of the English
bowling. Clem. Hill played all of them with ease, and
was never troubled. He gave not a solitary chance and
banged the Englishmen all over the Oval.
Ranji is a wrist-artist. His cutting and glancing'
impress) but in driving he is far below Hill. Ranji., hov£
ever, pleads that Australian wickets are a revelation to-
him. He says they are six yards faster than English
pitches, and one must depend not on the eye so much as
on correct timing.
Had S.A. fieldsmen fairly taken their chances-
England would not have made 200 in the first innings.
Kanji. was dropped in his second over, Mason was
dropped before he had made 20, Storer when his score
was 45. These mistakes cost S.A. 279 runs. Jones’
average of 7 for 190 was splendid, considering the fact
that in all six chances were dropped off him.
Lyons will probably play "himself into the test
games. He played solid chanceless cricket against Stoddart
and co., and made none of those streaky strokes of old.
Further, he has reduced his bulk, looks well and strong,
and is keen on getting into the big games.
There was much feeling in Adelaide over the
abandonment of the match. It was advertised to be
played out, but, when there was no chance of more gate-
money, Wardill and Sheridan hurried the team over to
see the Cup. It is stated that Stoddart*s personal inclina-
tion was to finish, but he is by no means boss of the
concern.
The gate-money of the match amounts to nearly
£l2OO. £541 was taken on the Saturday, representing
nearly 14,000, an easy S.A. cricket-record.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin,— lt is really remarkable
how successful Australian batsmen have been against
Richardson s bowling - at any rate as compared
with English batsmen. From 1894 up to and in-
clusive of the match just finished, England v. S.A.,
more than half the innings of a hundred or over
played against the Surrey crack have been played by Aus-
tralians. This is the more remarkable because during
that time Richardson has taken part in 130 matches, in
only 18 of which was he opposed to Australian batsmen—
i.e., during the period mentioned the Australians have
played more hundreds (viz., 11) in 18 matches than the
pick of England (viz., 10) in 112 matches Hill has
reached the century three times—2oo, 150 not out, and
118 not out; Iredale twice —140 and 108; Gregory twice —
201, 103 ; whilst Giffen, 161; Trott, 143 ; Darling,Tl7 ; and
Graham, 105, have each got there once. During that
time no English batsman has notched two centuries
against Richardson, though Ward, Grace, O'Brien, Gunn,
Bainbridge, and five others reached the hundred once,
the highest being Ward’s 163. Ranji has never got a
hundred against Thomas ; neither has Shrewsbury, nor J.
T. Brown, nor Wynyard, nor M’Laren. And it must be
borne in mind, too, that English players for the most part
meet Richardson when he has only the Surrey bowlers to
help him, whilst most of the Australians’ big scores have
been got against Richardson supported by the crack
bowlers of England. The only Englishman to get a cen-
tury against Richardson under similar conditions is the
champion, W.G., who scored 118 for the Gentlemen
against the Players at Lords in 1895.—Hugii Boylk, 4th
Nov., ’97.
• * * *
_An incident of the Ranjitsinhji boom. One
night in Adelaide a thin, dark-moustachioed gentleman
was seen walking down a main street. The news went
round that it was Ranji., and, in no time, about 500 men
and boys were following him down street, cheering the
while. The dark foreigner seemed surprised, but he
smiled, and bowed, and raised his hat, and walked on.
But the crowd would not stand that. They insisted upon
having a speech from the illustrious cricketer. The great
man went on ; he, apparently, did not Court popularity.
The crowd, now numbering quite 1000 persons, followed
him for about a quarter of a mile, cheering itself hoarse,
the gentleman always graciously acknowledging, until, at
last the procession reached a street corner. There the
great cricketer stopped and removed his coat and put on
an apron. He was Miltiades Demetrius Smith, ice-cream
merchant.
Stoddart and co.’s first appearance at the
'M.C.O. ground last Saturday gave no suggestion of
startling deeds to come from the English bowlers.
Neither Hearne nor Hayward strikes one as being nearly
as deadly as the little Briggs of ten years ago. Wain-
wright is a mere deliverer of fast “ tripe”” as com-
pared with the Lohmann we remember, and nuggety
Hirst is a poor substitute for Peel on a hard wicket. As
for Richardson, it is yet early to say that he can’t repeat
his previous successes. When he gets rid of some super-
fluous flesh and grows re-accustomed to Australian fine-
weather wickets, he may become the same fiery old Tom
as before, but he certainly hadn’t any terrors for Bruce
and Charley Macleod on Saturday. All the same, the
shilling crowd may have been rather premature with its
satirical cry of “ Keep Richardson on " when Bruce was
putting him to the boundary. The indefatigable long-’un
was a failure at the outset last time, but he finished with
a good average, and if he has merely mislaid his sting he
will find it again presently.
Hirst is a valuable member of the team for
show purposes, anyway. He seems to be almost up to
Briggs’ best form in the field ; also, his kangarooster style
of trundling gives variety to the game. Hirst, a left-
handed little fat man, starts proceedings with a low-
comedy skip. Hearne is an easy, graceful, uneventful
bowler. Hayward hugs the ball against his stomach in
the first stage of his journey to the crease. Wainwright,
who is always changing’ his fast pitch, seems to trust to
luck rather than judgment.
Bruce’s 88 was a great comfort to the selection
committee, seeing that, the stomachless solicitor had been
generally adjudged unfit for a place in the team. Slow
and cautious at the commencement of his innings, Bruce
made things lively after the four o’clock adjournment,
and saved the day’s play from scorn. It looked as though
the Britishers, at this time, were bowling to suit the
multitude.
The expenditure of =614,000 on improvements at
the M.C.C. adds much to the spectacle. A new two-
storied stand makes an important wing to the old original
structure, which has also been lengthened at the other
end. The right-hand portion of the inembers’-reserve,
seen from the opposite side of the ground, presents the
effect of an amphitheatre filled with women, the millinery
display being all the more gorgeous by contrast with the
rest of the reserve, extending towards the score-board,
where men only are permitted to sit and smoke in peace.
The women have lost their promenade, but they get far
more seating accommodation than of yore, and give a
finer show in the mass. Male enthusiasts are bound to
approve of the present arrangement, for they can watch
the game to their hearts’ content whilst appreciating the
collective glamor of their wives’ bonnets from a distance
that lends enchantment to the trimmings.
» • * *
The Victorians made 30G in tlieir first innings
against the Englishmen ; and when Tim Bulletin went to
press on Tuesday afternoon the latter were all out for
250. Ranjitsinhji was got rid of in slips off Trott, by
Trumble, when at 13 only. Hayward was the only man
who failed to get double figures. The Vic.’s second
innings started with the loss'of five wickets for 95—in-
cluding Graham, Bruce, Trott, and M'Leod.
• * * *
The Axemen's show at Melb. Exhibition Oval
didn't get half the encouragement it deserved. The con-
tests were so splendidly fought out as to raise the excite-
ment of the crowd to the highest pitch. It is doubtful
whether such powerful barracking has been heard since
the good old days when football boomed as that which
rose towards the end of the Championship Chop on Cup-
night. The fitst four men all finished within ssec,., and
that operating on logs 6ft. 4in. in girth, and, as the last
few blows rang and the stems grew narrower, the excite-
ment rose to fever pitch, and the audience yelled and
clawed the shoulder of the man in front in the good old
football style. Meanwhile the supporters of the competi-
tors were carrying to them fancy tidings of the opponents’
positions ; and when young Buckingham’s block at last
divided there was a cheer which was almost a roar.
Properly managed, wood-chopping eon tests should become
regular events of real public interest, and they have the
advantage over ordinary athletics, inasmuch as the work
is in itself directly useful, which even their best friends
cannot claim for sculling or football or cycle-racing.
Reeves, the ex-champion, finished third ; the
second man, Mclnolty, of Korumburra, being the sole
choppist of any note in the whole show who did not hail
from Raspberry-land direct. Both second and third men
are said to have suffered from the edges of their axes chip-
ping, while Buckingham’s finished intact and the divided
ends of his log were as cleanly shaped as the sides of the
axe itself. Unluckily, Chellis, of Tas., reckoned a cer-
tainty by many of his fellow-axemen, chopped his foot
during the afternoon and spent the evening in the hos-
pital. He left word to challenge the winner for £5O
aside.
Buckingham, the champion, is, as are indeed all
the competitors, a splendidly-built man. Of the 28 years
behind him he has spent nearly all in handling the axe
and 15 in competition chopping. He is a native of
Burnie, Tas., an axeman by calling, but is educated,
highly intelligent, and much above the average man in
his walk of life. The sporting axiom that “ they run in
all shapes ” is upset by a glance at the competitors, who
are invariably big, broad, and muscular, and with arms
uniformly developed to a far greater extent than among
any,batch of other athletes. Axeing as a.sport is almost
distinctively Australian, and is more deserving of support
from all who boast their Australianism than biking,
stoushing, or cricket.

A perennial Cup feature is Wimpole, of the
George Hotel, St. Kilda. Melbourne expects its Wiinpole
to tike tip a position at the paddock etid of the grand-
stand terrace in the character of sweep promoter, and the
ltori-grey identity is bound to do it. Tickets in these
sweeps are £1 each, the number of entries being limited
to the number of starters for the Cup—2D this year. A
swaying- crowd seems to fight for the privilege of giving
Win-pole a sovereign, and, in order to avoid bloodshed, he
opens a second, a third, and perhaps a fourth sweep,
booking the names in columns of 29 —or whatever the
number of starters may be —So that each line of names
may have its own distinguishing humeral, and all the
Sweeps can be draWrt at once. Cup-day must come father
hard Upon Wiinpole, who doesn’t even get a vote of
thanks for his labor. Bht he couldn’t possibly shirk the
job, except by stopping aWiy from the Cup. In years of
yore this ftmateur sweep business was ruh by a cronk
cirowd, ahd after two or three experiences of the prize-
money going astray, somebody dragged Wimpole to the
fore, as a guarantee of good faith, and the public have
been thrusting their “quids” upon him ever since.

Horse-breeders now do as well—if not better—
with their stunted stock than their big horses. Nearly
all the good ponies are leased to their trainers on shares
Of the prize-ntortej’ ; and a good investment some of the
PWhers find it.
tf * * *
A statement has been made by the chief
veterinary officer in India that “Walers” now suffer
more than formerly from ringbone and roaring. He put 9
the trouble down to the indiscriminate use of unsound
stallions. Australian horsemen say ringborie is no more
prevalent than elsewhere. It usually starts from an in-
jury to the ligaments of the pastern, and bony enlarge-
hieht starting, it eats its way down to the foot, when the
joints become fixed and the horse an incurable cripple.
As regards roaring, this country is particularly free from
the trouble, And it is hard to find an instance of a
Whistler, a disease so common in England. It would be
idle to say roaring is not hereditary, as the get of a
fashionable N.S. Wales trotting stallion dearly all roar
badly when extended, but the cases are so few and far
between amongst the thoroughbreds that it is extremely
Unlikely our studmasterS would pick upon an unsound
horse in that respect to breed from. An old English
groom ortce told the writer that most Of the cases of
roaring in the old country were caused by using a severe
hearing-rein and curb whert breaking in the colt, to make
him carry an arched heck and high crest. This distorts
the larynx, and the horse Whistles when exercised and
breathing hard through the stricture.

It takes a very small, properly-directed blow to
knock out an untrained man, arid a favorite little joke of
the late George Hill’s was to give a man, in play, a quick
jolt oh “ the mark,” and make him gasp again. One
day, at Larry Foley’s, Hill playfully poked a dead-beat in
the manner indicated, Wheh the man collapsed, and all
their efforts failed to bring him round. Larry guessed
the than Was foxing, but Hill Was very much concerned,
and kept saying, “ I’m sure the mart’s hurt, Larry.” “I’ui
ttfraid so, tho,” said Larry, “ and thebe’s only one thing
for it, we’ll have to slit his ears to bring him round
and, goihg for a razor, he entered the room stropping it,
When he Walked over to the miserable one as if to operate
the rrtse acted, and the dying nian sat up with : “ If you
touch mb, Foley, I’ll have the law of yer.” Hill was so
relieved that he give the than half-a-sovereign.

Prof. A. Jacobi, of New York, tJ.S.A., d
physician df world-wide the other day
reported td the Association of American Physi-
bians, a case ih which the patient’s temperature,
tested ih every possible way; rose tt>l4Bdeg. Fahr.
as the feverish result Of an injury, ordinary blood-

Duffy’s last slab of autobiography deals
with J. H. Brooke, of whom Vic. democrats would
gladly know more, Brooke was an architect and
engineer. He designed the old Melb. Exhibition,
William-street, on the present site of the mint,
the law courts having replaced old Govt. House.
In politics, as Lands Minister, "Brooke introduced
occupation licenses, a whilom triumphant piece
of illegality, which foiled the squatters, and gave
Duffy the main hint for his Land Act. M‘Cul-
loch, as a squatter, hated Brooke, and, in his com-
pact with Heales, the Radical, for the piebald
Cabinet, expressly excluded Brooke, who, in a
huff, went off to Japan, where he has since
flourished as a newspaper editor and merchant.
He was the first Radical who really or visibly laid
a hand on the ark of Victorian politics. Evi-
dently he inspired Grant, who became Lands
Minister on Heales’s demise.

A ghastly find was recently made by some
stevedores while unloading an American grain-
ship at Glasgow. A dead body, almost black,
was found, having, presumably, been shot in with
the grain at New York. Which story recalls the
scare among the patrons of a certain brand of stout
some dozen years ago, when it leaked out that
one of the brewery hands had suicided in a
monster vat before it was “headed up.” It was
never known when the discovery was really made,
but lots of people “ swore off ” stout about that
time.
Coolgardie Warden Finnerty has very pro-
perly taken the Afghan under his special protec-
tion, and doesn’t intend to see him imposed on.
The other day, his Worship said it had been com-
plained to him that a certain solicitor—name not
given—-had recovered some money for an Afghan
client, and refused to part. He advised said
solicitor to settle without delay, or he (Mr. E.)
would know why. The result has been quite a
flutter in Gent.-one circles; an indignation meet-
ing of members of the guild was called, and a
resolution passed to call the attention of the
Attorney-General to the P.M.’s action. It was
contended that his Worship should mind his own
business, and not presume to judge on a case till
it had been brought properly before him, and both
sides allowed to have a say.

The Earl of Hardwicke, whom the young
man Harry Picker stands accused of personating at
Sydney, is the strongest Equity name in English
history. The first Earl, whose family name was
Yorke, began as a solicitor and climbed to the
Lord Chancellorship of England. As an Equity
Judge his career was unique. No decision of his
was ever reversed. Only three of his judgments
were re-tried, and in each his decision was con-
firmed.
