PROSPECTUS
OF THE
Householders’ Co-Operative Stores, Limited
(To be Registered under the Provisions of the Companies Acts).
CAPITAL - - - £30,000.
Divided into 25,000 Preference Shares of <£l each, and 5000 Ordinary Shares
of each.
U'HE PREFERENCE SHARES will be payable ss. per share on application, ss. per share on allotment, and the
balance will be called up as the Directors may deem requisite in one or more calls. The holders of the pre-
ference shares will be entitled to receive out of the profits of each year, after due provision has been made for payment
of bonuses on cash purchases by shareholders, a PREFERENTIAL DIVIDEND for such year at the rate of TEN
POUNDS PER CENTUM PER ANNUM on the amount paid up on the Preference Shares held by them respectively.
2500 of the Ordinary Shares shall be issued as fully paid up to the Lessors of the premises hereinafter refered to as
part consideration for the granting of the lease thereof, and in consideration of a substantial reduction in the rent for
the first seven years of the lease. The remaining 2500 Ordinary Shares are offered for subscription only amongst the
applicants for Preference Shares as hereinafter provided, and shall be payable as follows : —ss. per share on applica-
tion, ss. per share on allotment, and the balance will be passable in such sum or sums as the Directors may determine.
Every applicant for Preference Shares shall be entitle to apply for One (1) Ordinary Share for every Ten Preference
Shares applied for, and any balance of such Ordinary Shares not taken up may be issued by the Directors from time
to time as they may think fit. The holders of Ordinary Shares shall be entitled to receive ONE-FIFTH OF THE
NETT PROFITS OF EACH YEAR as a dividend for such year after payment of the before-mentioned bonuses to
cash purchasers and the dividend to holders of Preference Shares. After providing for the above bonuses and divi-
dends, the residue of the surplus profits in each year will be appropriated for the formation of a reserve fund, and for
further dividends amongst the preference shareholders in proportion to the shares held by them respectively.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS.
Hon. J. F. BURNS, 38 Pitt-street, Sydney. E. W. PERKINS, Esq. (Messrs. Perkins and Williams, Pitt-
M. 0. OOWLISHAW, Esq., of Messrs. Cowlishaw Bros., Pitt-street, Sydney).
3 Macquarie-place, Sydney. EDWARD L. SAMUEL, Esq., 6 Post Office-chambers, Pitt-
O. B. PAYNE, Esq. (late Messrs. Payne and Sandford, street, Sydney.
Sydney).
BANKERS:
THE COMMERCIAL BANKING COMPANY OF SYDNEY, LTD.
SOLICITORS :
Messrs. DIBBS, GIBSON and PARKER, ELDON-CHAMBERS, PITT STREET.
BROKERS AND FLOTATION AGENTS :
HARDIE AND GORMAN, 133 PITT STREET.
OBJECTS.
This Company is_ being formed to Establish Large Stores on Modem Co-operative Principles, and to extend the
System of Co-operation in directions not hitherto undertaken in this colony.
It is intended ultimately to Supply all Requirements to Householders for Family Consumption and Domestic Use,
and to Cater for Supplies to Hotelkeepers.
The following Departments will be opened as soon as possible after the Company is formed :
1. MEAT AND POULTRY.
2. FISH ANN GAME.
3. VEGETABLES AND FRUIT.
4. PASTRY AND CONFECTIONERY.
6. GROCERY AND PROVISIONS.
6. WINES AND SPIRITS.
7. HOUSEHOLD AND KITCHEN UTENSILS.
8. GLASS, CHINA AND EARTHENWARE.
9. CUTLERY AND PLATED WARE.
10. BRUSHWARE.
11. BEDSTEADS AND BEDDING.
12. TABLE AND HOUSEHOLD LINEN.
13. COAL AND WOOD.
14. PIANOS AND MUSIC.
15. ORDER DEPARTMENT.
16. PIPES, TOBACCO AND CIGARS.
It will be the aim of the Directors to extend these Departments as opportunity occurs, so as to embrace all the
branches of a GREAT UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS’-ESTABLISHMENT, similar to those which have been worked with
phenomenal success in London and elsewhere. It is contemplated to arrange, as soon as possible, an interchange of
membership with one of the large Co-operative Stores of London, so that visitors to Great Britain who are share-
holders can obtain all benefits accruing therefrom. Most of the purchases of imported goods can be made from the
original sources of supply in the Home, Continental, and American Markets, thus saving all middle profits, and
enabling the Management to give every advantage to purchasers.
Arrangements will be completed soon after the capital has been subscribed to procure from London or elsewhere
a manager of large experience in modern co-operative systems of business.
An agreement, on behalf of the proposed Company, has been made to lease for 21 years, on advantageous terms
numbers 159, 161, and 163 Pitt-street, Sydney, adjoining the Government Post Office and Hoffnung’s Warehouse’
having a frontage of 80ft., and comprising 6 floors and basement. The premises are attractive, commodious, and
centrally situated close to the steam and cable tramways, near to the proposed Government Electric Tramway’ and
are eminently adapted for the conduct of a very extensive trade. The following particulars speak for themselves as
to the success achieved by similar Companies in London and Sydney, and there is ample evidence to warrant the
expectation of a great success to this Company.
“ A.”—ARMY AND NAVY CO-OPERATIVE SOCIETY, LONDON.-This Society ( vide report), which has been
markedly successful, had sales during the year of £2,895,608 on a capital of £60,000, leaving a net profit of £l3B 282
which, together with amount brought forward, enabled the Directors to distribute £156,606 ; out of this sum £9O 000
were paid as bonus to shareholders, leaving £25,337 to carry forward.
The following is an extract from an English newspaper published after the last half-yearly meeting of thp ARMY
AND NAVY CO-OPERATIVE SOCIETYLord Ebury presided on Monday at the annual meeting of the Aitov
and Navy Co-operative Society, Limited.” He said that “the year which ended on January 31st was the first in
which the Society had turned over £3,000,000, and the profits justified a dividend of 200 per cent, on their share
capital. Their sales, together with miscellaneous revenue (16544) had amounted to £3,051,060 which was an
increase of £llB,OOO for the year. There were over 17,000 shareholders on the books. It was unanimously resolved
to celebrate the completion of the 60th year of the reign by devoting one-fiftieth of the Society’s net profits to obiects
of charity.” J

capital of only £6993, made sales during the half-year ended 31st January, 1897, aggregating’£so 834 ’3s Id and
showing a gross profit of £11,783 4s. lOd. for payment of expenses and for distribution to shareholders in dividends
and bonuses on purchases, besides carrying forward a substantial balance. These £1 shares are now valued at i'l
each in the market.
Taking the last-named Sydney business as the nearest possible example, the Householders’ Co-operative Stores
Limited, with its more extensive and varied departments, and with its much larger capital should amrrep-ate sales
from £150,000 to £200,000 per annum, and should yield large profits for distribution to shareholders and bonuses to
purchasers.
Shareholders will be entitled to a special bonus according to the profits earned on the amount of their cash nnr
chases, and it is estimated that these will equal fully 10 per cent. It is further contemplated to inaugurate a svstem
by means of which the general public, whose supplies are purchased from this Company, may eniov in a lessor
degree, the privileges and profits of co-operation, thus ensuring a constantly increasing business J

Prof. Efenry (3. Marty at Toronto meeting
of the British Medicdl Associatibh :
Tendons taken from the tail of tide kangaroo are
greatly to be preferred as surgical sutures, since they are
smooth, even in any variety of size, have never been sub-
jected to decomposition, are much less difficult* of sterili-
sation, while in strength they exceed in equal weight that
even of a silver wire. When properly chromocised, they
hold in the tissues about three months.
This after trying buffalo, moose and caribou ten-
dons, in addition to catgut and silver, and finding
all wanting.
Prof. Koenig, of Gottingen, plagued by a
sudden inroad of bugs in his bedroom, sealed up
its windows on the inside, set a plate containing
2oz. of corrosive sublimate on a chafing-dish,
locked the door ahd sealed it up oil the outside;
Four hours after, he entered holding a cloth over
his mouth and nose, so as not to inhale the mer-
curial vapor, ahd opened the windows to air the
room. The floor was coveted With corpses of ali
sorts of unpleasant insects, Next day he re-closed
the windows, and burned some sulphur in the
room to pick up any odd inercury in its atmo-
sphere; Then he had it aired and cleaned afresh,
and his plague was dead for ever. He has since
found the same method a splendid success after
contagious diseases such as scarlet fever, measles,
and the blood-poisoning and erysipelas of hospi-
tals, and records that he has never had a single
case that could be attributed to remainders from
previous cases.

(From the Takrangower Times, Victoria.)
“ My daughter had been strong and healthy
nntil about two years ago,” said Mrs. Bowe, of
North Maldon, Victoria, to our reporter, “when
she grew very pale and thin. She became weak
and low-spirited, lost her appetite, her sleep was
troubled and restless, and the least exertion
fatigued her—in fact, she had to take her sister’s
arm lor support when out walking. She was of a
wonderfully active and cheerful disposition before
this change took place, and the alteration, there-
fore, caused us great concern, and medical aid was
CD
X
sought, but
no good re-
sulted. Seve-
ral medicines
were tried,
but she be-
came weaker
and more list-
less every
day. The doc-
tor said she
* was suffering
from anaemia
■ or poorness of
the blood.
We then be-
came serious-
ly alarmed,
and consulted
different me-

A Nasty Fall.
11 About six years ago, when residing in Hobart,
Tasmania,” said Mrs. Nellie Denver, of Countess
Cottage, Double Bay, Sydney, to a reporter, “ I
had the misfortune to meet with a serious accident,
which happened in the simplest manner possible,
as I will endeavor to relate. On leaving my house
by the back gate the bolt slipped whilst 1 had hold
of it, which caused me to fall very heavily, which
I subsequently discovered had seriously injured
my spine, although, strange to say, I felt no ill
effects for about two years after. I then com-
menced to suffer from great weakness and dizzi-
ness, and an intolerable feeling of exhaustion ; in
fact, I felt so nervous and uneasy that I feared to
cross the street, alarmed that I might meet with
some accident from a passing vehicle. Not at first
recognising the really serious condition to which
I was reduced, I did not seek medical assistance,
but, when I at last became aware of my serious
'A
A
condition, I was persuaded by my friends to go
to the Sydney Hospital, where I remained two
months, without receiving any permanent benefit.
On leaving the hospital, I again rather neglected
my state of health, and, on applying for re-admis-
sion, was informed that my case was perfectly
hopeless. Being, unfortunately, not over-blessed
with this world’s goods, I obtained admission to
the Newington Asylum, where I remained seven
months. Yet, still I got no better, being unable
to swallow any nourishing food. I then took
two courses of electric - baths, for which I
paid six guineas, and, after them, with the
exception of some slight alleviation of my suffer-
ing, I cannot say that they did me much good ; in
fact, I could not even walk, so I returned home
feeling very despondent, and imagining that there
was no cure in store for me. One day, on reading
the daily paper, I noticed an account of a mar-
vellous recovery effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People, and considering that this
case (the one of Boilermaker Jarvis, suffering
from locomotor ataxia) closely resembled mine, I
determined to make a trial of them, and sent a
boy from the house to buy some for me. I ex-
perienced relief after the first box, and have
steadily improved ever since; my appetite re-
turned, and I ceased to suffer from that terrible
feeling of nervousness. lam now in my seventh
box, and can confidently assert that 1 am most
decidedly improved in health. My friends also
notice a marked improvement in my condition,
and this I entirely attribute to the health-giving
properties of Dr. Williams' Pink Pills ; in fact, it
must be solely due to them, for I have tried no
other remedy. The proprietors of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pdls are at perfect liberty to make whatever
use they may think fit of this statement, for I feel
deeply grateful for the benefit I have derived from
them.

Unsolicited Praise.
GRATITUDE OF A NEWTOWN
INHABITANT.
It is an old saying that one volunteer is worth
ten pressed men, and when we receive unsolicited
testimony as to the value or efficacy of any par-
ticular article, we are inclined to place double
value on such voluntary statements, more especi-
ally when they hail from old-established and
respected men of business. It was in view of the
foregoing paragraph that a reporter interviewed
Mr. Charles Green, Provision Dealer, of 4 Alice-
street, Newtown, who for the last four years has
been a martyr to indigestion, accompanied by the
dry, hacking cough.
A reporter, on making himself known to Mr.
Green at his pleasant home in Newtown, asked :
“ Is it true that you have been suffering for over
four years from indigestion, Mr. Green? ”
“ It is, indeed, and it has been so bad at times
that I have often felt tempted to go up to a
stranger in the street and get him to punch me in
the back. I have often had friends to do it, ; and,
simple as it was, it gave me relief for a time.

Pale and Sallow Girls.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth?
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, ropy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, sallow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeak c. ease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents and guardians rest a great re'
sponsibility at the time their daughters are bud'
ding into womanhood. If your daughter is pale,
complains of weakness, is “ tired out ” upon the
slightest exertion ; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side ; if her temper
is fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
of the most dreaded of all diseases —Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
womanhood, and thus avoid all chances of disease
or early decay.

Rheumatic Gout: A Master
Mariner’s Escape.
(Shields Daily News.)
Case of Captain Holland, Master Mariner, Dock-
wray Bank, East Holborn, South Shields. Expo-
sure to the weather had so crippled him that he
was totally incapaciated for active exertion by
rheumatism and rheumatic gout, from which he
had suffered for nearly nine years. Three doctors
had treated him without effect. He suffered daily
the most agonising pains in limbs and body.
There was considerable swelling in the joints, and
altogether he had a most miserable time. Hap-
pening to come across an article in a newspaper
describing the cure of a similar case by Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, he resorted to the same means
as a last hope of recovery. Asked the result of
this proceeding, he said : “I took one box of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills, and have only been visited
with the pain once since taking the pills, and this
was a slight attack, which a few pills speedily dis-
pelled. I feel myself a young man again.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a sure remedy for
Rheumatism, Neuralgia, Paralysis, Locomotor
Ataxy, St. Vitus’ Dance, - Nervous Headache,
Scrofula, Chronic Erysipelas, etc. They are not a
purgative, but they restore pale and yellow com-
plexion to the glow of health, and are a speeifio
for troubles peculiar to the female ; while in men
they effect a radical cure in all cases arising
from worry, overwork, or excess of any nature.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are abtainable from all

order, 1 box for 2s. 9d., or half a dozen for 15s. 3d.

SYDNEY, N.S.W.


“Kameruka ” to The Bulletin :
All sea-going vessels should be compelled to carry a
rocket-apparatus with necessary line and gear. Time is
of the utmost value in most cases of shipwreck, and as
soon as the ship's position is ascertained, if a line to shore
will be of any use, rocket communication could be
established at once. The rocket brigade on land is all
very well, but ships mostly select places where there
isn’t any, and it isn’t always an easy matter to rouse out
the men—perhaps in the middle of the night, and hunt
up the horses required for the lumbering rocket-carriage
—after which, perhaps, a good many miles have to be
travelled to the scene of action
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The. firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
( With the Usual Apologies).
In the same old iron shanty, looking bold as bold
can be,
There’s a tow-haired girl a-serving, and I know
she’s fancy free;
For the dust is o’er your boot-tops, and the cruel
bells, they say:
“ Come you back, you Eastern Johnny, come you
back to W. A. ! ”
Come you back to W.A.,
Where you’ll thirst the live long day ;
Can’t you hear the glasses clinking for each
shilling you must pay ?
On the fields of W.A.,
Where “ dry-blowing ” is the play,
And the “girls” are like the “boys” are—having
quite a taking way.
Her jealousy was yellow, and her fits of envy
green; . . ,
But her name I daren’t reveal it, very queer though
this may seem; . ,
And I heard her first a-talking of horses and the
loot, . TT _ ,
And a-blowing powd’ry kisses to a W.A. galoot :
Awful larry, full of whisky,
But she called him “ nice and frisky ’
When he kissed her where she stood—oh he was
so “ game and risky ”
On the fields of W.A., etc.
When the dust was on the bottles, and their con-
tents “ going slow,”
She’d take her nip of whisky and murmur d n
the show,”
With her feet upon the counter, and the paint
upon her cheek,
She would sit and watch for “Johnnies,’ and
swear it seemed a week.
Yes, she’d swear it seemed a week,
In that weary, beery reek,
Where the flies made life that lively no human
could be meek
On the fields of W.A., etc.
But she’ll put that all behind her —into limbo,
far away ;
When she’s hooked a guileless hubby she’ll be
happy as the day ;
Sipping “ gold-top ” (’stead of whisky), smother-
ing down her bar-room yells :
Once she starts to be the lady she’ll soon rank
among the swells.
Yes, she’ll rank among the swells,
Where a fellow-feeling dwells,
And all ante-nuptial lapses banished are by
wedding bells
On the fields of W.A., etc.
With a nerve as tough as leather, and a face as
hard as stones,
Not a ball or dinner-party but she'll pose as
Hyphen-Bones;
Dancing here, and prancing there, quite the leader
of a band,
The afflatus of her presence always like draught
beer—“ on hand ” :
Flinty-faced and baubled band,
Never fear she'll be “ on hand,”
An older-soldier-matron from our newer, freer
land
On the fields of W. A., etc.
She’ll be settled where the “ boys ” are, where they
have a frequent “ burst ” ;
Where are only Nine Commandments, and a
“ girl ” mail have a thirst.
O, the wedding-bells will do it, they will ring her
past away,
Chiming out a sweet hiatus to the matron made
that day
On the fields of W.A.,
Where they have the charming way
Of accepting as quite vestal half the shunned of
yesterday.
On the fields of W.A.
Where “ dry-blowing ’ is the play,
And the “ girls ” are like the “ boys ” are—hav-
ing such a taking way, etc.
Coolgardie, July, 1897.
A Shattered Illusion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My friend insisted upon my going, and used this
unanswerable argument: “ To come to Paris with-
out seeing that, is like going to London and not
catching a cold.”
The truth of the latter connection struck me so
forcibly that I yielded. We took a cab, and my
friend, who resides in Paris, told the cabman to
drive to the Moulin Rouge.
I expected to be shocked. Nay, more—l began
to want to be. I was grossly disappointed.
Our destination turned out to be a large imita-
tion windmill—its revolving windsails gorgeous
with electric-light, shaded by red globes. Behind
this was a large garden and restaurant. In the
centre, a band-stand and band playing quadrilles.
On the left, a gigantic elephant, with a theatre
“ inside of him.”
The place was packed with people, mostly ladies.
Between the band-stand and the chairs, in the
open space, some women dancing a quadrille.
They danced in fours, and in doing the various
figures they raised their garments —but they had
tights and frilled undergarments, same as at the
opera ballet where we had just been. That was
all—except the garters. (Why is there such
fascination in garters ?)
My friend looked at me as if for appreciation.
I said: “Is this all? Why, Jimmy M'Mahon
gives us all this in the Cinematographe in
Sydney.”
He took me up the elephant’s leg into the
theatre inside. Two francs extra. “ Now I shall
be shocked,” said I to myself. It was the “ Danse
du Ventre,” and when it was finished my friend
looked at me as before. I said, “ This is chaste.
You should see a Maori haka in the King
Country.”
We strolled out and sat down at one of the
little tables. A couple of young ladies came up
and sat near us. They were gaily dressed, and
had their hair pulled up and banged like Cleo de
Merode, whom we had just seen at the opera.
(But then the opera is a most respectable place.)
One of them said: “Me spik Anglishe. Pur
exemple : E u-lee to bet, earl-ee to r-r-r-rise, make
a man ’ealty, velty and vise.” We bought them a
drink (champagne served by the glass at 50 cen-
times) and strolled away. Again iny friend
looked at me and I said—“ Nothing ! nothing at
all. Either of those ladies might be a countess as
far as manners and behaviour are concerned.
Why, I have seen a Clontarf picnic in the old
days.”
He said no more, and we drove to the Cabaret
du Ciel (the “Heaven-Pub.”). In a dirty little
room painted to represent the interior of a church,
with long tables down the centre, there was a
pulpit at the side and a band-stand in one confer.
A band was playing, and male attendants, dressed
as angels, with faded Roman costumes and dirty
gauze wings, droned “ Venez, mes freres et mes
sceurs ! Assei/ez-vou:s, mes soeurs et mes fibres ! ”
For there were ladies, too—Amurcans —who came
with Amurcan gentlemen.
When we were all seated, the angels brought
vile beer in small glasses and collected 60 cen-
times a glass, and a man in monk’s costume gave
us from the pulpit a burlesque sermon dealing
with the life of a pig.
At the conclusion of this the lights went out.
“ Now we shall see something,” I said to myself.
But it was only a series of living pictures thrown
on a glass.
Then we were invited to go upstairs. There
was a universal “ Ah ! ” and audible “ I guesses ”
from the Americans. But all we saw upstairs was
a representation of the demoniac effects familiar
to Sydney as “ D’Arcy Reed’s Ghost.” A young
gentleman was invited to ascend the platform. He
sat in a chair, and demons and fairies came forth
(the latter had much more clothing on than the
opera ballet). The demons encouraged the fairies,
who tempted the young man with dances and
gold.
Next we went to the Cabaret d’Enfer (the
“ Hell-Pub.”)—a dirty little place: coffins for
tables, little candles burning on them ; same bad
beer at same price. All round were pictures on
the walls. Lecturer begins. Pictures represent
various subjects —battle-pieces, a monk praying in
a cell, a girl with a guitar, a bull-fight. As the
lecture proceeds the pictures change, and the sub-
jects become skeletons. Ghastly enough, but not
“shocking.”
Then, each with a lighted taper in hand, we
proceeded through a series of black tunnels, sup-
posed to represent catacombs, and eventually
reached another little theatre, with a long tunnel
before us on the stage and a coffin upright at the
end of it.
Gentleman requested to stand in the coffin.
Then “D’Arcy Reed’s Ghost” again. The gentle-
man became paler and paler, shed his flesh, and
was turned into a skeleton. Next, a young lady
went up. She sat upon a little platform, and
presently two babies appeared on her lap. Then
she seemed to have nothing on but corsets and
skirt, but developments stopped at that.
Then a young gentleman went up. He sat
down, and a “ D’Arcy Reed’s ghost ” young lady
danced in and began to undress before him..
“ Now or never ! ” I said to myself, but it was
never ! She hadn’t got very far with the opera-
tion when she disappeared utterly. The young
man came back and they threw the ghost lady on
again to show him what he had missed. He
said : “Oh, Id, Id I Si j'avais connul” and there
was a shriek of laughter.
Then we went home, and, as we sat down to a
bit of supper at the hotel, my friend said : “Well,
what do you think of it ? Aren’t you glad you
saw it? Isn’t it a thing everyone who comes to
Paris ought to see ? ”
“ Yes,” I said—“ even the clergy ! Why, man,
I have seen more nudity at a swell ball or a bicycle
race, if that is what I was expected to see. In
fact, although the French are, in their own gay,
lighthearted way, a naughty lot, I have. seen more
lewdness and heard more filthy suggestion in one
half-hour in a London music-hall than I could in
Paris in a whole year—if that’s all you’ve got to
show.”

The Everlasting Nay.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For ages men have pondered what has proved the
Ages’ burden—
If life be worth the living, if its honey be not
gall ;
If Right be duly glorified, if just men win the
guerdon,
Or “Vanity of Vanities” be sum and end of
all.
And quaint fakeers of East and West with mystic
rites baptismal
Sing loud and long an ancient Song of Joy and
Jasper Sea,
A wondrous tale of blissful pale beyond the Deep
Abysmal
For him who bends a supple back and gives
fakeer a fee.
I looked around the coasts of Life and read the
message graven
By countless wrecks of noble craft on every
mile of shore,
Though here and there a tiny ship ran safely into
haven
Because the gods forgot themselves a moment and
forbore !
And everywhere the men I meet raise plaintive,
endless clamor
Of battling in the market-place for dole of daily
bread,
Of days devoid of beauty, of a sky bereft of
glamor,
Of weary body battling on when heart and hope
are dead.
Came one I knew, in days agone, with many a
gaud resplendent—
A crafty, blear-eyed worldling, “flown with
insolence and wine ” ;
I left him in the splendor of his diamonds and
his pendant,
And thanked the stars success like his was ne’er
success of mine.
And some there be who altars build of starry-eyed
Ambition,
Who cling to Honor’s garment in the hope to
clutch her crown,
Who sow to win the mob’s applause and reap—
the mob’s derision,
What time the “ swinish multitude ” shall
madly tear them down.
“ But happy they who live for Love,” ye cry in
hopeful chorus ;
Ay, happy for the few brief hours our hearts in
dalliance play ;
Ay, happy while the wizard wave his purple
pinions o’er us
To leave us weeping o’er the urn of ashes cold
and gray.
For some I know to whom this Love was draught
of fiery fusion
That poured with dreadful potency a virus
through the vein ;
And Love, at best, can ne’er be more than god-
devised illusion,
A day-dream sweet of Paradise where earth is
barren plain.
We pine for Power and give therefor our life-blood
in oblation
To feed that awful altar where the fires of
Mammon glow ;
We seek for Fame —the crowd’s acclaim — a
moment’s exaltation,
A name engraved on mountain peak —in melting
mountain snoiv.
Yet well I wis how fair is Earth to fleeting mortal
vision
(As fair she shone a few short hours before the
Deluge morn);
And well I wis what Night was hers, what pangs
of parturition.
What throes of Death environed her when Life
was newly born.
And ever “Is it well?” we ask—while up from
valley vernal
Sings Heart in yearning hopefulness her Ever-
lasting Y ea !
While Intellect despairing cries from heights of
Truth Eternal
Through Heav’n and Earth and Nether Deep
her Everlasting Nay ! -

P. LUFTIG.
Pirihira.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Pirihira was a hundred years old—lean,
wrinkled, toothless, and nearly blind. Her back
was bent double; her head, as she stood, was
raised but two feet from the ground. Her arms
were as thin as the black stems in a flax-bush ; her
hands were like a hawk’s claws—she had one talon
missing ; it had been broken off short, leaving
only a stump. She walked as nothing else walks
on God’s earth. Shuffling, scraping her naked,
bony feet along the ground—her right claw grasp-
ing a long manuka stick in the middle—she pro-
gressed somewhat in the manner of a crab,
somewhat like an anthropoid ape.
Pirihira’s voice was more guttural than a man’s :
it was the voice of a frog. When she croaked, she
wagged the stump of her maimed talon, by way
of emphasis, at the listener ; and, with but little
encouragement, would tell the awful story of that
mutilated claw.
Pirihira had an atrocious idea that clothes were
almost superfluous to people of her ago. In the
heat of summer it was her custom to go about
half-naked. The sight of Pirihira’s back was
sufficient cause for a nightmare ; a glimpse of her
breast was a nightmare itself. She lived in Tohi-
tapu’s kainga, where she was always in the way,
and often short of food. When she was not at
home she was at Villiers’ pa— she was one of his
protdgies— where she had the run of a detached,
unused back-kitchen. There was her lair.
Therefore, when Villiers declared his intention
of journeying to Ingarangi, that far-off country
from which no traveller returned, Pirihira was
smitten with grief, and Tohitapu’s kainga rang
with her cries.
The pakeha took his wife and daughters with
him, and his house was shut up. He said he
would return in a year or two —but the Maori
mind finds it hard to reach so far. The Ngati-Ata
“ tangied ” for him as if he were dead.
When the tangi was over, Pirihira begged, bor-
rowed, or stole a little sucking-pig from hergrand-
son, Patopato. It was the only little pig left over
from the feast.
* • » •
Yilliers had a maiden sister, as prim and
proper a person as all England could produce.
She had led a sheltered life beneath the shadow of
the South Downs, occupied with the education of
female youth. Also, she was impecunious.
Her nieces were told to call her Aunt Cornelia.
She became their governess, and taught them
French and German, calisthenics and deportment
—and they thought that Ingarangi must be another
Reinga, which is the Maori Hell. They wished
they had never seen the place, but their mother
said—“ Hush, hush, girls. We shall be going
home soon.” Imagine their consternation when
they were told that their Aunt Cornelia was to go
home with them.
* * * *
When Yilliers returned to Te Rapa, there
was great rejoicing in Tohitapu’s pa. Tohi’, ac-
companied by a proper retinue, came to congratu-
late Te Wiria on his safe return, and to present
the compliments of the Ngati-Ata hapu.
When the dusky savages ranged themselves
upon the front verandah, Miss Cornelia sought
her bedroom, that sanctuary which had never
been desecrated. Pirihira was not at the levee.
She had been ill.
* * * *
Miss Corney was religious. To her, mirth
was an offence, and laughter a sacrilegious thing.
She had strange ideas of the extreme value of
food, and thought it “ too priceless a gift of God
to be eaten in the ordinary, prodigal, ‘ colonial ’
way.” Also she believed in the divine use of the
rod. And the Villiers girls prayed for an earth-
quake, even if only a little one ; they petitioned
Providence to erupt the nearest volcano. But the
gods were on a journey, or asleep, or something.
* * * *
Pirihira’s pig had grown immensely fat.
It could not squeeze itself through the door of
Pirihira’s raupo whare. It was so fat that it
could not stand—a whole side of Pirihira’s hut
had to be pulled down to give it egress. Then
four men carried it down to the beach and placed
it in a canoe. Pirihira’s two great-grandsons
paddled her and the pig over to Te Wiria’s pa.
It was night when they reached Te Rapa. The
boys helped the old woman ashore, and moored
the canoe. They could not lift the pig—it could
not lift itself—so they left it where it was. All
three camped quietly in Pirihira’s lair.
• • * *
With the first streak of dawn, Pirihira
awoke and commenced to croak. She wanted her
great-grandsons to fetch up the fatted pig. The
boys said they could not do this without help, and
the pakeha people were still asleep. Arguing
loudly, Pirihira walked round the house —the boys
following—and woke the pakeha people up.
The Yilliers girls immediately recognised the
croaks of the old makutu wahine, and went down-
stairs to greet her. There was a great re-union on
the front steps.
With all the sweetness of maidenhood, the
girls asked Pirihira if she would like to see their
mother. They pressed her to see their mother.
But Pirihira needed no pressing to see her “ mata
Wiria ”■—it was what she had come for.
Carefully they guided the ancient witch into
the house, and assisted her to ascend the stairs.
Safely at the top, she was shown a half-open door.
Pointing to it they said : “ Hacre, Pirihira, haere
in there.” Pirihira entered.
The room was dark —the blinds and curtains
were drawn. A figure lay in bed, wrapped in
tranquil repose. Pirihira could see nothing ; so
she raised her voice and whined in high falsetto,
“ E mata Wiria, you have come back, you have
comeback.” There was a gentle stirring of the
bed-clothes. Pirihira felt her way to the bed-
side, and laid a loving claw upon the sleeper. “ E
mata Wiria , I have brought your pig, I have
brought your pig.” The tears streamed down
Pirihira’s cheeks—tears of joy and affection. She
laid her other claw, the maimed one, on a bare
arm that had strayed outside the sheets. “Hi
mata Wiria, it has been fed on kumera and
peaches, on kumera and peaches.” Pirihira peered
with her sightless eyes close into the pakeha
sleeper’s face; shrivelled brown features were
pressed against white ; nose touched nose.
The morning sunbeams streamed through
the crack of the blind. A shriek, as of a soul
in Hell, rang through the silent house. A
white figure sprang from the bed, burst from
Pirihira’s loving clasp, and shot from the room
like a rocket.
The old uiakutu wahine groped her way down
the stairs, in her crab-like, anthropoid fashion,
and went to see about getting the pig up from the
beach. No white wairua was going to frighten
her—she knew too much about ghosts.
* * * *
Miss Cornelia packed up her traps that
selfsame day. She did not even stop to taste
Pirihira’s kumera-ied pig. She said she was not
going to stop to be eaten alive by cannibals. But
she had extreme ideas of the value of human food.
ML.
ALFRED A. GRACE.
“ Ought the man who has lost £50,000 in
some pastoral experiments to know more about
them than the man who has made £50,000 through
those same experiments ? ” Depends ! If you
want to get information for your own guidance,
go to the man who has made the money; but if
you want the information to lay before the
Government or a land-board in an appeal for re-
duced rent, or before working-men for reduced
wages, then put up the man who has lost the
money to pose as the authority and do the talking.
More than one such “ authority ” is helped to make
a pretty fair living in this way. —P.
Is there no satisfying America ? The
American Naturalist briefly describes a new
American earthworm (Mecjascolidcs amcricanus),
a brother of the giant earthworm of Gippsland
(Megascolides australis), which is said to be very
abundant around Pullman city, Washington,
U.S.A., and to make burrows over 15ft. deep,
also to have more kidneys, or what pass for
kidneys in worms, than any other worm on earth.
Melb. University Professor Spencer will now have
to arise and barrack loudly for M. australis ,
hitherto regarded as the world’s prize worm. The
man who writes this has put a foot-rule over a
Gippsland worm which went just a shade less
than 7ft., and was as thick as a laborer’s thumb.
